THE RED PAGE.
SWAN-SONG OF A DYING POET.
Well-tasting dishes
I always ate:
In well-lined dresses
I warmly sate.
Seven years and seventy.
These are my days :
The Buddha unending
1 therefore praise.
A. DEQYD, from the Japaness.
KIPLING BY CABLE
We have seen the professor at the breakfast-
table, we have now to see the poet in the telegraph office-
..We see in imagination Mr. Kipling sitting at the far
end of the South African cable engaged at his task,
printing his letters very clearly lest the operator should
make a mistake. “ Cap. here in a large and sunlit land
comma where no wrong bites the bone comma cap. I will
lay mj' hand in mj r neighbour’s hand comma and together
we w'ill atone cap. for the set folly of the red breach and
the black waste of it all semicolon cap. giving and taking
counsel each over the cattle-kraal full stop..
A'The Settler” as a piece of poetry is one more un-
happy instance of the decadence of a fine quality.
There is, however, some grain of comfort in the
thought that possibly the most melancholj' of all Mr.
Kipling’s most recent phases—w-hat can only be de-
scribed as “bloody”—is for the time being over. The
shattered limb, the lyddite shell, the rifle range, and the
Mauser pistol are all toys that have been laid aside ; and
instead of them Mr. Kipling has got a nice new Peace-toy
with little green trees made to stand upright, and a prac-
ticable well, and a bright new plough, and a field of grow-
ing corn, and two nice red spotted bullocks to draw the
plough. His delight in these new toys is supreme.. .
It is not so long since the cable poet was blessing very
different things from ploughs, in those naughty days
when “ there was blood on his head of my kind, or blood
on my head of his kin.” But that is all over now, and
we have cleared the table, and are going to have a new
game with the spottj' bullocks and the plough.- B.O.E.,
in The Daily Mail.
MORALITY AND MOMMY
The distinction made by human societies
between good and evil has never gone beyond a gross
empiricism. It has been settled in quite a practical
spirit, simply by way of convenience. We do not bother
about it in the case of a crystal or a tree. We practise a
moral indifference in regard to animals. We practise it
in regard to savages. That permits us to exterminate
them without remorse—what you call “ colonial policy.”
Nor does one see religious people exactinga high standard
of morality from their god. In the existing state of
society, thej r would not willingly admit that he was lust-
ful and compromised himself with women ; but they
approve of him being vindictive and cruel.
Morality is a mutual agreement to keep whatever
one has—land, houses, furniture, wives, and our
lives. It does not imply any particular effort of
character or intelligence in those who submit themselves
to it. It is ferocious, instinctive. But written law follows
it closely, and fairly agrees with it. Thus we see that
men of noble heart or of distinguished genius have
almost all been accused of impiety, and, like Socrates,
have been doomed by the justice of their country. And
you can say that a man who has not been condemned at
least to prison does only moderate honour to his native
land...
You remember a reflection of Auguste Comte’s :
“ Humanity is composed of the dead and the living. The
dead are much the more numerous. By their multitude,
and by the greatness of the work they have accomplished,
they are the more powerful. It is thej r who rule : we
obey them. Our masters lie beneath these stones : there
is the legislator who has made the law' that I live
under to-day, the architect who has built my house,
the poet who has created the illusions that trouble us
still, the orator who has persuaded us before we were
born. Here are all the artisans of our knowledge, true
or false ; of our wisdom and our folly. They lie there,
the inflexible rulers whom none disobej's. They have
strength, continuity, permanence. .What is a single
generation of the living in comparison with the innumer-
able generations of the dead ? What is our will of a daj'
before their will of a thousand centuries?. .Revolt against
them, how can we? We have not even time to disobej'
them 1 ’’—Anatole Frame, in La Revwe de Paris.
The Heart of Japan.
A BOOK by Clarence Ludlow Brownell,
entitled The Heart of Japan, and published by
Methuen at 2s. 6 d., gives of that charming
country some most charming glimpses. Of
the material side of Japan Basil Chamberlain
is the best interpreter : of spiritual Lafcadio Hearne
has written with loving sympathy; but this new
collection of sketches captures our fancy with
gaiety and humour. The writer is surely American, with
an American’s love of “ seeing things ’’ and an American's
brisk, picturesque style. Once more we are impressed
with the utter difference between European and Asiatic
modes of thought. Two civilisations, advancing on
diverging lines, have built up brains loaded with instincts
and prepossessions that cannot be transferred from one
to the other. The Japanese are sometimes said to have
assimilated European ideals. But they have merely
super-posed them upon their own. Assimilation is a
physical impossibility.
THE INVERTED LANGUAGE
Many things in Japan seem to us inverted ; but
the language most of all—perhaps. “The honorific” is
comparatively easy to understand. “In Japanese all
one’s own things are mean and vile, while the other
fellow’s are honourable, august, divine.” If I wanted
anything from ray mate (says Brownell), when we were
trying to think in Japanese, I would talk like this : “ To-
morrow will your augustness condescend to remind
decayed me to buy some honourable tea?” etc. And
here are examples of Japanese translated by Cham-
berlain. The first line is Japanese ; the second is a literal,
and the third a free English version.
O ki no doku Sama.
Honourable poison-of-the-spirit Air.
I am sorry for j our sake.
Go burei moshi-agemashita.
August rudeness (I) said-lifted.
I am very rude to you.
Kiite itadakite gozaimasu
Hearing- wishing-to-put-on-the head am
I wish you would he so kind as to ask.
O shiete itadakitai
Teaching wish-to-put-on-the-head
I wish you would be so kind as to show me how.
And to talk Japanese is easy, comparatively. If you
wish to read and write, you have to learn sets of syllables
instead of letters. There are two sets, and six ways of
writing the one more generally used. Then there are
some four thousand ideographs to commit to memory,
each written in several ways, and each with several mean-
ings according to context. When you have mastered
these, you can read a Japanese newspaper with some
intelligence.
JAPANESE JOURNALISM
Japanese newspapers are under a censor ; but
the rules of the censorship are not published. It is only
by guessing and by bitter experience that an editor can
tell what to avoid.
When a paper ventures too far, and the censor is called
upon to write the order of suspension, he is brief but
polite. He puts the honorifies “O” or “Go” before
the nouns and verbs. Prefixed to the n t oun “ 0” means
honourable, to a verb it means honourably; similarly “Go”
means august, augustlj'. So the order when it arrives
will read somewhat as follows :—
“ Deign honourably to cease honourably publishing
august paper. Honourable editor, honourable publisher,
honourable chief printer, deign honourably to enter
august jail.”
The honourable editor and his honourable co-workers
bow low before the messenger of the censor, acknowledg-
ing the honour of the august notification, and then
accompany him to the honourable jail, chatting the
meanwhile of the weather, or of the flower shows, or of
the effects of the floods on the rice crops. Centuries of
breeding under Japanese etiquette have rendered it im-
possible for them to show annoyance. They do not know
how.
Some years ago the Japanese journalist invented the
dummy or “prison editor,” as he was known in the
Oriental sanctum. This functionary had an easy time.
He had nothing to do on the paper, never wrote a line,
but when those who did write said anything that the
censor judged might mean something, and the paper was
suspended, the prison editor stepped forward, bowed low,
and said, “ What augustly must be, probably augustly
must he.” Then he trotted off to prison. This scheme
worked well for a long time, but after a while the censor
demanded that the principal three men connected with
the paper should go to the “honourable jail.” Three
dummies were more than any paper could afford to main-
tain, and so there are no proxies now.
The Japanese reporter makes about as much money as
the Japanese policeman—that is, about twelve shillings a
month. In Tokio some of them make more, and in the
smaller towns they make as little as eight shillings a
month, but twelve shillings is a fair average. They are
not sent out on regular assignments as a rule, but are
given a roving commissson. The editor tells them to get
news, real news if there is any, but to get news ; and they
never return empty-handed.
CAUCASIAN REVENGES
It is only fair that English should puzzle the
Jap as much as Japanese puzzles the Englishman. Since
Government has adopted English fashions, the little
brown men have followed obediently. They eagerly
study English ; they learn to dress like Europeans ; and
in the treaty ports the tradesmen put up English signs.
Like these—
The Tas are restful and for sharpen the Minds
above a tea-house. Another :—
The Genuinely bier buy the health for drink.
And a third—
Of smokes our tobacco is preesure to our Tongue and
give the Healthiness to Hers and Hes 1 Also All
People by It.
Mr. Pinecape, who dealt in coals, took the public into
his confidence and confessed the secret of his success.
Beneath his name and address are these two lines—
Honest, Indistorious make the Oont
inual Prosperity.
Yet Brownell gives examples to show that, while
Chinese merchants are exceptionally honest, Japanese
are such rogues that it is hardly possible to do business
with them on credit.
THE JAPANESE ACTOR
I believe (says Brownell) that there are no better
equipped actors in the world to-day than those found on
the Japanese boards. Many of the plays would be
intelligible to an audience that did not know a word of
Japanese. Danjuro, whose real name is Horikoshi Shu,
and Kiugoro the great comedian, speak a world language
and will make you laugh or cry at will. It is a pity they
cannot be prevailed upon to make a foreign tour. They
would draw well.
Their versatility is marvellous. Thej- play comedy,
tragedy, and farce, in either male or female parts, with
equal facility and happiness. They were born to the
stage, as were their parents and grandparents before them
for ten generations, and have taken parts from the time
thej- were of sufficient size to be seen by the spectators.
With such inheritance and such training it would be
strange if they did not excel.
In spite of all this excellence, however, it is only re-
cently the theatre has been in good repute in the
Mikado’s Empire. The records of the census-taker show
the position of the actors under the old regime. This
official counted them, “ip piki, ni hiki, sam biki,” etc.,
when reckoning the number of men in a theatre. That
seems harmless enough until explained that, in counting
in Japanese, “ ichi, ni, san, shi, go, roku,” etc. (one, two,
three, four, five, six), certain auxiliaries to the numerals
are used, according to the kind of things that are being
counted. For instance, human beings are “mei” or
“nin,” and are usually counted “ichi nin, ni nin, san
nin,” etc. Flat things, such as sheets of paper, are
“mai’’—“ichi mai, ni mai,” etc.; houses are “ken” —
“ik ken, ni ken, san ken,” etc. ; boats are “so”—“is so,
ni so, san zo,” etc. ; and living creatures, except human
beings and birds, are “hiki”—“ip piki, ni hiki, sam biki,
shi hiki,” etc. Actors, therefore, came under the general
classification of beasts.
LET US BE MODERN!
When Japan started the revolution that is to
put her on the level of’European thought and action, she
wanted everything at once and as quickly as possible.
One order to the London agent of the Board of Public
Works read as follows :
Urgent. Send to Tokio at once as follows :
1 Professor of Electrical Science.
1 Do. Mining.
2 Blast Furnaces.
And in due course the London agent forwarded an
invoice declaring that he had sent out for Yokohama,
Japan, by steamer Maru, four items as per order, to wit—
1 Professor of Electrical Science.
1 Do. Mining.
2 Blast Furnaces.
It was hard for the Japanese to drink milk, however.
But the medical man prevailed. Foreigners drank
milk, therefore the natives would. Officials set the
example nobly. Every morning at eleven o’clock the
milkman called at the various Government bureaus and
made his ostentatious rounds. Not an official escaped.
Each one received his proper portion of the potent
potion, ajid drank it—a liqueur-glassful. At the “ Kaita-
kushi Jo Gakko,” or Girls’ School, belonging to the
Colonisation Bureau in Sappuro, on the northern island
of Yezo, the young women early learned to wonder at
the ways of foreigners. The mistress of the dormitories
made each drink a mixture of egg and claret, like'the
American claret-flip, every night before retiring. The
girls did not like it, but “ no flip no futon” was the order,
and up so far north as Sappuro futon (the quilted beds of
Japan) are desirable.
A BOOK OF POETRY
We cannot pretend to quote all the interesting
things in Brownell’s book. The whole of it is uncom-
monly interesting, and bookfellows should not miss it.
There is space only for brief reference to a book of verses
to hand, Japanese Poems, adapted from the German of
Karl Florenz by A. Lloyd, and published by T.
Hasegawa, Tokio. From this book is quoted the
verse heading this page. But Japanese poetry, when
it has passed through the translator’s mill, holds
little or nothing of interest. Moral reflections and
descriptions of nature are its staple. There is just one
modern piece, a description of the great earthquake of
1855, that stands out from the mass of Florenz’s repre-
sentative collection. In the original it must have been
forcibly dramatic. But we still find Aston’s Japanese
Literature the best handbook available.
LITTLE CONTRIBUTORS
J.A.C. : “ It was not Jerrold, but Arcedeckne,
who made the historic remark to Thackeray about the
piano."
Fax : Out of 12,000 people gathered at Melb.
Exhibition-building when the (now) Prince of Wales
spoke a short printed speech and read a telegram re the
opening of the Commonwealth Parliament, fully 6,000
could distinctly hear him. It was a full, manly voice ;
the elocution excellent. The thin, penetrating, nasal
accents of Lord Hopetoun had served to emphasise the
superiority of the Duke’s speaking powers. He differed
from the average trained speaker in being more musi-
cal. From my point of hearing—about ten yards
distant—I judged him to have a good bari-
tone voice for singing purposes. And his English
sounded absolutely free from German accent or gut-
turals. More : I asked Detective Christie, the man who
“shadowed” the Duke around Australia, whether he bad
detected any foreign accent in conversation. “Not
the least suspicion of it,” said Christie. “ I am always
being asked the same question. 1 dined at the Duke’s
table many times, and I’ve talked with him on the
quarter-deck for two hours at a stretch. You would n’t
think there had ever been a German in his pedigree.”
Now as to King Edward’s alleged habit of saying “ de
man” or “ my tear father.” I heard him speak at odd
times in the early eighties. On a railway platform ; at
Drury Lane Theatre ; and on the lawn at Goodwood race-
course. If there was any strong suggestion of German
parentage in his accents, I did n’c notice it. Nor did I
ever hear of it until I came to Australia and found that
Eng. papers of notorious mendacity were accepted as
fountains of truth.
[Then ears differ. These ears heard the Duke of York
speak twice in Australia ; King Edward twice in England.
If both did not say “Dank you!” then these ears are
German too. The Duke of York’s German accent at C.
Parliament opening was noted by an independent Bulle-
tin observer at the time. And what then ? No blame
is imputed : no criticism is made : the fact was stated
merely. And, though it is quite, unimportant, we shall
be pleased to hear from Eng. authorities.]
David : How a bookfellow can carry “ gold and
notes ” as a general thing passes my comprehension. My
pockets contain to-day : Coat —outside top, handkerchief.
Outside, side indifferent, pipe and tobacco, occasionally
a book or newspaper. Ticket pocket, coppers and three-
penny bits. Inside breast, fountain pen, pencil and
letter book (5f" x 3|"), which again contains visiting
and business cards, stamps, two obsolete tram tickets, an
electric tram ticket bearing the number of the year I was
born, two Tatts.’tickets (blank), a valueless cheque, an
I O U, scrip for 25 shares in a mine that is to make my
fortune, a black su&de glove redolent of memories, three
midget photographs, a MS. copy of verses, a map of the
harbour, several notes of ideas, souvenirs of events and
addresses of men whose names I have forgotten. That
pocket is pretty full, as a rule, but it can hold more if I
don’t button my coat. Vest —three pockets. Top, note book
(4"xlJ") and spectacles, frequently a short pencil. Watch
pocket, watch. Other pocket, match box and knife (if I
have n’t left it at home, got it in my jacket pocket, or
lost it). Left trouser pocket, keys and a large hole (as
the keys are chained to my suspender button the hole
does n’t count). Right pocket, money, mostly silver. In
the summer, when I wear a soft shirt, there’s a pocket in
it—there is also a pocket in my pyjamas, but Oh yes,
I’ve got a wife who wears a pocket in some of her skirts.
I inspected one last night. Contents—two draper’s re-
ceipts, a handkerchief 9" square, a broken button-hook, a
recipe for making she came in before I found out
what, and I had some difficulty in explaining that it was
purely in the interests of science that I was “ running the
rule over her." “ Ignotus ” wears his vest pockets empty
—I don’t. I usually work with my coat off—he probably
wears his all the time.

R. A.K. : In Cassell’s Magazine for March,
1899, appears “ The Professional Lover, a complete story
from the French of Charles Foley ; by Dora Moore.” In
The Australasian Stage Annual for 1902 appears “With
This Ring; a slightly true story, specially written for
The A.S.A. by Bernard Espinasse.” I would like Bernard
to explain why his “original” story has precisely the
same plot as Foley’s—the account of a birthday party
where a backward lover is tricked into offering the lady a
packet which, when opened, is found to contain an
engagement ring—exposing him to the congratulations of
the company and preventing him from “ backing out.”
J.O. : I wish to table the case of the uneducated
against the classical poets. I state the case of the man
of average intelligence and education now and again the
Red page runs riot over Bacon coKper milton or some
other old fossil that the majority of people don’t under-
stand in fact to be a great poet you want to write so that
people won’t understand you. There must have been
harsh libel laws in those old times so that the poets had
to cover up there tracks.
A chap that had been mates with me for years served
me a dirty trick. I’m a hatter now. I would have trusted
him with my wife if I had one. He returned from town
with a book by one poe. This poe at first I thought he
was a new writer but I find he is a very old hand also
he’s mad. He is about the barmiest chap I’ve struck in
print or out of it. How ever he got into print licks me
but praps the poor fellow had it bad and his friends
thought by publishing his ravins it might cure him. One
of his yarns where a friend of his steps down into the
wine vaults to get a nip of some fancy brand and he bricks
him up with it. Well in my opinion it served him right to
trust himself with a looney bloke at a late hour of night.
The reason why these poets are great is because it gives
some doo-da a chance to let people know he’s been to
college and took a degree or something worse, and whose
brown paper collar stick up that far under his chin that
when he wants to expectorate he has to stand up to spit.
What the good of apoet if somebody else has to explain him ?
And thatremindsme The Bulletin once took a mean advan-
tage of me it prased up a fellow named waiter whitman and
me in my blind faith bought a copy. Walterif some body
had left him sixty or seventy millions would have done a
lot of good and he w'ould not have wanted people to send
him suggestions either. His own great heart would have
told him. But waiter as a poet is something awful. He
cant rhyme enough to keep himself warm the only time
he rhymes is when he flukes it and that is pretty rare. I
tried hard to understand waiter because I could see he
was a good fellow. I used to read on and not understand
now and then a ray of light would break through and
then it was gone. Then I would pub him by and think
and the only thought that would come to me was that
waiter was a good fellow.
One day a traveller asked me if I had any books to
swop. His a yankee at the court of king arthur. I
swopped him waiter. He looked at it suspiciously and
asked if it was any good. I told him waiter was alright.
That evening he returned and abused me something
awful—call that a book yer might as well have lent me
the blanky bible. I took waiter back again and I was
ashamed. I packed him about for two years or more
brought him in out of the drought on to the tableland
then down the coast range on to the coast and I never
understood him. Then I gave him decent burial by a old
gum and scratched on the bark poor waiter.

NOTES AND
Last week we scandalised Mummy. This week
“Mummy is become merchandise,” as Tom Browne
declares ; “ and Pharaoh is sold for” a bob in Old Tim/,
(The Commercial Publishing Co. of Sydney). Old Time,
is designed as a serial publication for printing and re
printing illustrated narratives of N.S.W. early davt
This is a cheap and nasty Number One. The letternreaa
and pictures have considerable interest; but interest
stifles in the odour of unusually blatant advertise
merit. Not content with inserting advts in the
body of the booklet, the publishers have even in
terpolated them in the midst of consecutive readinv
matter. The account of early N.S.W. Governors is
mixed with stables, funerals, and confectionery
Reminiscences of old-time bankers are brought up to
date by displayed advertisements of their everyday
banks. Insurance managers with a fond eye on the'past
have a very modern eye to the lengthy notices setting
forth the claims of their institutions upon possible “ new
business.” The whole publication leaves the impression
of an advertising circular baited with old bones. ’
Sonne Verses , by Frank Wilmot (The Microbe
Press, Melb., 6 d.) were not worth the pains of printing.
The Origin and Ethics of Land Owning, a pam-
phlet by George Black (The Worker ; Sydney) collates
authorities against private property in land" and generally
Tne writer foreshadows an era when “universality of
idea’’in agreement with his views “will induce com-
munity of interest ” in wealth. The writer is jolly san-
guine. However, we have asked McGowen, leader of
N.S.W. Labour party, to review the item when he can
spare time from his building investment at North
Sydney.
Some “women of N.S. Wales” have collected
£3OO to present N.S. W. Gallery with a portrait of Queen
Alexandra, to be painted by Longstaff. L. could have
no finer subject. If he will paint the woman he sees,
without conventional flattery of the Queen, the work
should make him famous.
Said Arthur Lynch, in prison, to an Australian
visitor : “ My idea of happiness is to lie under gum-trees
and look out to sea, with a whip in my hand,—and, when
I want something to do, just to flick the air.”
Glad tidings of moderately-great joy 1 A first
dividend of £1 per recitation has been declared on account
of eighty recitations in The Bulletin Reciter ; and writers
represented are requested to forward their present
addresses to the Accountant, Bulletin Office. It was
hoped that The Bulletin Sttry Book also would be on the
div.-paying list; but the expense of producing that book
was comparatively much heavier, and the profit in
hand at present has not seemed worth sharing. Perhaps
by the end of the year—«
Louise Mack, writing from London, says: “lam
getting-on splendidly—have more work than I can do.
Black and White is printing my dialogue-series, ‘The
Conversations of Clarissa ’; and in the autumn will be
published my new book, A Love-Story from the Bush."
The Walkley-Jones dispute has entangled
Clement Scott. Bourchier referred unpleasantly to
Clement in The Pall Mall Gazette, and Clement has
issued a libel-writ against the paper. Brander Matthews,
the Amer. writer and play-writer, is telling a story
apropos of the dramatic critic’s obligations. When
Matthews was a critic, he received from an Amer.
dramatist a ticket of admission to a certain play. He
accepted it and went to the theatre. At the end of the
first act, the curtain went down amid the blank silence
of the whole audience except himself, who, conscious of
the courtesy shown him, applauded to the best of his
ability. At the end of the second act the audience
hissed, while he maintained a stoical and compassionate
silence. At the end of the third act, he went ouk
bought a ticket, and came back to hiss with the rest.
Smith, Elder and Co. announce The New Nation,
a sketch of the present conditions of the life and of the
economic and political outlook in the Australian Com-
monwealth, by Percy F. Rowland. The title is attrac-
tive,—and it headed a Bulletin article three years ago.
Vic. Surveyors’ Board sheds light on the quality
of some Aust. University teaching. The secretary told
Melb. University Commission that local University
graduates—so-called bachelors and masters of _ civil
engineering—had to be examined afresh before admission
to the ranks of practical surveyors. “ They usually
failed in computation and seemed unable to solve prac-
tical questions.”
At a solemn commemoration of the fiftieth
anniversary of the death of three citizens of Ferrara, shot
in 1853 by the Austrians, a member of the Italian Ministry
quoted Swinburne’s verses glorifying the Italian revolu-
tion. “Italy will always owe to Swinburne a debt of
gratitude,” he said. So would the Transvaal,, if only
Britain had been Austria.
Dr. Macfadyen, of the Jenner Institute, has
found that by crushing the cells of the typhoid bacillus
the intracellular juices can be obtained apart from the
organism, and that these juices are highly toxic. By in-
jecting them in small and repeated doses into a living
animal its blood serum is rendered powerfully antitoxic
and bactericidal. Animals dosed with the protective
serum and subsequently treated with lethal doses oi
typhoid bacteria were immune from typhoid fever, f ur-
ther, animals dosed with typhoid bacteria, and treatea
with the serum after half the time required to ki
them had elapsed, recovered health. The way
seems open for a generalisation that serums ro
from any bacterial juices will be prophylactic against
living bacteria. We see from Sydney Alfred Hospi a
report that the typhoid death-rate last year was s p
cent, in 88 cases. The Hospital has 500 rules ! Main-
tenance of pauper patients cost 3s. 6d. per day. But n
much out of the £7955 expended on salaries was pam w
the medical staff we cannot see, for the balance-sne
gallantly lumps all wages and salaries together.

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Omar at the Shanty.
I think that Noses never wax so red
As where the Northern Beers their fragrance
shed ;
That one fell Drop of potent Northern dew
Hath power to stir the Bones of Northern dead.
Oh ! many a Potion-blender have I spied
And marked him con his wondrous “ Brewers
Guide,”
And noted then the Liquor that he drew
And how the Drinkers drank and fought and lied;
And how the House was festive Night and Day,
And how the Moon herself was waxen gay,
And how in curious Spirals men would walk
And hug the savory Gutter where they lay;
And how they gave the Mob their varied views,
Or painted Things in wild and lurid hues,
And told of many a Foeman they had slain
What time their Arms were mighty with the
Booze.
Some for the Sodas of this World—and some
Pine for the lips of Lemonade to come ;
Ah, take the Beer and let the Rubbish go,
Nor heed the voice of him who flouteth Rum !
They say the casual Copper watch doth keep
For them who, Jamshyd-like, have drunken deep,
But neither Fear of Jail nor Copper’s Club
May break in Northern land a Boozer’s sleep.
Yea —Tanglefoot hath laid them on the Sod,
And little care they now for Beak or God,
Till Pawning brings the Trap who leads them
Home
And Day reveals th’ accursed Walls of Quod.
Q.
P.L.
My Busliland Home.
Oh, I long for a breath of the old mountain breeze,
All laden with incense of flower and of leaf ;
And I sigh for the voices of murmuring trees
That laughed with my laughter, and sighed
with my grief.
There were ripples of song in the purl of the
creek
That wound through a tangle of blossoming
vine;
There was gold on the hills where each glittering
peak,
Thro’ dawn mists of silver, rose, flushed and a-
shine.
There were diamonds too, where the tussock-grass
waved—
A tremulous sea, silver-crested with dew ;
And the pearls from the pool, where the water-
fowl laved,
Flashed sprays of white gems from their wings
as they flew.
And the charm of the voices that woke with the
dawn
In a clamorous chorus of melody sweet!
And the glint of the gold in the murmuring corn,
When the springy turf flashed ’neath the gallop-
ing feet !
And at dusk when the shadows, with garments of
gray,
Stole soft in the tracks of the vanishing light,
The wind crooned a dirge for her lover, the Day,
And the silent stars rode in the van of the Night.
N.S.W.
A. CAR. B.
“So-Lonff!”
THE SONG OF LONELY WOMEN.
With a laugh they ride to the West away : “ So-
long ! ” from the road they call;
But the echoes ring there night and day, and the
bush-girls hear them fall.
“ So-long ! ” they cry as they take the track for
sheds at the back of Bourke;
But the notes resound for the hearts in black at
the dawn and the twilight murk.
“ So-long ! So-long ! ’Tis a sad heart-song that
rings in the bush-girl’s ear,
When the boys ride out with a farewell shout that
rings all the lonely year.
With a kiss they mount at the slip-rail gate,
with a good-bye squeeze they go ;
With a smile they ride for the Fields of Fate
where the golden apples grow !
“So-long, old girl; be back next year !”—but the
“ next year ” turns to three,
And still each lone bush-heart can hear “So-long”
ring endlessly !
Farewell! Farewell! Oh, they ring love’s knell
when they sing that two-word song !
Good-bye ! Good-bye ! Oh, no eyes are dry when
the boys call out “ So-long ! ”
“ So-long 1” —each day is an ason dark, and the
nights seem never to end
When the girls gaze out at the stringy-bark and
the tracks that twist and trend !
They wait and wait, and they guess and guess,
“ where Jack and Tom are now ’ —
There are eyes oft wet and hands that press at
dawn to the breast and brow !
“So-long! So-long!”—how the echoes throng,
and the hearts are sad and sore
For the arms they knew when they came to woo
in the old bush nights of yore !
W. A.

A LONG PERIOD OF SUFFERING.
A Permanent Cure in Newtown.
The Case of Mrs. C. WALKED
(For the “ Independent ”j
A subject necessarily inviting to all, and
the discussion of which has of late years
employed many able pens, was recently
touched upon by Mrs. Gertrude Walker, of
No. 17 George-street, Newtown, when chat-
ting with our special representative.
“It is in reference to the most effective
means of gettiug rid of one’s personal
enemies that I wish to speak to you,” said
Mrs Walker, “and I think that I am
entitled to speak on the subject because my
liver was out of order for a long time and
my sufferings were extraordinary. lam
twelve 3 T ears older since my troubles begin
with an attack of neuralgia, which I
endured for three months without abate-
ment. The terrific pains lodged in the back
of my head at first, then they affected my
gums, which became very sore, making it
impossible to properly masticate my food,
and tlfen the tortures shot up to my eyes.
Later on there was not a part of my head
which escaped the maddening agonies of
neuralgia, and often I thought they would
drive me out of my mind.”
“ I expect you got but very little sleep ? ”
enquired the write*’.
“ Sleep was deeded me altogether,” re-
sponded Mrs. Walker. “Cften I have paced
the floor for hours when everybody else has
been fast asleep, and when morning came I
was not fit for anything. My nerves were
all of a shake, nervousness having such a
hold upon me that I was afraid of my own
shadow, and when a knock came to the door
it caused me to tremble so violently that I
could scarcely move. I was afraid at every
turn, an idea being in my mind continually
that somebody was standing behind me,
though when I looked round there was
nobody, and I could only reproach myself
for my folly. Still, I could not help feeling
timid, and to be so every moment of the day
had the effect of making me fretful and
despondent. I lost flesh till I became as
thin as a skeleton, for which I blame my
poor appetite and the trouble I had in keep-
ing my food down. My stomach was in a
dreadfully weak condition, a fact which
accounted for the frequent fits of vomiting
I had, for it was all the same in effect what
kind of food I took—it would not stay
down. Many a time I thonght I was going
to be suffocated by the sense of oppression
in my chest, for when it was at its worst I
had to gasp for breath in a most alarming
way. ’f could not make out what was
wrong a little distance below my neck.
There seemed to be something pricking me
there—a very tantalising sensation—although
at other times the pain was as great as if I
had strained myself. I cannot imagine
anything more vile than the taste which was
in my piouth, and to make it more un-
pleasant still there was a eoating on my
tongue which made me feel quite sick. On
arising from bed I was distressed by the aches
running through my legs and across my
loins, for they set at defiance any attempt I
might make to do any light work about the
house, although at the best of times I was
so easily overpowered by exhaustion that I
had to let matters take their own course.”
“ Did you obtain advice from any
source ? ”
“ I should rather think so. A doctor
from Sydney was doing his best for me, yet
I cannot say there was anything satisfactory
resulting from his treatment. lam sure his
medicines did not benefit me, because my
internal troubles multiplied week by week.
If I walked a few yards I was brought to a
standstill by an attack of giddiness, and on
many sccasions when I walked a little
distance in the garden a mysterious sensa-
tion come over me which made it appear
that the further I walked the further the
fence seemed to be away. And then my
heart gave rise to serious reflections. The
slightestexcitementorexerbion always caused
it to flutter and throb, but even when I was
sitting still it used to suddenly start pal-
pitating with such vengeance that its
beatings could be heard quite distinctly.
The sharp pains which ran through my heart
from time to time also lent color to the idea
that it was diseased in some way or other, and
as the physic I was taking did not alleviate
my sufferings in the least, it appeared
certain that I was going to be an invalid
for life. With my yellow skin and my
features distorted by the physicial agony
and the anxiety of mind I was enduring, I
looked as if my days were almost numbered,
but now, thank Heaven, there is a great
difference in my appearance, and although
I am over thirty years of age, I feel as young
and active as a girl of sixteen.”
“Can you account for such a wonderful
transformation.”
“ Easily Clements Tonic did it, and only
for an old friend who told me about that
medicine I might still be suffering, or per-
haps be dead, as nothing else could cure me.
Several days passed before I felt any better
by taking Clements Tonic, and I was
beginning to think that it was no benefactor
of mine, but I kept on with it and then I
commenced to feel revived. The neuralgic
pains, I noticed, were dying away, and then
they suddenly stopped; but although this
was a great relief, I was more concerned
about my internal organs, and I was in a
state of painful suspense as to whether
Clements Tonic was equal to the occasion.
Sure enough it was. I became regular in
all my habits, freed from the unpleasant
taste in my mouth, my tongue was cleansed,
and I had an appetite to enjoy my food.
The wind which used to arise from my
stomach did not form now, and I never had
the least inclination to vomit my food. I
thought this was a remarkably satisfactory
test of the merits of Clements Tonic,
and when my first purchase was exhausted
I got more, as I meant to get quite well
whilst I had the chance. From that time
onwards I gained strength by leaps and
bounds. As regularly as I lay down to rest,
I had calm and refreshing sleep, and I
did appreciate it after passing so many
wretched nights not long before. The
freedom with which I could draw my breath
was evidence that my digestive organs were
being benefited by the use of Clements
Tonic, and as my heart had resumed its
normal pulsation, without ever paining me
either, I knew that it was not diseased, but
that my anxious fears had been induced by
the bad state of my liver. For my nervous
system there was nothing I had taken which
could approach Clements Tonic for general
excellence, as my nerves were left without
a tremor and I could walk anywhere in the
dark without any fear. By adhering
to the use of Clements Tonic for some weeks
I became as healthy as I have ever been, and
for several years my constitution has beqn
perfectly sound. If these admisssons will
assist people similarly afflicted, you may
publish them in any way you please.”

There are men in this world who are almost successful, almost religious, almost well, almost
happy. But a miss is as good as a mile. A recruit who is 5 feet 9 inches in height, when the
Standard is 5 feet 10 inches, is rejected just as surely as if he were only 4 feet in height. It is of
little avail how valiant, and how strong, and how experienced, and how willing to fight he may be.
He is not up to the standard required, and so he is rejected.
If you run and jump 7 feet after a ferry boat leaving the dock, and the ferry boat is 7! feet
away, you fall into the water just as surely and get just as wet as a little child who only jumps
one foot, and you sink just as deep, for you both go to the bottom.
“ Almost ” is a dangerous word. It is so alluring, but is really so deceptive. What you want
is a certainty. You want to be assured. You want definite information. You want —not “almost,”
but absolutely—the best, YOU want a Ruberoid Roof.
Fremantle, W.A.
J. M. Ferguson, Ltd.
Adelaide, S.A,

SATURDAY, MAY 2, 1903.
The Case for Private Enterprise.
A conspicuous feature of the attempted Tory
reaction in N.S.AV. (and, indeed, in all Australia)
is the daily newspaper concert to sing the praises
of “Private Enterprise,” and to chant a mournful
wail over the decay of the community through
the “selfishness and ignorance of the Labor
party.” This is most of all noticeable in N.S.W.,
where the Sydney Telegraph heads the chorus
and gives to its readers daily a brew of reactionary
misrepresentation, which bears clear traces of
Melbourne Argus and Brisbane Courier, as well
as local influences, A very few months ago the
same D.T. had nothing too good to say of the
Labor party, and whatever views it had as to the
immorality of State interference in private enter-
prise it kept carefully concealed. Whilst Mr. G. H.
Reid w r as in power and the Labor party was
supporting him and encouraging him to substitute
public control for private enterprise, and whilst
Mr. Bill Lyne held office by the aid of the
Labor party, with a more accentuated policy in
the same direction, the Daily Xelegraph dis-
seinbled its opinion of that party. But then
things began to happen. The Daily lelegraph
all that while had had a greedy privy paw of its
own in the State Treasury. Though a remarkably
profitable Private Enterprise, it had been “leaning
up against the State, 55 had been steadily refrain-
ing from “going out into the bracing open air
of free competition,” had been taking advantage,
of a heavy protective postal tariff against inter-
state competition, and had been drawing a big
subsidy in the shape of free railway carriage as
well. When this was stopped, largely at the
instance of a member of the Labor party, the
Telegraph turned, with an embittered heart
and a hand on which the Treasury door had been
slammed, to view public help to Private Enter-
prise in quite a new light. It thus has a reason
for special bitterness against the Labor party ;
but, probably, in any case it would now' be crying
out'that the Labor party is ruining the country.
Hard times are coming—not so much because of
the drought, djtfTdiecause the borrowing boom is
bursting and London is reluctant to lend more
money, partly owing to doubts about the security,
partly owing to the fact that London has not too
much to lend to anyone just now. A scapegoat
to bear the sins of all the politicians is w'anted, and
the daily newspapers consider that the Labor
party is the best scapegoat. They all think so
and preach so, but the Daily Telegraph, with its
mind soured by the loss of its subsidy, thinks so
with special fervor.
, * » *
Tt was the Telegraph—with its own little grie-
vance lurking in the background—which presented
to its readers last week a remarkable collection of
opinions on Private Enterprise. There were
opinions from gentlemen w'ho know nothing,
from gentlemen in their dotage, and from gentle-
men who know something but who were not going
to let that interfere with telling a good lie. All
voiced the one cry—that the Labor party was
ruining the country by discouraging Private
Enterprise, and all showed either a pathetic
imbecility or an extraordinary belief in the
gullibility of the public. The occasion for
collecting their opinions was, appropriately
enough, given to the Telegraph by a speech
from Premier Philp, of Queensland, glorify-
ing Private Enterprise— Philp, whose Govern-
ment in Queensland has so conspicuously identified
Private Enterprise with public robbery. Since
the Telegraph (with its desire for Tory reaction
and with its private grievance in the background)
probably took care to interview the people who
could make out the best case for its side, the three
columns printed by it on April 23 may be fairly
taken to represent the complete case for “ Private
Enterprise.” , • •
Summarised, the opinions of the dozen or so
gentlemen whose tangled views make up that
case are: That the Labor party is ruining the
country by its opposition to Private Enterprise ;
that Private Enterprise—the upgrowth of sturdy,
self-reliant industries free from State help
and State interference—is the only hope of
the. country’s revival; that employment is
now diminishing; that the investment of private
capital is diminishing; and that the money
in the banks is diminishing. These three last
statements (which are only the first two in more
detail) may, without tracing them directly to the
particular mental incapable or deliberate falsifier
who uttered them, be summarily dealt with in a
word ; they are simply lies. In N.S.W. more
workmen are now employed by Private Enter-
prise than ever before. Private employment
in manufactures (which are comparatively
free from drought influences) is increasing at
a greater comparative rate than ever before.
Capitalists are investing their money in N.S.W.
industries ( industries , as distinguished from land-
jobbing companies and bogus banks and concerns
that discount each other’s bills, and the other
useless and noxious growths of the boom days)
at a far greater rate than they did when there was
no Labor party. The banks have as much money
as before, if not a little more. The most
casual reference to Coghlan’s statistics will prove
this. Taking the year 1893 (as a time when the
Labor party had been in existence some two years,
and was, presumably, making its influence felt) to
compare with 1902, these facts show out for
N.S.W.:
1893. 1902.
Persons employed in factories .. 42,057 .. 66,135
And this comparison is of value :
1893. 1901.
Savings Bank Deposits .. .. £6,535,758 £11,808,710
Gentlemen, who are said to be prominent bankers
and merchants, are presumably able to read and
understand plain figures like these, and The
Bulletin is driven to the unfortunate conclusion
that an absolutely reckless disregard of the truth
frequently marks the Tory capitalist.
• • • •
Considering the various champions of Private
Enterprise in detail there is none more pathetic
than ex-Premier Dibbs. He tells the Telegraph
that he “goes with Mr. Philp all the way in his
plea for Private Enterprise and his condemnation
of State interference. Yet Mr. Dibbs is now
manager of the Savings Bank, a private institu-
tion which was helped over a great crisis by State
interference, in the shape of a guarantee,
that interference being supplied by George
Dibbs himself in his then capacity as Premier.
The institution out of which George Dibbs
makes his living still leans up against that
guarantee and shows no signs of casting it off
as an unclean thing and an unjustifiable inter-
ference with Private Enterprise. And when Mr.
Dibbs was Premier he helped all the banks over a
crisis by State interference, giving the guarantee
of the State to their note issue. In both these
matters he acted rightly, and it was only because
he was a firm, honest, capable financial adminis-
trator that he saw the right course and was able
to take it. Private Enterprise had at that crisis
brought the State to the brink of ruin ; had in-
flicted a blow at its credit which is still felt, and
will be felt for another generation. And George
Dibbs stood in the path, as the country was being
dragged into bankruptcy by Private Enterprise,
and saved it by State interference. To hear
George Dibbs now dodder about the evils of
State interference, causes sorrow rather than
anger—sorrow that so strong and capable a man
can’t always remain young and vigorous. But
there are no mixed feelings with regard to the
brassy impudence of Burns, Philp and Co,, who,
through their Sydney representative, contributed
this to the discussion :
With ali the artificial and experimental legislation now
in vogue, with Factory Acts, Labor Arbitration Courts,
and many other disabilities, no sane man could he
induced to embark in any new industry, and perforce
employ workmen, who could harass him and force him
to lose his money and his time, and crush out any enter-
prise he might naturally possess. One of the most dis-
heartening influences we have in Australia is the utter
selfishness of the so-called Labor legislators, who legis-
late for the hour only, and who are sowing the seed
which must eventually 7 lead to ruin and disaster if per-
severed in.
Apart from the falsehoods in this {e.g., “that no
sane man,” etc. —it is not possible to believe that
all the people who are now rushing to establish
new industries in N.S.W. are insane), anything
in praise of Private Enterprise from Burns, Philp
and Co. merits a scoff. There is no organisation
in Australia which has leaned up against the
Government to a greater extent—for shipping
subsidies, for black labor aid, for sugar loans and
meat works advances. It has been the sturdy
beggar of Queensland (where the sort of Private
Enterprise which resolves itself into getting the
State to take the risk of loss, whilst the private
individual takes all the profit, especially flourishes)
and has filled in its moments of leisure by tapping
the N.S.W. Government on various pretexts. No
sane Treasurer would leave a spoon lying about
where that hungry infant was.
» » • •
The impudent ignorance, or worse, of the con-
tributors to the great Private Enterprise pajan
comes out strongly also in the statement of
Larke, Canadian Commissioner, “ that the
Government of Canada does not undertake as
much work as those of Australia.” The Blue
Nose gentleman, if he had had an atom of candor,
would have stated that in Canada, of all places in
the world, Private Enterprise confesses itself a
dismal failure and relies on State inter-
ference and State aid for its support. The
Canadian Government interferes to help the
railways with land and money grants, to help the
farming interest, to help immigration and almost
every big industry. By its bonus system it has
only recently built up a great iron enterprise.
Speaking generally, nothing large or important
has been successful in Canada without Govern-
ment interference and assistance. In the
United States, too, land-grants for railways,
bonuses and prohibitive tariffs for industries,
enormous subsidies and State-enforced monopolies
for shipping companies, show how much Private
Enterprise is indebted to State interference for its
success. Despite which, the Telegraph allows a
Yankee person to bounce in with his little story
that all Government meddling with industry is
disastrous. But the sublime height of shocking
ignorance is reached when one <7. S. Brunton is
allowed to instance the Maffra sugar works
(Victoria) and the Queensland, sugar mills as
instances where Government interference lias
wrecked Private Enterprise. The Maffra sugar
works were founded by Private Enterprise, bled
the State Treasury for all that was possible, and
when a limit was put to their beggings and borrow-
ings they failed. The Queensland sugar milling
industry under Private Enterprise was a ghastly
failure until the State interfered with special
legislation and generous loans, when it began to
flourish, just as the Queensland meat industry
began to flourish when State interference gave it
a stimulus.
• * * •
A little apart from the main issue, but inextri-
cably woven with it in the Telegraph’s exhibition
of bumptious ignorance and shameless falsehood,
was a condemnation of the Labor party as a
“ Communist party.” A certain O. C. Beale
was especially strong on this point. A nodding
acquaintance with the elementary principles of
political economy would teach him that there is
notiiing communistic in the Labor party platform
though it is in a measure State-socialistic. Com-
munism is an essential part of the Christian reli-
gion, but it is never put into practice in any
Christian country, and the Labor party makes no
suggestion in that direction.
• • * *
Summed up, the case for Private Enterprise, as
put forward by its chosen champions, amounts
to this: A large amount of senile ignorance;
a large amount of simple falsehood ; . and
the citing, as instances of successful Private
Enterprise, of undertakings which have re-
ceived generous aid from the State and nave
flourished in consequence of that aid. Private
Enterprise likes the State to interfere when it is
threatened with disaster ; it rejoices as it collects
huge subsidies from the State Treasury ; it resents
State interference only when a Government
enforces on it some conditions of decent wage-
paying and hours of labor in the treatment of its
employees. State interference to protect capital
from loss, to add to the profits of capital, is a holy
and blessed thing. But State interference to
come to the rescue of tl»e destitute old soldier of
industry, crippled and maimed in the battle of
life, is a thing accursed; and State interference
between the capitalist (who, perhaps, relies on
the State for his profits) and the workman whose
bones he wishes to grind in the sweating mill,
is abominable. The State may help those
who have no exigent need of help, but a
lying slander and a curse on its head if it dares
to give a hand to the weak and the oppressed.
“Private Enterprise,” as it is advocated in its
newspapers and by its capitalist, captains in
Australia, is indeed an ignoble thing, pitiful in
its ignorance, shameless in its mendacity, dis-
gusting in its mendicancy and its greed. It is as
a sturdy, sinewy beggar —with one hand, half
demanding, half enforcing alms ; with the other,
striving to push away the widow, the orphan, and
the aged poor.
Double-banking the Bill of Sale.
Just when the Premiers of Australia are on the
verge of apoplexy with rage, shock and grief at
the aspersions cast on this country’s credit, they
have themselves combined to devise a gross politi-
cal fraud which isn’t calculated to improve public
opinion concerning us. The fact that Federal
Treasurer Turner has put an honest and emphatic
foot down on the proposed fraud makes things
better, but not so very much better; there still
remains the fact that the fraud has been pro-
posed ; that it has been cordially endorsed
by the State Premiers—the representatives of
about two hundred millions of accumulated
public debt ; and that it shows the sort of
characters to whom all that money has been
entrusted. The device was a curious and
unobtrusive one. The Commonwealth Govern-
ment has taken over from the State Govern-
ments about £12,000,000 or £14,000,000 worth
of property buildings, telegraphs and sun-
dries. This property was almost wholly con-
structed out of loans ; it is practically mortgaged'
to Bull and others for its full value. Possibly,
allowing for depreciation, it is mortgaged for
more than its value. The States have no interest
in it whatsoever, except as agents for the real
owners Cohen and Co., mostly of London.
Therefore the honest and obvious course is for the
Commonwealth to take over the buildings, &c.,
and an amount of State debt corresponding to
their value, and let the thing remain at that.
• • * *
The State Premiers, however, view the matter
quite differently. They propose that the
Commonwealth Government shall take over
the State debts, which is, in itself, a highly
commendable idea, and one that can t be
carried out too soon. Also they propose that
the Commonwealth Government shall pay sepa-
rately for the State assets taken over, either with
per cent, bonds (the States can sell these and
have a great drunk on the proceeds) or with cash
raised by the sale of these bonds in the open mar-
ket (the States can have a corresponding drunk on
the cash). This will give the States £12,000,000 or
£l4 000,000 with which to have a fine, large series
of orgies. It will add £12,000,000 or £14,000,000 to
the aggregate Australian debt without there being
one pennyworth more in the way of reproductive
assets to show for it. It will put two mortgages
on the same security, each equal to the full value
of the security, and thereby bring Australia down
to the level of the petty swindler who borrows
£lOO from Cohen on his furniture, and then,
without Cohen’s permission, fraudulently mort-
gages the same goods for £lOO to Moses. The
post-office worth £lOOO would, under this sys-
tem, have £2OOO liability upon it—first, the
£ 1000 which the State borrowed to build it,
and second, the £lOOO which the Commonwealth
borrowed to buy it off the State. And the con-
ference of State Premiers had the unspeakable
gall to propose that the Commonwealth should
take the responsibility of both liabilities. It was
seriously suggested that the Australian Govern-
ment should take over the State debts, and pay
for the assets which partially represent these debts
as well; that it should take over the post office
which is—or was—worth £lOOO, and should under-
take, by assuming the State debt, to pay the £lOOO
which was borrowed to build that post office, and
should pay £lOOO cash for the post office as well.
Under this policy the Australian Government
would have £14,000,000 more to find interest on ;
and the States would have £14,000,000 more
loan money to use as revenue; and the public
creditor would have £14,000,000 less security for
his debt, or £14,000,000 more of his debt wouldn’t
be represented by visible security. It is only
in a country where politicians, in matters of
public finance, have become rotten to. the core,
that such a proposal could be entertained. All
the empty unprofitable wrath of B. It. Wise and
others, at the people who basely cast reflections on
Australia’s credit, is of no avail, while the Ministry
to which B. R. Wise belongs, and the other
equally disreputable State Ministries, insist on
dragging Australia’s good name in the gutter, by
devising schemes like this for mortgaging one
piano to two Jews.
• • * •
Yet strangely enough this proposed political
dodge has friends in quite a number of
influential quarters. Richard Teece, of the
A.M.P. Society, apparently approves of it, and
Bruce Smith, M.H.R., is almost violent on the
subject. This is his view of the matter, given
with the promptness of a cock-robin stating his
opinions over a freshly turned-up worm :
My own opinion is that the properties should be paid
tor out of Commonwealth moneys; and that money
should be borrowed on loan by the Commonwealth for
the purpose. Whether a loan should be actually floated
now, or whether negotiable bonds of the Commonwealth
should he handed to the States is, a matter that should
depend upon the opportunities for floating a loan for the
purpose.
Some fear has been expressed that if the purchase
money for the properties is handed over to the States
they will spend it, either by disposing of the cash itself
or by disposing of the bonds or debentures; but I do not
think the Commonwealth has any right to meddle with
that aspect of the subject. The Commonwealth has no
paternal jurisdiction over the States, under the Consti-
tution ; and the States Parliaments should be trusted to
guard their own. If any Parliament—as, for instance,
that of New South Wales—should allow the Government
of the day to spend the purchase money, instead of
making a partial and corresponding reduction of its
national debt, then the State in question deserves to lose
it all for electing such a Parliament, and tolerating such
a Government.
The point which never seems to soak into the
Smith order of intelligence is that while the people
of Australia are the States, they also are the
Commonwealth, and it is just as much the duty of
the Government of all Australia to prevent its
Seople being swindled as it is the duty of the
rovernments of parts of Australia. The Aus-
tralian Government in this matter has been
asked to perpetrate a fraud on its own people to
oblige the subordinate State Governments—to
help to load its own people with £12,000,000 or
£14,000,000 of needless debt —and to actively
assist at mortgaging to asecond Jew the piano that
is already mortgaged to the first Jew. The Aus-
tralian Government has an equal voice with the
States in deciding how the price of State properties
taken over is to be paid ; and the theory that it
can do nothing but helplessly acquiesce when
the State Premiers propose that it should
swindle its own people to oblige them won’t hold
water or any other fluid. It is a good thing for
Australia’s credit and fair name that Treasurer
Turner has declared flatly against the un-
principled juggle that has been suggested. But
the fact that the representatives of the State
Governments to which Bull has lent nearly
£200,000,000 should propose such a thing is cal-
culated to make that already agitated lender feel
even worse than he feels already.

B. R. Wise’s Defence.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wise, who knows
an immense deal about law, and, ® finance,
public is aware, next to nothing a mter .
has returned to Australia and h RS <’ _ tll< jiess
viewer how shocked he is at the g ipf, e
aspersions cast on this country s ere* • t
story told by B. R. Wise is, unfortunately,
The “Great Nation” Craze.
The S.M. Herald is howling at Australia to
awake, and arise, and pull herself together, and
gird up her loins, and buck up, and become a
great nation without delay. The way to do this
is—according to the S.M.H.—absurdly clear and
simple. Australia must first give up her “exclu
sionist policy,” and throw open her gates to all
comers—or nearly all. Population will then flow
in like a tidal wave and—there you are:
If education count for aught,
A schoolboy should despise
This old barbaric mode of thought
Which counts as greatness—Size.
When Attica her glory won,
She was—and did not weep
No bigger than a Queensland run ;
But she grew more than sheep.
She did not boast about the mass
Of folk of all degrees—
But she could raise a Phidias,
Plato, and Socrates.
When Israel was a little folk
Beneath the Eastern sky,
Her Poets and her Prophets spoke
The words that cannot die.
The Great Republic of the West
Counts millions ; is there one
Amongst her Bravest and her Best
Like old George Washington?
Yet he came ambling on the scene
When there were really far
Less people in the States Thirteen
Than in Australia are.
What makes a State ? Old W illiam J ones
Has said it. Listen then
’Tis not the sum of men she owns
It is the kind of men.
Let be ; our time has not come yet,
Though we already boast
A large and stately Public Debt
That will compare with most.
A Nation, we may be assured
However smart and bright,
Will not grow up like Jonah’s gourd
To full size in a night.
Nor sum nor size will make us great,
Bulk need not carry Brain ;
Not oft the Big Electorate
A Big Man can obtain.
Meantime, what are we waiting for?
If each doe > what he can
A common Cow Flat councillor,
Or Five Dock alderman,
May be—if he will, as his own,
The cause of Progress boost
As great as Edward on his Throne,
Or Roosevelt on his Roost.
CREEVE ROE.


much more convincing than the other stories we
have heard, but, for what it is worth, it reads
thus:—
It was difficult not to be carried away by indignation
at the baseness and malignity of some of the
charges against Australia, but i' refrained from any
controversy of a personal character, and preferred rather
to deal with the larger questions which critics raised.
For instance, in dealing with our finances I purposely
avoided mere questions of accounts and tried rather to
point out that the true basis of a nation’s credit is the
character of its people, and that in all essential respects
of honesty and readiness and ability to make sacrifices in
order to redeem their obligations, the Australian
people stood second to none; so that so long as
we and our Governments continued the same, we would
no more repudiate our obligations than as a people we
would lie or steal. I also pointed out that the amount
of our indebtedness was most unfairly stated, and that
for any purpose of comparison with the indebtedness of
other countries account must be taken of sums which
elsewhere are spent upon public works by private
persons but which in Australia are spent upon similar
works by the Government.
“The true basis of a nation’s credit is the
character of its people.” That is a true but very
stereotyped statement, and after it is made things
are no “forrarder.” What is the character, in
financial matters, of the Australian people? B.
R. Wise baldly states that it is Al, and that this
country’s yearning to pay its way is something
tremendous, and that its people would no more
fail to meet their obligations than they would lie
(which they do occasionally) or steal (which some-
times happens). The Bulletin states that it
doesn’t know how the national financial character
will turn out when the pinch comes, but if the
bulk proves anything like the sample, and if the
future is anything like the present, and if the
frantic and ruinous Government to which B. R.
Wise belongs is any criterion, it will turn out
very badly.. If Australia is so anxious to pay all
its liabilities; why should it not demonstrate the fact
by doing a little in that way now ? Why should not
N.S.W. lead the way, and why should not the
Government to which B. R. Wise belongs set an
example? The finances of N.S.W. under the
Government to which B. R. Wise belongs are one
long lie, steal and failure to pay the State’s just
obligations. Every year the Government tacitly lies
by implying that from £500,000 to £900,000 worth
of works are “ permanent and reproductive” when
they are not, and it knows they are not; yet it
charges them to loans as permanent and repro-
ductive. Almost every year, by adding a new
deficit to the accumulated liabilities, the State,
especially under the guidance of the Government
to which B. R. Wise belongs, fails to redeem its
obligations. Every year the State, especially
under the guidance of the Government to which
B. R. Wise belongs, shows its alleged readiness to
make sacrifices to keep its credit good by refusing
to pay enough taxes to cover its outgoings. One
main reason why the world begins to suspect that,
the day foreign loans cease to be obtainable,
Australia will be within measurable distance of
default, is the gross and shameful lack of political
principle shown by the Government to which
B. R. Wise belongs. When, after charging about
£BOO,OOO a year of ordinary, everyday, unproduc-
tive expenditure to loans, and thereby relieving
the revenue to the extent of that huge political
swindle, it won’t take the trouble to make the
rest of the accounts even look square and respec-
table, the outside world is apt to size up the
Government to which B. R. Wise belongs as one
which is likely to repudiate, when foreign loans
give out, rather,_than face the struggle to pay
up. It think! this simply on the ground
that the Governmtent which won’t face a small
trouble isn’t likely to be any more good in a large
one, and that the Government which doesn’t care
enough for the State’s credit to keep it clean when
the task is comparatively easy isn’t likely to be
much good at it when the task is difficult. It is
all very good for B. R. Wise to be carried away
with indignation at the charges made against
Australia’s credit; his wrath is nothing to the
indignation of people who really understand
finance, that the Government to which he belongs
should have made these charges inevitable. The
real basis of our credit is the character of the
the people ! Well, what is the character of a
people which has in 22£ years had 16 deficits, even
after using about £9,000,000 of loan money for
revenue purposes, and which refuses even now to
realise that there is anything seriously wrong?
The Australians may be, politically, as honest as
B. R. Wise says they are, but in N.S.W. at least
the Government to which B. R. Wise belongs is
doing its best to teach them to be as dishonest as
their worst enemy says they are. Judged by its
record up to date, the Government to which
B. R. Wise belongs looks more like a Government
that is likely to repudiate when the supply of
loans stops than most other Governments.

Day Labor.
N.S.W. “Reform” party, led by Carruthers,
breaks out now, though not yet frankly, against
the day labor system. From its very nature, it is
alleged, day labor cannot be best. But if the
“Reformers” believe that, and want to be fair
all round, why does this crowd not make the con-
tract system general ? If the contract system
must always be the best, why not let out the
police work, the Post Office, and the Lands
Department ? The law work might also be put
out in the same way ; likewise the collection of
taxes of all kinds. The “Reform” crowd pro-
duces a ‘ ‘ well-known citizen ” who points out
that—
A contractor who may be the lowest tenderer for one
job may be 50 per cent, the highest tenderer for another
job. What is the inference? One man may see a means
of carrying out work more economically than another
man ; one tenderer may propose to carry out work with
the pick and shovel, another with the plough, another
with the scoop, and somebody else may have a still
further improved manner of carrying it out, so that the
lowest tenderer, although perhaps only half that of the
highest, will be able to carry out the work, paying full
wages, and still make a substantial profit for himself.
J ust so. The State as a tax-collector, employing
men by the day or the year, has no resource. A
private person, with a contract to do the job,
would suit his methods to the tax-ovver —he
would sit on one’s doorstep, another he would
waylay in his pub., he would know everybody’s
pay day, he might even try a little force
as they do where the job is still done
by this “ essentially-best system.” And, after
“shouting” for everybody who paid up
promptly, he would “still make a substantial
profit for himself.” The collection of taxes having
thus been successfully performed by contract,
tenders might next be called for the supply of a
Parliamentary Opposition. At present Car-
rutiikrs and the rest are paid by the day, and
from its very nature, it is alleged, that system
cannot be best. Likewise there is a large scope
for the application of this “ essentially-best ” con-
tract system in connection with the Governors
and the Judges. One Governor “may see a
means of carrying out the work more economically
than another man ”■— he may, for instance, make
the ball sandwiches of corned beef instead of
chicken, and thus, while still doing the work,
and paying the aides-de-camp the Trades Hall
standard rate for aides-de-camp, he may “make
a substantial profit for himself.” Wherefore that
job ought also to be done by contract. All these
things, however, are never mentioned. Even the
people who swear loudest about day labor stop
suddenly less than half-way along, and are not
game to apply their cure to anybody who wears a
starched shirt— their faith in it doesn’t carry
them that far.

Tlia River Foolishness.
After appointing a commission of experts to go
deeply into the whole question of the use of the
Murray waters, the State Premiers, who are not
experts, have thrown the other people’s recom-
mendations into a corner, and have agreed to
something altogether different, so far as they can
agree to anything without the sanction of Parlia-
ment. A choice has to be made between naviga-
tion and irrigation, because there isn’t enough wet
for both purposes ; and as railways are more or
less of a substitute for navigation, while nothing
can be provided that is anything of a substitute at
all for irrigation, the obviously proper thing is to
preserve and use for irrigation the little water
that there is. It would be a good thing to have a
navigable river, of course ; but it is no use having
a navigable river through a desert. Yet if we
insist upon having a navigable river (that is, if
we insist upon navigation to the mouth having
first call on the water), nobody in his senses will
venture upon a.big irrigation scheme, and so there
won’t be anything for the navigable river to carry
worth speaking of. Practically on these lines the
expert commission decided that S.A. (which
wants navigation to the mouth maintained)
could not reasonably claim a flow of more
than 70,000 cubic feet of water per minute,
in the four “ dry ” months of the year—the months
at the end of summer, and before the winter rains
come down. Even that was a pretty large con-
cession to make to a State which doesn’t provide
any of the water itself. The Premiers, however,
for no earthly reason of which the public are
aware, except that S. Australia’s Attorney-
General, J. H. Gordon, who was at the confer-
ence, has a winning way with him, have jumped
up the amount conceded to that State to 150,000
cubic ft. ; which means that, at the very time
when water will be needed most for irrigation, it
must be turned down, to flow into the Southern
Ocean. Nobody, knowing that the waterless
period at the end of summer may destroy all the
work that has been done during fair years, will
embark in irrigation experiments on these terms ;
so South Australia will have its river, but there
won’t be anything more for it to carry than the
few barge-loads of wool and sheepskins which it
carries to-day.

The N.S.W. Reform Party.
In four newspaper columns Joseph Carrtjthers
on Monday announced the N.S.W. Liberal and
Reform Party platform, and took up all that
space to say : “ I am Joseph, trust me and all
will be well.” With the exception of a proposal
to reduce the strength of the Assembly from 125
to 84 members there is not one definite assurance
in the whole windy document. It is all the smug
statement of Joseph that he is all right and that
the fleshpots of Egypt should be given to him, for
he will re-establish Responsible Government in its
integrity and restore the public credit. That occu-
pies one column of space. The next column states
thathe will re-establish thepublic credit and restore
Responsible Government, and that he is Joseph.
Two more columns say the same thing again—
especially that he is Joseph. There is not a word
about taxation, not a word about borrowing, nor
about spending loan money on non-productive
works. What points of public policy are touched
upon receive a gingerly yes-no treatment. The
Upper House may be reformed, but that question
must not be raised now. Local Government is
necessary, but it must not be after any method
hitherto proposed. A new system (details not
given) is hinted at, by which, apparently, the
country districts are to pay for their own
roads and bridges, but are not to be in any
way taxed to do so. Economy in the public
service is needed, but there is no suggestion of
reduction in public service salaries, or other form
of retrenchment. The manifesto is the weak and
weary document of a man who wobbles unsteadily
towards the distant vista of office, ready to take
any road so long as it is the sure one—a man who
wanted a portfolio in the See Government, but
was persuaded by evil advisers that if he waited a
little while he could have all the portfolios at his
disposal, and who has waited a long, long while,
and is now very hungry, and almost desperate.
The Joseph platform once more suggests
the sorrowful lack in N.S.W. State politics
of any party to which an intelligent elector
can give his vote at the next general election.
The See Government—the reckless, ruinous, ridi-
culous See Government, like a bull raging
through a pawnshop—is impossible. The Joseph
Opposition—smug, sleek, sly, with no alternative
policy, and no excuse for existing but office hunger
—is a shade more hopeless and repulsive. The
Labor party seems to be drifting daily more and
more into the position of being a mere parasite of
the Owe’Sullivan, a foolish little bull-calf follow-
ing the Government wherever it goes—mostly
into the pawnshop. Yet its opportunity is there.
If the Labor party chose to do so, it could probably
capture the government of the country at next
elections. The country is sick of Premier See ;
and it is more than sick of the anxious-to-be
Premier Carruthers and his windy denuncia-
tions of the Government he was so anxious to
join. Any new party promising something sane
and reasonable would, with proper advocacy, be
given a chance by the electors. Were the Labor
party to re-shape its policy and platform a little,
shake itself from off the Owe Government, and
come before the electors with an honest progres-
sive policy, it would almost certainly win power.
It has prospered in the past on its eagerness for
reforms, such as old age pensions, factories and
shop Acts, and industrial arbitration (which were
needed most of all by the city workers), and has
secured most of those reforms. But it allows its
progress to halt. What is called for now in the first
place is a reform policy designed for the Man
with the Hoe—a programme designed to end the
scandal which sends the settler out to the water-
less west whilst the rich coastal and central dis-
trict areas are monopolised by great landlords —
many of them absentees. What is further neces-
sary is a financial reform policy—a suggestion
as to how to carry on without borrowing which
must soon cease. A party with its wits about it
would strike out a rough balance-sheet for the
benefit of the electors: The ordinary services
of the country should cost so much—put us in
power and we will see that they cost no more;
the maintenance and extension of public works
must cost so much—if we are in power we will find
the money; the revenue is so much, we propose
to increase it to so much by such and such mean*,
and stop borrowing altogether. Our new taxes will
be a progressive land-tax, heavy taxes on absentees,
and what increases in the death duties and the
income-tax are necessarv. With such a platform
as that—a definite, straight proposal for extricating
the country out of its present state of wild con-
fusion, any party with a reasonably honest repu-
tation should gain the confidence of the electors.
It is not too late for the Labor party to move now.
Both the other parties have their announced plat-
forms. The See policy is to have a good time
or bust ; the Carruthers policy is to get into
office or bust. It is left to the Labor party to
propose something with no suggestion of bust.
