The "Primary Producer."
Sydney Telegraph, reporting that a few Aus-
tralians have gone to Canada, hangs thereupon a
comparison of Australian and Canadian progress,
and, looking at the matter cross-eyed, proceeds to
tell why. It is represented that Australia has
‘‘levied upon the new-comer from the moment he
seeks to make a home in the land,” whereas in
Canada newcomers are offered ‘ 1 free or very cheap
land . . . without any fear that they will be
called upon to pay higher taxation than at pre-
sent in order to ensure the national solvency.”
Even the D.T. sees that " the essential primary
producer ” in Australia has been so much
of a failure that he must be excused some-
how ; and this is the excuse ill-treatment
by the State. The best that can be said for
this is that it is as good a lie as the average
excuse. So far from ‘‘the primary producer”
being harshly treated in Australia, he has been
coddled as no other set of persons has been. He
has had land offered to him at low prices and on
ridiculously easy terms, and when he hasn’t been
able to pay the instalments the instalments
have been remitted. In hundreds of cases he
agreed to pay a price which the State subse-
quently reduced bodily by two-thirds. When he
has agreed to pay a certain rent for a term
and disaster has come, the State has had the
rent cut down by as much as 50 or 60 per cent.,
although it has been wretchedly hard-up itself at
the time. Even when the State has well known
that the “primary producer” has ill-used the
land, and that that has been a chief cause of his
failure, it has still coddled him and forgiven him
half the rent; and when he hasn’t paid the other
half it has not thought of turning him off. Parlia-
ments have made liberal land laws ; and then land
boards have been appointed, one of whose chief
businesses has been to find excuses for men
who have not complied with even the easy
conditions imposed by those laws. And on top
of it all the State in Australia has built railways
for the convenience and profit of the “primary
producers” on a scale which no other country in
the world has approached. At the request of
half-a-hundred settlers and men who have talked
vaguely of becoming settlers, it has run lines into
the desert places and has carried “primary pro-
ducers’ ” traffic at a price which hasn’t paid inte-
rest on outlay, let alone working expenses. This
has amounted, in a multitude of instances, to a
heavy State bonus on every pound of the “primary
producers’ ” product. On 16 N.S.W. branch
lines alone, built to serve the “primary pro-
ducer,” there is at present an annual call for a
State subsidy of £250,000. Also, the State has
built roads and bridges and culverts for him
instead of making him build them for himself; and
by largely subsidising his local agricultural shows it
has devised another plan of paying him for grow-
ing things. In its almost insane prodigality Aus-
tralia has, in various ways, incurred a debt of over
£215,000,000, very largely to make things good
and easy for “ the primary producer,” while all
Canada has only a debt of £87,000,000; and now
the Telegraph has the gall to allude to that fact
as proof that “the primary producer” has been
badly treated by Australia ! It certainly is the
fact that, in spite of the coddling, the “primary
producer ’’ has been to some extent a failure; but
that doesn’t call for still more coddling.

Touching 1 the Treatment of Tigers.
The Vic. Chief Commissioner of Police professes
to find fault with the State for “feeding and
pampering human tigers such as Shaw” (the
alleged Sydney murderer, who killed a constable
in Melbourne a while ago) and then “letting
them go free again to prey upon the public,”
The system ought to be reformed, says the Chief
Com. M ‘Cutcheon, M.L.A., who also spoke at
the unveiling of a monument to the murdered
Melbourne constable, thinks “it is ridiculous that
criminals should be kept in comfortable quarters
and fed well, whilst the honest poor of the city
almost starve in hovels.” Condemnation of the
gaol system comes more easily to these loud-
tongued reformers than any practical suggestions
for its improvement. Granting that Shaw afore-
said was found to be a human tiger after his
release from prison, how was the State
to become assured beforehand of his unfitness
to be given his liberty ? And if it is right to keep
suspected tigers in captivity all their days, and
wrong to give them wholesome food and comfort-
able quarters, what sort of food and quarters
would Chief Com. O’Callaghan and M'Cutchkon,
M.L.A., appoint for such criminals. All things
considered, the bitter cry of the boss policeman
is unintelligent and unbusinesslike. The Foorce
doesn’t want habitual criminals to disappear
from public life. It wants them to be let
loose frequently in order that they may pro-
vide “clues,” “clever arrests,” newspaper puffs
and promotions for the active and intelli-
gent officers who run them in on suspicion
in the first place and justify the suspicion
afterwards—as often as not. Of course, it
is disquieting to know that any one of the
“ habituals ” at large may prove himself a
murderous suicidal Shaw in the event of an
attempt to capture him, but the police can’t fairly
expect to have everything their own way. And it
is absurd to complain that the State makes prison
too comfortable. A “tiger ” who has had experi-
ence of all the alleged luxuries of gaol life should
be thereby deterred from the foolishness of killing
a policeman when he gets his liberty, seeing that
the wages of murder is death. There are no more
comfortable quarters and “pampering” for the
criminal who has been satisfactorily hanged.

A Person called “ Pull-Up.”
A curious letter signed “ Full-Up ” appeared in
a Sydney paper the other day. It read as
follows:
I am an employer. I confess it with shame. I have
been starting a new industry in a tentative way with a
very fair amount of sucoess. Before putting in machinery,
as Mr. Jambs Watson says in to-day’s Telegraph, ** I put
on my thinking cap ” and considered the inwardness of
our performing legislators. I was then paying £27 per
week in wages, and expecting to pay more. As a result
of “ thinking ” I now pay £l5 per week, and in a short
time I shall have wound up my business (unless indeed
our Labor pundits fall with a sharp thud at the coming
elections), and be moving to some free country where
industry, money, and brains are at a premium.
This is only one of the long series of letters about
the destruction of the country’s industries through
" socialistic legislation ” which appear nowadays,
and The Bulletin grows curious. It is curious
to know where the imaginary industries are, and
what they are. It would like to know what
deadbeat writes these letters, and how much beer
he gets for them ; or what junior clerk puts them
together, and whether his boss pays him extra for
them; or which reporter concocts them. For its
own part, it doesn’t believe there is any such per-
son as “Full-Up,” or any such industry as
“Full-Up ” describes ; it believes the £27 a week
is a lie, and the £l5 a week a falsehood;
and it considers that the whole story is a fraud
and imposture concocted by some silly Tory poli-
tician, landlord, importer, commisssion agent, or
some such trash. It holds this opinion because
Coghlan’s official statistics show that the manu-
facturing industries of N.S.W., though still
miserably small compared to what they should
be, are growing faster, both in the number of
factories and in the number of hands employed,
than they ever did before. And when it sees this
result, and also sees every few days the letter of
the person who is apparently ashamed to give his
name, stating that the industry which is ashamed
to give its address is closing up on account of
“socialistic legislation,” it is difficult to come to
any other conclusion than that the “Full-Up”
tribe is a sham from beginning to end.

A correspondent writes re the registry office
swindle, fraud, lie and steal:—
Wanted a man recently, so I coasted along Castlereagh-
street, Sydney, and one or two other streets where the
fin of the registry office shark is generally visible above
the surface, till I saw a likely looking one standing with
an expectant air outside a registry office. I called him
aside, and finding him suitable, explained that I had
hired him outside to save him from the rapacity of the
registry fiend who would otherwise have claimed'his first
week’s wage. The poor devil thanked me, and explained
that the greasy iniquity inside owed him 7s. 6d. already.
He had paid this sum a week previously for the address
of an employer who, on the arrival of the billet-seeker,
denied ever having given the registry office authority to
interfere with his affairs in any way. I urged him to go
in and demand his 7s. 6d. while I waited. A few minutes
later there erupted from the office a small angry man
who attacked me with great fury, and declared that I
had made myself liable to as much as years’ imprison-
ment under an imaginary Act of Parliament, which he
invented on the spot, for hiring without his sanction a
man who was registered with him. I couldn’t think of
any Act of Parliament with which to smite him in reply,
but I fell on him with many adjectives and an umbrella
and drove him off the field. The Sydney registry-office
keeper habitually advertises fictitious positions and
charges the score or so of applicants who call about each
one a fee of Is. or 2s. 6d. for registration before giving
any details whatsoever. He can easily find a con-
federate, to whom he can send the dupes, and the other
thief tells them on arrival that he is just suited. The
advt. costs sixpence, and seldom brings in less than 15s.
No special law is required to deal with these callous
ruffians. The statute about obtaining money under false
pretences is quite sufficient—if it were only used.
A letter from Mr. Arthur MacArthur in the
Sydney press on theN.S.W. Royal Agricultural
Society has more than a local application. Mr.
MacArthur points out that the R.A.S. was
established (with a big State grant and subsidy)
to establish relations with similar societies in
other lands, to investigate diseases of stock and
plants, to offer premiums for skilful agriculture
and the manufacture of agricultural implements,
to encourage irrigation, to promote the discovery
and introduction of new cereals, grasses, stock,
&c., to investigate methods of manuring, to pub-
lish herd and stud books, and so on' -That’s the
work it is paid to do. The work it actually does
is to promote annually a Cheap-Jack show—a
combination of uproarious drinking dens, penny
gaffs, shoddy shops, racing and jumping contests,
with some flavor of real agricultural show. The
R. A.S. undoubtedly holdsitsarea of parkland and
draws its huge annual subsidy from the Treasury
on false pretences, and no man should be quicker
to appreciate the fact than Mr. John See,
president of the Society, and Premier of New
South Wales. But though the R.A.S. is a
conspicuous object among the band of sturdy and
worthless beggars who infest the Treasury of
every Australian State in search of a subsidy,
it is by no manner of means the only one.
There are hundreds of others—so-called agricul-
tural societies, choral unions, old-cat local poor
relief societies, schools of the art of reading
Marie Gorelli and Hall Caine. And there
will be no adequate financial reform until the
hose is turned on all of them, and a dripping
and dejected band of daylight robbers is taught
that the day has passed when a Balmain choral
society or a royal agricultural variety entertain-
ment society can cadge from the State Treasury.
♦
Melb. Argus correspondence on the question why
men don’t go to church, or do go to church, drew
at least one calm, worldly epistle from a parson.
Canon Tucker reduced it to a practical question.
Assuming that the result of closing all churches
would be unsatisfactory— i.e. , disastrous from
his point of view—the Canon asks “is it not then
the duty, apart from the pleasure, of every man
who calls himself Christian and loves his country
to deny himself in order to help maintain by his
manly and consistent example the practice on
such high and universal authority founded;?”
When a clergyman comes down from his pulpit
and refers to the practice of church attendance as
a pill all exemplary citizens should swallow, just
to show they have no animosity, he disarms a lot
of criticism. The question as to whether a weekly
dose of Divine Worship may indirectly benefit the
soul by shaking up the liver is one that seems worth
considering ,But it is not usual for a practitioner
in the cure of souls to put his doctrine in this
frankly medicinal form, and admit that the aver-
age citizen achieves an act of self-denial by swal-
lowing it. Worship, hitherto, has been submitted
by the cocksure churches as the only alternative to
Damnation (with a big D), so the ungodly pro-
tested against the Damnation theory by letting
Worship slide. They were always being told
about the comforts of religion, but they saw no
evidence of comfort in the faces of the snufflesome.
Now Canon Tucker cheerfully invites sinners to
drop into the tabernacle, not for repentance, but
in order to assist in averting a possible public
calamity. He suggests that it is advisable to keep
a roof over the parson’s head. Maybe he is right.
Anyway, there is no real danger in going to
church if you don’t join the choir or become
associated with the Sunday-school.

The situation in the Victorian railways becomes
even worse as time goes on. Bent’s latest order
is that the sweated service is to furnish a further
£22,000 to balance matters for this year: this
though on the last Estimates there appeared an
item of £46,000 to recoup the department for
carrying the farmers’ wheat (not seed wheat for
drought sufferers) at less than a payable rate.
On top of this the blundering ex-boomer issued a
notice that “increased rates of pay allowed men
filling higher positions will cease after 31st
March, ’O3. Men called on to fill such positions
must do so at their ordinary rates of pay.” The
railway slaves have submitted to percentage
reductions, to working Six (and sometimes seven)
days a week for five days’ pay, and to the wholly
immoral and unjustifiable Stoppage of increments.
But they protested strongly against this last
injustice, that of being expected to do an engine-
driver’s work for a cleaner’s wage—for that is
what the swindle amounts to. Bent, how-
ever, opened his mouth and bellowed, and

Tenor Lloyd’s reception by Sydney
audiences ought to be a warning to prophecy-
mongers to leave misfortune-telling strictly to the
professionals—to those who do it by palmistry,
cards, stars, and tea-cups, and then take their
fourteen days and ignore the option. Whilst
Lloyd was en route to this Commonwealth local
authorities prophesied, muttering “ He’ll be
150 degrees of frost. True, when he retired from
the contest he knew the rules of the game,
but now he is past his prime. Besides, we have
had a surfeit of singers, and the public won’t
carry another note.” Well, Edward Lloyd, fresh
and vigorous after seven years of holy hush and
contentment on his model farm near Tunbridge
Wells, floated in to find a packed house the first
night, a more tightly packed one the second
(when it rained buckets), and on the third night a
crowd which couldn’t flap its elbows without
injuring its ribs or bursting the walls. Lloyd’s
singing of the “Holy City”—it was written
specially for him—gave his hearers a capital view
of the lifting up of the pearly gates. Everyone felt
his (or her) heart beating out Hosannas, and saw
through the gates angels loafin’ round the Throne.
Basso Peterkin and contralto Emily Foxcroft are
next in order of favor, and the little Canadian,
Jean Newman, looks pretty and dresses well.
Girlish ’cellist May Mukle pleases the public and
captures encores, but the public which wouldn’t
patronise Gerardy to the extent of the price of a
pair of spats need not pretend that it knows any-
thing abo'Ot ’cello playing.
* * * *
At Sydney Royal the tail of Whittington’s
cat continues to be jammed nightly to the amuse-
ment of hordes of sweet young things in book-
muslin evening dresses, who go to the theatre
? repared to laugh at everything and anything.
t is this determination of the kids of all sizes
and both sexes to laugh loudly, because the
advertisement says the show is a feast of fun,
that gives pantomime such wonderful staying
power: if the public brought the same mind to
bear on pantomime that it brings to criticise other
plays, the days of that dreary branch of stage art
would be numbered. But an audience that would
hiss a variety artist for serving up last year’s joke
will yell hilariously at a 1000-year old chestnut in
the ancient Xmas-time spectacle—the spectacle,
by tlie way, which is now unfairly breaking out
at other seasons besides Xmas. The why of this
extraordinary forbearance has never been fully
ascertained.
* * * *
“ Are You a Mason 1 ” at Sydney Palace,
continues to attract large audiences—of husbands
and males liable to become such, who wish to pick
up points as to how to come home at dawn with a
ruined hat in one hand and a perfectly satisfactory
explanation in the other ; also by wives and ladies
who intend to be wives, anxious to learn how to
circumvent the base deceit hereinbefore alluded
to. The farce has won a popularity—due, proba-
bly, more to good acting than to its own merits,
which are not startlingly original—quite unusual
in Sydney.
* * m *
At Sydney Lyceum, Ada Guildford’s trou-
blous career of pleasure is still on view, and every
view increases the wonder that any lady should
take her pleasure so sorrowfully. Her enthusiasm
for having a giddy time lands her in difficulties
from which she can only be extricated by dangling
by a rope from a balloon and taking a trip through
the clouds with her feet trailing among Wragge s
disturbances. The stage heroine, as exemplified
by Anderson’s leading lady, is getting not only
stronger - minded, but stronger - limbed. Ada
hangs manfully on to the balloon till it gets
among the clouds, then down again to sea-level,
a fact which shows the possession of great muscle,
especially as the villain is all the time pounding
on her hands with an umbrella or something. The
scene where a large ship goes down without help-
ing the plot at all is still a most impressive affair,
and its irrelevancy doesn’t matter. Nothing
matters much in melodrama so long as the fire and
balloon and the battle and the lunatic and the blue
giraffe are there.
* * * *
“Charley’s Aunt,”at Sydney Criterion, is
drawing good houses, and the Brazilian lady no
doubt could draw them longer, but the manage-
ment has decided to stage “ Sweet Lavender” on
Saturday. Consequently, Frank Thornton will
be seen in a black shawl, and curls, and a dread-
ful cap for only a few weeks longer, and his
mental anguish at being cast among a drawing-
room full of ladies who may forget themselves and
tell after dinner stories will also cease to be. The
last, or second last, time “ Sweet Lavender ” was
played in Sydney, Lavender, through a misunder-
standing with the management, entered with a
red head and a stern expression which presumably >
won’t be repeated. &

Fitzgeralds’ Circus adds a few new items
to its bill, among which is a bright stair-
case act by La Belle Rose, and novel acrobatic
violin performances by the Walhallas. An
educated giraffe takes a prominent position among
the beast performers now, and Pagel goes in as
usual to lift an astonished and insulted lion in his
arms and show his teeth to that half of the
audience that pays most. The other and cheaper
half of the crowd sees the beast’s tail. £

** * *
Sydney Tivoli has had better audiences
since the* revival of the “ first part ” : the corner
men have so far made the return to old-time
* ‘ minstrelsy ” a very satisfactory event. Edward
Ford, facial comedian, is an attractive new turn,
and Miss Fanny Powers contributes some fresh
imitations of vaudeville artists who sing through
the nose. Perhaps all variety, singers do that;
perhaps it is the fault of Miss Powers’ nose. Last
nights of Hill and Sylvainy are announced, and as
a new attraction for the coming week the Boisett
Co. of pantomimists and comedians is heralded
with much loud advertisement.
m* * *
At Melb. Bijou the giddy, red-haired
matron who impersonates Vanity’s victim in a
hay-loft will flop against George Willoughby’s
bosom for the last time on Friday. Also her aged
husband will abandon his pursuit of Roxy Barton
on the same evening, and the complications which
reduce the audience to the verge of hysteria (see
advt.) will be suspended for the present. “Mis-
takes Will Happen ” is a swifter 3-act frivolity
than it was at first, but three weeks is a long time
to keep Melbourne verging upon the same hysteria,
so Willoughby and Geach are about to change the
bill. Next Saturday (May 2nd) they introduce
“ On and Off,” late of Sydney, and the girl with
the automatic wink has got her eyelid in active
training for that coming event.
** « *
Wirth’s Circus, likewise the menagerie,
will make for a warmer clime after Friday,
leaving the bones of the bucking horse buried
somewhere in the vicinity of the scene of its final
buck. Anew “outlaw” steed has been engaged
for the Brisbane season. The one that strove for
liberty in Melbourne bucked not wisely but too
well at the matinee last Saturday, and died of a
merciful bullet, resulting from a fractured spine.
A typical Australian crowd accepted this exciting
calamity in a philosophical spirit. It was the
concluding item of the programme, and as
the crowd sauntered from the tent several
people remarked in a casual way that the rider
who fell with the horse had all the best of the
luck. He was not hurt and didn’t need to be shot
by a policeman. The Circus is committing sundry
novelties this final week. On Monday night a pan-
tomime constructed for tent purposes was brought
into action—if no unforeseen circumstances
chanced to prevent. And to-night (Thursday)
two Hindu wrestlers will catch-as-catch-can for
a purse provided by the management. One of the
dusky rivals is Butta Singh, a hitherto invincible
Hindu, and the sanguine individual who hopes
to lay Butta low is named Gunga Braha. The
contest is expected to prove a soft thing for
Butta.
The Opera House seems to have found a
strong attraction in Rudinoff, the lightning-
sketcher, whistler, and entertainer at large.
Rudinoff is not startling, but he is versatile, and
the fact that he speaks eight languages seems to
make itself felt, although he merely talks English
with a mixed foreign accent during his long
sojourn on the Rickards stage. Moreover his
legs look lovely in silk stockings, and his shirt frills
set off his smiles to advantage. The sort of man one
associates with a fancy name such as Rudinoff.
G. W. Hunter and Gracie Emmett are leaving the
Opera House this week ; other people _ will be
turned on in place of those vanished tourists.
* 4K * #
At the Royal all is going well with the
G.N. Steeplechase up to time of writing, and the
Prodigal Daughter’s aged father clears off his
mortgage every evening by means of a desperate
investment on the favorite. The grand old sport
appears to be awfully insolvent before the race,
but the unsuspicious Ikeys book his wagers for an
enormous amount, so they are bound to lose what-
ever happens. Ikey is a sorely victimised person
in melodrama, for he never knows that his prin-
cipal customer is betting on the never. In this
case the sin of the unfinancial plunger would find
him out, and bring his grey hairs m sorrow to a
defaulter’s grave, if there was no Walter Baker
ready to meet the emergency. When a very thin
jockey gets prevented from riding the “dead
bird ” he hands his jacket over to Baker, and that
opportune hero wins the race, carrying fully 3st.
overweight. Under these circumstances any
ordinary attempt to tamper with the scales would
be useless, because Baker can draw the right
weight plus a lump of lead.

Robert Courtneidge will make one or two
appearances at Melb. Princess’s in the comedy
“ On ’Change ” if he hasn’t started returning to
England before “Midsummer Night’s Dream ” is
taken off. The present arrangement is to stage
“ On ’Change ” for a night or two previous to the
next Shakspearian production—“ As You Like
It.” Jn tjiat case Courtneidge will pop up again
as the cranky old Scotch professor who makes
wild, visionary speculations in stocks and shares—
a part hp scored in at the Princess’s ten years ago.
“ On ’Change” was originally a Brough-Boucicault
show, Brough playing the professor. The Firm’s
subsequent production of it served to introduce
Courtneidge—the burlesque actor —as a matter-of-
fact comedian with a native Scotch accent.

Six nights revival of “Paul Jones,” at
Her Majesty’s, started last Saturday, and ere the
present issue of the Bulletin has lost its bloom,
the buccaneer in question will quit the bill. This
revival brings a new Insect into play, otherwise
the cast is what it was last December, when
Florence Young made her first pleasing
ance in the name part. On Saturday the
Williamson Opera 00. is to try the “ Toreador ”
for one week, and “My Lady Molly ” follows.
“ Molly ” is the latest production of Sidney J ones,
who made the music for “A Gaiety Girl” and
“ The Geisha,” and it claims to be more of an
opera than any previous work by the same
eminently tuneful Jones.

Edward Lloyd, having been a growing
success in Melbourne three weeks ago, is about to
return there to pick up his local popularity again
before it becomes cold and forgotten. The fame
of Edward Lloyd seemed to be hot enough in
Melbourne when he came to the last concert of
the first series, so the four farewell concerts
should produce audiences worthy of his great
reputation. Saturday, May 2, the sth, the 7th,
and again Saturday, 9th, are the dates fixed.

Also there will be a matinee concert at
Melb. Town Hall next Saturday to introduce
Ernest Toy, the Australian violinist from Europe,
as an artist who deserves to be a prophet in his
own country. Miss Davis, Miss Maud Read, and
Edward Scharf, will support the gifted Ernest,
whose other name is, unfortunately, Toy.

At the Adelaide Royal, “The Messenger
Boy ” has taken liberties with the Pyramids for
nearly a fortnight, and the season is being filled
up with revivals of “San Toy,” and “The
Runaway Girl.” Olive Lenton, being dropped
into the cast, shows herself capable with
both voice and feet. Musgrove’s Opera co.
is to follow. At the Tivoli, Wallace Brown-
low has written and played a kind of modernised
“ Mrs. Caudle ” sketch, in which his wife
made her first appearance on any stage. She
proves to have a pretty talent for light comedy,
while W.B. himself is funny as * higgling
journalist who has to discuss the “Is
Edmund Barton a fit and proper person to guide
the destinies of this great Commonwealth, or
should he get work?” John Coleman has also
developed a wife, who actually did the scarecrow
dance for him when the house clamored for it at
a matinee (Johnnie himself being too ill at the
time to give the exhausting performance); and at
night they both did it together! Inaudi has
passed on to other continents, and the Brownlows
go to Maoriland, where Manager Marcus also
betakes himself. Spry and Monti and the ever-
opular Winton fill the vacant places.

“X.” sends along a reminiscence :
George Willoughby, discoursing of the “ inventive and
resourceful ” actor, who teaches the author how to im-
prove his plays, is presumably speaking of the plays that
have six doors to one sitting-room and are mostly peopled
with lunatic characters. Old Dion Boucicault, a play-
wright addicted to successful impersonations of his own
stage Irishmen, was very scornful on the subject of the
“fat-headed ‘star’ actor,” and his inability to under-
stand the meaning of his part. Author Broadhurst
seemingly consented to let Willoughby alter his farce,
“ The Wrong Mr. Wright,” so the actor gives himself
credit for genius as an editor because the piece was a
success. Perhaps another actor could have made a bigger
success of the farce in the original form. Anyway,
Willoughby’s notion that “an actress who’d begin the
honeymoon by throwing the crockery about could never
hope to enlist the sympathies of her audience,” is an
amazingly funny answer to a question re Sarah Bernhardt.
The tempestuous Sarah has been breaking crockery
all her life. Her path is strewn with wreckage.
Willoughby has heard of the awful stampede of William-
son and Garner and all their employees from Melbourne
Princess’s on the historic occasion when the curtain was
dropped 10 minutes too soon upon Sarah’s death-scene.
The mistake was promptly remedied, of course n,
curtain rose again and Sarah resumed her agonies ww
they had been interrupted. But within those relmin,
10 minutes all persons in authority, or otherwise-lS 1
prompter, stage hands, call-boy, evervbodv-flert S
the wrath to come. The actors, as many of them as had
not already left the theatre, locked themselves in theh
dressing-rooms and pretended to be dead. And wl
the “ woman with a truly feminine soul and a beamif„!
conception of harmony ” (vide Willoughby) was left K
wreak her vengeance on the scenery and roar i l
solitude. Meanwhile, Williamson was thrusting w/
crowns through the trap-door in the roof of a ha, ! ''
cab, exhorting the driver to beat the record. n
Eked out by music, scenic arts and dresses,
Bill Shakspeare’s play proceeds at the Princess’s.
Fair Helena pursues her one desire
O’er hill and dale, through painted bush and brier,
Wringing her hands, bewailing her ill-luck
Till things are altered by the elfin Puck,
And two young men, unconscious of the joke,
Dispute the right to be her fancy bloke,
Whilst Hermia, to whom they’d tendered troth,
Upbraids the other cat for snaring both.
King Oberon, who sways the fairy throng,
And treats his subjects sometimes to a song,
Continues to get cross, as erst he did,
Because Titania’s struck upon a kid
(A Hindu infant semi-naked alien—
A Little Bourke-st. anti-White Australian),
And dooms the Queen, asleep on floral bed,
To love a weaver with a donkey’s head,
From which delusion T’ania duly wakes,
And all is peace. Green newts and spotted snakes,
That dart and crawl beneath the fronded fern,
Are charmed away by Mendelssohn’s Nocturne,
And Shakspeare’s ghost looks down from distant
star
To breathe approval on the orchestra.
But William’s spirit feels a pang of grief
Whene’er the bounds of “farcical relief”
Are overstepped. It must be gall for Willy
To find his scenes of humor going silly.
And yet the Bard —himself an actor once—
Knowing the public to be but a dunce,
Forgives some clap-trap mummeries of Bottom
And says “’Tis biz., ’tis pity ’tis. He’s got ’em.”
The Wild Cat Column.
Some of the financial proposals of the Stnta
Premiers at the Sydney Conference recall the storv of ft!
desperate dipsomaniac who pawned his mother's thru,
payment false-teeth in order to continue his lone- rtr. T
The Premiers find that Bull-Cohen, upon whose 8laUo?r
have hitherto relied for their financial thread
to abolish the slate so far as they are concerned •
still tormented by the old disease, they hit uDonffi.
magnificent idea of inviting the Commonweath
to pawn over again the post-offices and custom*
houses which they have themselves pawned already’
and to hand over to them the proceeds of the
new pawning transaction. Having taken over tw
customs, posts, and military depts. it was necessary tm
the Commonwealth to take over the buildings which ua
been used by the States for carrying on those depts
Also, as those buildings had been, in almost every case
built with borrowed money, it is likewise the Droned
thing that the Commonwealth should, in some way take
over the State debt represented by those premises ’ The
States have so much less assets ; and, even on the ground
of doing the fair thing towards Bull-Cohen, who has lent
the money, the States ought to be relieved also of so much
liability. That is to say, the liability and the assets
should go together. If the Commonwealth undertook
to pay interest on the cost of the works taken over the
case would, for the present at any,rate, be met fairly
well; though later on, when it has had time to look
round, the Commonwealth ought to formally undertake
the repayment of the principal, as well as to keep the
interest going. But the Premiers don’t want the thing
put on any such basis as that; wherefore they ask not
for the Commonwealth to take over the liability along
with the assets, but for a payment in cash or the
equivalent of cash. If they undertook to use the cash
to pay off the money that was borrowed on the
property which they transfer, there would be little or
nothing to say against the proposal. But they mention
no such simple and honest intention ; and if they had the
intention it is reasonable to assume that they would
mention it. Everything, in fact, points the one way-
that, having raised one lot of money on these assets, they
now want to surreptitiously raise a second lot on the
same assets. Which is quite in keeping with John See's
dictum (for political use only) that the prosperity of a
business man is always known by the frequency of his
visits to the pawn-shop.
The matter affects the ordinary citizen this
way. Already as a citizen of the State he is paying
interest on the money borrowed to build the post
office, customs, and other premises. If this remarkable
proposal of the Premiers were carried into effect, it
would then mean that, as a citizen of the Commonwealth,
he would also have to pay interest on a second lot of
money borrowed on the same premises. Out in the West
they tell of a very accomplished cattle-thief who, first of
all, sold a cow to a man, then stole the cow back again,
killed it, and sold the same cow to the same man as beef.
Even out there they thought that was carrying private
enterprise a bit too far, and the man who paid twice over
got a lot of sympathy.
• • • •
The Conference of State Premiers, which re-
cently met in Sydney, decided, among other things, that
the Commonwealth should take over the Stale debts.
On that point, and so far as they went, the State
Premiers were certainly in the right. But when Aus-
tralian Treasurer Turner, who also wants to take over
the State debts, asked the Premiers for a full statement
in order that he might draw up a scheme, he had to
write over and over again, and wait for many weeks
before these uncouth and grumpy individuals would even
reply. And though the State Premiers want their debts
taken over they absolutely refuse to state what they
would agree upon as a fair and reasonable basis of con-
solidation. The great trouble is to draft a scheme which
five out of the six State Premiers won’t repudiate with
scorn. It is a pity, when the six were together, they
didn’t indicate what sort of arrangement would suit all
of them.

* * * *
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of 10|in. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

A. Kozminsky, 106 Queen-sfc., Melbourne,
having returned from England, is now prepared to
purchase paying mines, and mining properties (not
merely surface shows), situated in any part of
Australasia requiring further capital for development and
machinery. Only genuine and legitimate ventures,
capable of bearing the strictest examinations will be
entertained.*

• • * *
Assayers throughout Australia will be interested
to hear that Ernest de Beer of Salisbury Building’s
Melbourne, has taken unto himself a partner—Mr. H. B.
Silberberg. The arrival of the new partner, who brings
7 years Kalgoorlie experience into the business, means
that the firm of De Beer, Silberberg and Co., will be in a
position to stock all sorts of laboratory requisites and
assayers’ sundries. At the present time they can supply
from stock plumbago and other crucibles, plumbago
finely ground, microscopes and scientific instruments,
chemical apparata, including full lines of jena and other
glassware. Royal Berlin porcelains, and so forth. In a
few months there will be nothing that De Beer, Silber-
berg and Co. cannot supply from stock. Test orders are
confidently invited. Special attention is called to
Morgan’s Battersea “ F ” shape plumbago, and salamander
crucibles, also extra special consideration is claimed for
the gasoline furnaces, which are rapidly growing in
popularity. Messrs De Beer, Silberberg and Co., are
direct agents for Schott and |Genossen’s compound, and
the famous “ Durax ” gauge glasses, which, by the way,
have lately been adopted by the Victorian Railways and
the Melbourne Tramway Co.*
• • * *
It is not generally known that in the treatment
of ore as much ol the valuable minerals is often lost in
the tailings as ia saved by the appliances. This is due
in most instances to the inefficient machines used and
the difficulty in saving the fines and slimes. The best
machine to treat the latter classes are Luhrig Vanners.
These machines are in successful daily use in the principal
gold, galena, copper and tin mines in Australia and
Tasmania, proving their superiority over other so-called
slime machines. Messrs W. & J. Lempriere, 506 Little
Collins-street, Melbourne, are the general agents, and full
particulars can be obtained from Diem.*
Scottish Critic, October, 1902, writes re the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society Limited “ The
Company was established in 1873 and the management
has always been characterised by abundant enterprise,
tempered with the prudence which can alone make
energy valuable in a life assurance company.”*
I* • * ,+
The field of concentration after stamps or
amalgamation is an old and recognised one, yet this
treatment is now improved upon in Certain instances by
concentrating direct from the stamps. It is found that
gold heavy enough to sink through the film of water
moving rapidly down the copper plate, is heavy enough
to b« successfully settled upon the deck of the Wilftey
table, and thus concentrated. With certain refractory ores,
a common practice is now to stamp and amalgamate on
plates and to pass the tailings over Wilfley tables,
extracting the troublesome sulphides -and then re-treat-
ing the tailings by pan amalgamation, cyanide or any
of the special lixiviation processes that may be demanded
by the peculiar characteristics of the ore. Illustrated
catalogues with valuable notes on ore dressing can be
obtained from the Sole Manufacturers in Australia of the
Wilfley Concentrating Table, the Austral Otis Company,
South Melbourne.*
• * * *
Associated (W. A.) has been having a thorough
overhaul, on behalf of continental holders, by Pierre
Ledoux, who last visited the property in May, 1900, after
the sensational collapse of the returns. Following his
visit metallurgist Dunstan and his assistant, Cummipg,
have resigned. Ledoux managed to get down the Oroya,
and also managed to see a plan of that mine’s under-
ground workings, on the strength of which he has started
a third bore hole from the 1050 ft. level of the Associated.
This bore is going down more vertically than its prede-
cessors, and, it is hoped, will locate the continuation of
'the rich Brown Hill-Oroya chute. It is probably also as a
result of Ledoux’s visit that the detailed working costs of
the mine are given. These tot up to 61s. lid. a ton, and, as
the ore is worth fully 80s., there is an apparent profit of
nearly £1 a ton. Milling costs as much as 265. 3d., to
which has to be added 2s. lOd. for the treatment of
slimes and tailings. Even then work has been ineffective.
The introduction of the new furnaces should, however,
oelp to reduce these costs.

Sydney Telegraph arrives at the astound-
ing conclusion that the Commonwealth Government
should not take over the State debts unless the States
agree to restrict future borrowings. If they won’t agree
to restrict their future borrowings, the Telegraph reckons
“ the Commonwealth had better wash its hands of the
State debts altogether." As it is tolerably certain that
the States won’t, for the present at least, consent to
restrict their borrowings, this would simply mean aban-
doning all hope of debt consolidation. The Bulletin’s
idea is that the Commonwealth should take over the
Stats debts whether the States agree to restrict their
further borrowings or not, and tax the States (in other
words, its own subjects—the people of Australia) to pay
off these debts. When the old debt— i.e., the debt now
existing—is paid off, the States will probably have built
up a new debt—a very considerable one. Therefore, the
Commonwealth should go on taxing the States to pay off
the new debt by buying up the State securities, and con-
stituting itself the creditor of the States. In other words,
if the States won’t pay their debts, the Commonwealth
should do it by taxing the States. To wait till the States
agree to reform their borrowing habits before the
Commonwealth Government starts to do anything to-
wards setting things straight is to wait till doomsday, or
thereabouts; it is almost certainly to wait till the ex-
travagance of the subordinate State Governments has
made Australia—the Commonwealth’s Australia—or some
parts of it, bankrupt, and to propose that the Common-
wealth Government shall look quietly on while its own
country is made bankrupt is the worst possible folly.
The Commonwealth Government can’t “ wash its hands”
of the matter any more than it can wash off its own
identity.

The Mutual Life’s income thus increased more than in any
other year, with one exception, during the past decade ;
while the expense rate was the lowest in the same
period, also with one exception. Further, the amount
added to the funds is the largest on record.
The directors of the Mutual Life report that
“ reduction of expenses by £1 6s. per cent, is evidence of
economic administration.” They might have been more
explicit on this point with advantage, for they evidently
don’t mean that there has been this reduction compared
with the previous year, as Sydney Daily Telegraph, for
example, has assumed. Nevertheless, the year’s reduc-
tion has been very substantial—at a time, too, when
reductions haven’t been the rule. Comparative figures
show up in this way :
EXPENSE RATE TO TOTAL INCOME.

1895 .. 19 10 2 „ 1900 .. 20 11 2 „
1896 .. 21 311 „ 1901 .. 19 19 3 „
1897 .. 19 19 7 „ 1902 .. 19 9 10 „
So, taking the past decade, the mean expense rate during
the first quinquennial period was £2O Bs. 4d., and during
the second £l9 14s. 3d. Read in conjunction with the
large increase in new business, this reduction of 14s. Id.
per cent, certainly is good evidence of economical
administration. It isn’t the Association’s fault that in
the concluding analysis the results are less satisfactory :

This, it will be seen, is the poorest return in the 10 years.
A comparison of the assets shows that the movement is
due to a considerable increase in municipal debentures
and in cash on deposit and current account (the latter
item has gone from £BB9O to £41,962 in the 12 months),
while loans on mortgages have increased hardly at all.
The directors scent possibly troublous times, and are
prudently lying low for a while. In those circumstances
a reduced rate of interest is rather to be welcomed than
otherwise. In that connection, however, a little more in-
formation might with advantage be given regarding the
public securities—whether they are taken in at par, at
cost, or how.
* * • *
The Victorian Treasurer now reckons that he
will close the financial year with a deficit of £316,000.
This will make ten deficits in 14 consecutive years. Of
the four surpluses, George Turner was responsible for 2£,
his last one being £382,000 ; in fact, taking his whole
term of office, though he started in the worst period of
depression, his revenue was some £250,000 ahead of his
expenditure. But Victoria hasn’t another Turner.

The Federal Building, Land and Investment
Society (Brisbane) looked 12 months ago as though it
were going to pay its way in its next year ; but it has
suffered a relapse in one respect, and is still down with
the old complaint:—
Forfeited
Outgoings. Total Capital Total
Loss on Loss on Loss, carried to Re-
Business. Property. Reserves, serves.
Mar., 1895 ..£2141 £715 £2,856
Mar., 1896 .. 2154 58,482 60,636 £68,272
Mar., 1897 .. 1450 604 2,054
Mar., 1898 .. 1289 1,520 2,809 250
Mar., 1899 .. 1431 867 2,298 366
Mar., 1900 .. 897 540 1,437 456 £14,453
Mar., 1901 .. 649 163 802 1,830 15,481
Mar., 1902 .. 196 665 761 484 15,204
Mar., 1903 .. 256 59 315 62 14,951
The foregoing table isn’t complete, but it is as complete
as the society’s remarkable book-keeping in the past
will allow it to be. The arithmetic nowadays is of the
ordinary variety, and the statement of the position is
reasonably intelligible, as far as it goes, though it ought
to go further. The loss on business has been heavier
than in 1901-2, principally because of the excessive
amount of the interest bill. Excluding “ sundry credi-
tors, £4923,” who presumably aren’t charging interest (or
if they are charging interest they ought not to be bunched
and presented in that way), the society owes to outsiders
(depositors and bank), £38,395. Its interest bill for the year
just ended was £2167; so that it paid £5 12s. lOd. per cent,
per annum. Even including the whole of the “sundry
creditors,” it was a fraction of a penny over 5 percent.
Twelve months ago the directors felt hurt because The
Bulletin said they were paying £5 7s per cent, on
£39,448, and explained that they really owed £44,058,
wherefore the interest was equal to only £4 16s per cent.
Even, however, assuming that the ordinary
sundry creditor charges it interest, the rate would now
be over 4s. per cent, worse than it was 12 months ago. On
the other hand, the net interest and commission earned,
after deducting cost of repairs and rates and insurance
premiums, still fell a trifle short of 3 per cent, on the
nominal value of the assets. To raise money at 5 per
cent, and invest it at under 3 per cent, net isn’t
profitable, and if the concern wants to get on a
fair footing, there still appears to be only one feasible
way of doing it—the shareholder must pay up and wipe
off some of these expensive liabilities. Of course there
is the alternative of waiting for something to turn up—
but it is so seldom that anything turns up.
* * • •
Westralia, which talks a good deal about its
wealth and its surplus, is floating a 4 per cent, loan of
£600,000 for four years. Pretty nearly all the States are
borrowing now for two, three, four, or five years, in the
glad hope that things will be better by then.
Australia, about four years hence, will have the
usual batch of long-dated loans to renew ; also the pre-
sent batch of short-dated loans ; likewise it will presum-
ably want to float the usual batch of new loans. And if,
four years hence, times are as bad as they are now, or
worse than they are now 111

At the commencement of the half-year the shareholders’
funds stood at £481,489 (capital £438,424 and reserves
£43,065) ; and the profits represent £6 14s. 9d. per cent,
per annum on that amount. This compares with recent

Sept., 1900 £5 11 7 p.c. Mar., 1902 £7 6 6 p.c.
Mar., 1901 6 5 0 „ Sept., 1902 616 10 „
Sept., 1901 612 7 „ Mar., 1903 614 9 „
The Colonial has no branches outside Victoria, and has
made no advances against N.S.W. or Q. station proper-
ties, so it has escaped the effects—the direct effects, any-
way—of the drought in those parts. But for that
circumstance it could not present such a satisfactory
front.
Comparing the position six months ago with
the present, the principal items show up in this way :
Sept., 1902. Mar., 1903. Increase.
Reserves ... .. £43,065 £48,327 - £5,262
Decrease.
Profits ... 16,264 16,223 41
Increase.
Note Circulation.. 107,815 122,455 14,640
Cash and Liquid •
Securities .. 477,860 638,584 160,724
Bills in Circulation 40,592 54,577 13,985
Deposits .. .. 2,206,631 2,299,969 93,338
Advances .. .. 2,095,920 2,066,828 29,092 ’
Six months ago a remarkable series of decreases was
noted ; here, on the other hand, is a remarkable series of
increases. For some years the Colonial was busy building
up a doubtful reputation for working on a very slight
cash basis ; but the policy seems to be changing—wisely,
too, in these days when, before long, pretty well anything
may happen in a financial way. The new policy may
mean somewhat smaller profits, but it is a great deal
safer. Six months ago the reserve of cash and liquid
securities (including bills and remittances in transitu)
represented less than 20 per cent, of the bank’s indebted-
ness to the public ; at the end of the half-year the corres-
ponding amounts represented 26 per cent, of the liabilities
to the public. Included in the reserves are now £35,094
worth of Victorian Government stock and municipal de-
bentures against £12,273 held six months previously.
With the recent substantial and steady addition to the
reserves, and with the cautious policy indicated in the
foregoing figures, and without the incubus of a connec-
tion in the droughty parts, the Colonial looks like
growing into a bank of very considerable dimensions.

The business hasn’t been quite as profitable as for the
previous six months; still, in view of the bank’s large
connections in the droughty north of Victoria, the result
is fairly satisfactory, or even rather more than that. No
doubt this is due to the fact that it has been able to do a
profitable business in S. Aus., and that, outside Sydney
and Broken Hill, it has no branches in either N.S.W.
or Queensland. The profits have been equal to £4 7s. 7d.
per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds at the
commencement of the half-year (capital £1,498,220, and
reserves £85,600); this compares with some recent
results as follows:
Mar., 1900 £2 17 0 p.c. Mar., 1902 £4 7 7 p.c.
Sept., 1900 3 9 8 „ Sept., 1902 4 810 „
Mar., 1901 312 8 „ Mar., 1903 4 7 7 „

And the £2 17s. with which the list opens was the best
since 1894 ; so that, though the rate is still low, the pro-
gress during the last four years has been eminently satis-
factory. Yet, in view of the turn which is now shown, ib
is plainer than ever that there should have been no in-
crease in 1901 in the dividend on ordinary shares. The
other main features of the business, along with a com-
parison of the position as ib was 12 months back, are

This rather remarkable run of decreases supports the view
expressed above with regard to the dividend. The cash
and liquid securities have been considerably diminished
during the past term ; nevertheless they still represent
36 per cent, of the liabilities to the public, which is a
larger percentage chan a good many banks carry.
• • * *
At Broken Hill, where the drought hasn’t
broken up yet—where, in fact, the drought never is more
than slightly damaged—a start has been made in a tired
way to construct a supplementary water supply. In the
interests of a syndicate, the strings were tugged, at first
gently and in the end viciously, to have the Yanco site
taken over; but the Umberumberka scheme was finally
adopted. And, on the whole, if the money can be
got to finish it, it will be best. Out there, the
rain falls usually in small patches; and, as the
Yanco catchment and the catchment of the present
reservoir join, the rain-patch that missed one would
almost certainly miss the other. Uinberumberka, on the
other hand, is in another direction ; so that a thunder-
storm will have to dodge hard to miss both. The new
scheme, even if it is completed (and it isn’t safe just now
to “go blind” on the completion of any scheme which
depends on the Owe’Waddell for funds), will not interfere
to any great extent with the B.H. Water Co., the idea
being to let the co. have the job of supplying the mines,
the new concern restricting its operations to the town.
Up to now the mines have provided the co. with three-
fourths of its revenue.

The year’s claims have been unusually light in proportion
to premium income, but, as with many other insurance
institutions, the expenses for the year were high, though
the increase was not so much as in some other cases. The
company’s funds, when it put up the shutters on Dec. 31,
1902, and went home for a well-earned 24 hours’ holiday,
amounted to £78,528,271.

At present the interest earnings of the Mutual
of New York cannot be distinguished from other earnings
apart from premiums. All earnings except premiums
were equal to £4 9s. Bd. per cent, of the average amount
of funds for the year. This figure, which includes profit
on investments as well as interest, is rather seriously
below the corresponding item of last year. The list of
assets seems to be as substantial as usual, though any
further remarks under this head, along with some possi-
ble further remarks regarding the rate of interest earned,
are held over until the laggard balance-sheet, expressed
in English money, comes along.

“Hermit” writes: —“The Golden Victory,
near Canowindra (N.S.W.), opens up well. For 16 years
a local man, Kirkland, hunted for the reef ; he was just
on it when his farm called him home. A couple of Gipps-
land prospectors, Balmer and Davis, then came along, hit
upon the reef, and in a few weeks four men and a bucket
took up 200 tons of ore. Only 170 tons were crushed,
at the Blue Jacket battery ; the yield was 2Joz., with
lldwts. lOgrs. in tailings, besides concentrates. A
former small crushing gave nearly 6oz. to the ton.
Kirkland has now an interest in the show for which he
looked so long, and is manager. A shaft is down 70ft.
The reef is easily worked, and expenses should be low.”
* * * *
“BobR.”: At Junee Reefs (N.S.W.) “another
reef has been struck, bearing good gold.” For 30 years
reefs have been struck in that vicinity ; a few have turned
out well, many have absorbed more gold than was good
for them, and none have proved permanent. Writer’s
opinion is that rich “ pockets” may be discovered, but
none will be worthy of a rush. Probably this field will
for a time mean fair wages for a few fossickers—nothing
more.
• * • •
The light rains which have fallen in western
N.S.W. missed Broken Hill again ; anyway, not enough
fell there to do any good. Only once before has the water
supply been lower ; that was during the famine of 2£
years ago. The outlook is extremely serious. Usually
after a spring in the price of metals Broken Hill hums ;
but there has been no hum this time.
• • • *
Cobar Gold Mines (N.S W.) return for Feb.
was £9290. General, development and capital expenses
totalled £5044, thus showing a clear profit of £4246. The
Jan. return was £9151. March yield was probably well
up to this average, but the current month will show a
considerable falling off, owing to the stoppage of crush-
ing, through an accident to the machinery. Co. ought
soon to be in a position to declare a dividend, which has
been threatened for many years past.
• • • • i
Mount Boppy gold mine, near Cobar (N.S.W.),
in February, crushed 2350 tons for 26090 z. Mine said to
be looking better than ever, improvement in value being
recorded from the deeper workings in all directions. This ;
is quite an exception in New South Wales gold mines. ’
A new manager has arrived on the mine. A co. has been
formed in Cobar to prospect and develop a large area of j
ground to the south of Mount Boppy co.’s holding, j
Prospects so far disclosed are very encouraging. Co. starts \
with a fair credit balance, which is to be applied solely ;
to proving the value of the property, with a view|
to sale on the formation of a larger co. Cobar is to be \
congratulated on this departure from its old-fashioned j
ways. To prove the mine first and provide machinery!
afterwards is a new principle at Cobar.
• • * * I
Extensive alterations are being made to Cobar-j
Chesney concentrating plant to increase its efficiency.
Manager Woolcock is confident that he will show profit-
able results. Should he succeed he will rank as a top-
notcher in his profession. Writer has serious doubts
about any low-grade copper mine, which is minus a com-
plete smelting and refining plant, being made to pay.
Profits’may be made, but they don’t go into the pockets
of mine shareholders—middlemen and smelters absorb
the lot.
• * * *
Occidental (Cobar, N.S.W.) was on the point of
being closed down last week for want of water, when
there was a welcome rainfall. The present supply is
equal to several months’ work, and an occasional div of
3d. may be looked for.

Rumored that experts’ reports on the Grea* ;
Cobar and allied properties confirm all the representation"
that were made, and that the deal is practically com
pleted. The only thing to defer the transfer is the long
pending law-suit instituted by a minority of dissatisfied
shareholders. Whether they are in the right or the
wrong need not be discussed, but it is monstrous that
there should be such intolerable delay in determining the
simple question at issue. The sale of these properties
will be a most important event in the history of N.S.W.i
mining. It will give an impetus to the industry in the)
Cobar district quite beyond local expect#tions. It is
quite safe to say that it will not be long before 10 men!
will be employed where one is now kept at work. Cobar!
will assert itself as one of the leading mining fields of;
Australia. Cannot something be done to stir the slug-f .
gard of Law into reasonable activity when such large
questions are involved ? .
• Crowl Creek (Shuttleton, N.S.W.) shares have
been in steady demand of late, but there are very few on)!
the market. Mine opening up splendidly. The sulphide
lode at 270 ft. has been driven on for about 130 ft, and isL
continuing at both ends, the whole of the stuff as broken ,
assaying between 10 and 16 per cent. It seems assured
now that this lens of ore is as long in the sulphide zone as
it was in the upper workings. A winze has been started
below this level, which is down 30ft. in massive
sulphide, reputed to be of " splendid quality.” This
winze is to be continued to 100 ft., when levels will be
driven with a view to determine the dip and course of the
lode in settled country. When these points have been
decided a main shaft will be started in proper position to
command the lode to a considarable depth. 00. is push-
ing ahead with preparatory work for the erection of
smelters, and has been fortunate in discovering a big de-
posit of first-class fire-clay within about six miles of the
mine. There is now sufficient ore broken and proved, to
keep the proposed plant running for some years. It
may suit the owners of the mine not to popularise it at
present, but should they have any desire in that direc-
tion, it would be well for them to publish a plain unvar-
nished statement of the financial position, and explain
how co. stands in relation to the original vendors.
* * * *
A syndicate has been formed in Sydney to take
over a 12 months’ working option on three leases
immediately adjoining the Crowl Creek mine
to the south. The promoters had no difficulty
in floating it privately in the course of a few days. The
syndicate will be known as the Shuttleton Mines. The
promoters rely not only on position—which is excellent as
the Crowl Creek deep workings are within 450 ft. °f the
boundary— but also prospects revealed in two of the
blocks. More than half the shares were applied
for by men identified with the field. Some of the
local men are sanguine enough to believe that
long before the term of option expires the syndicate will
have accumulated sufficient money from the sale of ore
to form the larger company provided for in the agree-
ment with the leaseholders. The latter have taken up
a large number of the syndicate shares, and are content
to accept a paid-up interest in a company which may be
formed as the sole consideration for the transfer of the
leases—which looks like confidence.

Speaking at a Rechabite banquet last week
two members of the Vic. Assembly referred in
feeling terms to the awful insobriety which
appertains to the Bar of the House —not the Bar
where newspaper publishers are called to
apologise, but the Bar within the meaning of the
Licensing Act. Anstey, M.L.A., referred to it as
an accursed thing, established for the ruin of men.
Brother Hickford, M.L.A., said that “ members
who had entered Parliament as total abstainers
came out total wrecks.” The case seems to call
for a new temperance hymn :
AN APPRECIABLE
DIFFERENCE.
FIRST HEBE : "Yes! Mr.
Montmorency Melvarlios
is the only gentleman in
the company who is not
married."
SECOND HEBE: “I don't know so much about that.
At all events he is the only one who is not
divorced."
A dentist: “ Procrastination is the thief
of teeth.”
Cootamundra, on the Sydney-Melbourne
main line, is having hard luck. It was drowned
out the other day to begin with, and it had hardly
got itself baled out when a tornado, accompanied
by a “ fire-ball ” the size of George Reid’s stomach,
tore along the main street and wrecked two hotels
—one of them so badly that a boarder found 60
bricks falling on him while he was in bed. And
the incident has suggested a nice point in insur-
ance law. If the wind wrecked the building no
claims are valid ; if the fire-ball was responsible
the insurance oo.s must pay.
* * * *
The police haven’t been able to get any-
body punished for robbing the White Cliffs
(N.S.W.) mail of two or three thousand pounds
worth of opal 18 months ago. They laid low for
months till the opal, or what was alleged to be
the opal, got into circulation ; then they made
three arrests—one of the man who, they said,
“ put up the job”; the second of the man who, it
was alleged, had got rid of the stuff; the. third of
the alleged, go-between. The case against the
third was withdrawn in the expectation that he
would give the show away; the second was
acquitted, off hand ; and the other one —the one
whom the police presented in the first place as the
principal in the business —has just been discharged
after a long run. So the mystery isn’t at an end,
and the Far West, which hasn’t too much to talk
about, is still mercifully left one subject besides
the drought.
• * » •
There was a novel demonstration lately at
Kookynie, outside Kalgoorlie (Westralia). A de-
termined strike against the prevailing price of beer
has been declared there; and, in order to prove
that they hadn't been blacklegging and still
meant business, between 30 and 40 men “ pro-
ceshed,” each with a new Savings Bank pass-book
held in front of him. But the worm gnaws, and
the money is coming out of the Savings Bank
pretty soon to start a co-operative beerery.
« • * *
A curious case occurred at a city police
station, t’other day, when a woman carrying a
baby gave herself into custody and asked to be
reprimanded or otherwise severely dealt with for
being intoxicated. She seems to have wakened
up in the middle of the night, noticed her dis-
graceful condition, and betaken lierself with great
presence of mind to the protection of the con-
stabulary. A staggered magistrate with no sense
of humor dismissed the case. A man who was
equal to the occasion would have told the woman
who gave herself into custody to go home and
whack herself hard, or confine herself in the house
for seven days, or fine herself ss. and reprove
herself in strong terms for her misconduct.

* • * •
A spirit of humor seems growing among
newly-made widows, judging by the obituary
notices they insert. There were three “In
Memoriam ” notices of dead husbands the other
day in the one paper. The first had a nice little
poem, the opening line of which was—
Call not the dear departed back.
Later remarks proved that the lady’s motives for
desiring no immediate resurrection of hubby were
purely altruistic. Still it was open to misinter-
pretation. Lower down, the bereaved Mrs.
Brown gave expression to her resignation on the
decease of Brown —
Thy will be done.
The familiar “The Lord gave and the Lord hath
taken away,” with a consequent benediction for
the Lord, supplied the third instance.

in the theatre to-night, my dear. I couldn't
keep my eyes off her.”
WIFE: “ Indeed! How kind of you to tell me.”
HUSBAND: “ Keep calm, my love, keep calm—it
was yourself.”

Dear Bulletin. —Be that
Melbourne lady who suddenly
lost her memory and all sense
of her identity, and was much
distressed thereat—how on earth
did she know she had any
identity? It is reported that
she walked up and down, and
in and out, and roundabout,
saying excitedly, “ Who am I ?”
How did she know she was any-
body ? This loss of identity
business puzzles me. I have read various yarns
about some man or other who forgot who he was,
yet he seemed to invariably remember that he
was some person or other.—L.W.

“ Petramos ” : I manage to make a holiday
trip to Smelbourne every year and get hold of the
latest in slang. Last year “boshter” (as in “a
real bosh ter” —a real beauty, a dazzler) was the
prevailing expression. This year “ bonzali ” holds
sway. It has precisely the same meaning.
Imagine a future slang dictionary—“ Boshter
[obsolete), a bonzali.”

“Petramos” :
Judging by the extraor-
dinary punishments in-
vented by the profesh.,
the life of the irritable
type of schoolmaster must
be durance vile. Was ac-
quainted with a country school
teacher with a habit of “slap-
ping the tongues” of offending
youngsters of the girl persuasion.
The slapping consisted of making
the culprit stick out her tongue to
its full extent, whilst the chastiser
struck it with the flat side of a ruler.
That master was eventually dis-
missed for fixing a rope round a boy’s neck and
making sundry other preparations for a hanging
ceremony.
* * • •
“Leno” (whose letter had 4d. to pay on
it) writes of a Westralian diversion :
I put in a few days among the “ Jarrah-jerkers,” alias
the timber hewers and sawyers and other savages in the
jarrah forests of Westralia, and was invited to a concert
and ball. It was held in the local schoolroom, which was
built of jarrah, and the audience sat round on jarrah
planks roughly nailed to jarrah blocks; the performers
sang from a jarrah stage (about 6ft. square) in a
wooden style. The admission was 10s. a double ticket.
The ball, however, was the great attraction. Some score
of wives and half as many maidens represented the fair
sex. The wives had mostly brought their babies, and,
whilst dancing, deposited them on the schoolmaster’s bed
in his one room adjoining. I counted eight babies there
whilst one dance was going on. The schoolroom had a
dancing space of about 20ft. x 20ft., and in this 30 to 40
couples surged wildly to and fro, the noise of over 70
blucher-clad feet almost drowning the solitary fiddle. A
pleasant diversion was caused by one of the dancers, who
had got “elevated" too soon and had been packed off
home to bed, turning up on the scene clad only in his
shirt.

flows downhill.

An incident of the Vic. Easter camp.
The reveille had sounded, and the Mounted Rifles
were late. One officer, with anguished bawhngs
and a liberal application of spur, was trying to
get some headway on, but for some reason or
other his horse refused to go forward. After
some minutes of tinted language and stained-glass
phrases, another officer happened along, and
casually informed the lunatic on the horse that
his orderly had omitted to undo the heel-ropes.
It was a much-subdued officer who sneaked into
his place in the lines a few minutes later.
The private bar of Parliament 1
Its uses are accurst;
It tempts the legislative gent
To cultivate a thirst;
It lures him from his task below,
His senses it ensnares,
It lays high aspirations low,
That little bar upstairs.
Alas! Alas 1
That little bar upstairs.
A sober new-chum M.L.A.
Is greeted with a wink ;
Old sheep who long have gone astray
Induce the lamb to drink,
And he, at first as chaste as ice,
Pours whisky down his neck,
Until at last, poor slave of vice !
He dies a soddened wreck.
Alas ! Alas 1
He dies a soddened wreck.
Remove the evil, cut it out—
This canker in the State,
Compel the fevered lips to shout
“A glass of water, straight! ”
Shut up the bar and peace serene
Shall fill the House at night
When ev’ry wreck that might have been
Becomes a Rechabite.
Oh, joy 1 O, joy !
Becomes a Rechabite.
FATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O Thou, who knowest whence we came, and can
Endow a moment with the mood of Man,
When my wan moment like a dream is gone,
Destroy or take me then where I began.
If it be in that moment I have err’d
A thousand times, remember I’m a word
That Thou hast spoken, and its echoes have
All from Thine own intensity occurr’d.
I am no other than what Thou hast made,
Apprenticed to Thy purpose, like a trade,
I now not why ; and if I care or no,
’Tis to Thy purpose, too, how I am paid 1
R. CRAWFORD.

••- * •
Ail instructive maintenance case cropped
up at a Melbourne suburban court last week.
The fair complainant stated she was already
twice a widow, when she had the misfortune to
be beguiled by the advertisement of a widower,
aged 60. He wanted to meet an elderly lady of
means, so she met him and compared him favor-
ably, in her memory, with her two dead husbands,
whilst he vowed he loved her better than lie had
loved either of his three deceased wives, though
he had adored them all. So they got married as
soon as possible, and the bridegroom borrowed
37s 6d from the bride to give to the officiating
priest. Then he took her home to his store,
which was so dirty that she had to spend £3 15s
in getting it cleaned Also, she had to clean her
husband free of cost, because he was dirty, she
says. Presently the old man grew mad over her
hearth ss refusal to give him all her money. He
had married her solely for her little bib of
property (as he told the Bench last week), and
when she denied him his cash dues he told her he
had thrown himself away upon the wrong widow.
There was another widow who wanted him badly.
The old man’s alleged prospects of future great-
ness (he claims to be heir presumptive to the
Earl of Allendale) seem to have dazzled the
other suitress he spoke of, but anyhow it is quite
clear that on his bare merits as a somewhat grimy
citizen of 65 he could find lots of widows willing
to come along and lawfully wash him.
* • • •
In spite of the patronage of certain highly-
placed ladies, the Maoriland movement to co-
operate with the “League of Loyal Women” in
South Africa, in putting vegetables over the dust
of the Maorilanders who died while burning the
Boer farms, is a sorry failure. Beyond a few dozy
speeches, principally by mayors, on the achieve-
ments of the youths who died for their country
“in that far-off land ” no interest whatever
appears to be manifested in the movement. No-
body cares now for the foolish stalwarts who
perished in the cause of colored labor.
MILITARY ITEM.
“Good gracious , what are you doing? That?s not
playing at soldiers.”
“ Oh, but we're pretending we’re the Grenadeir
Guards !”
A Royal Australian Artilleryman, who left
a wife and nine children, was killed in the scrub
beyond Randwick, last week, by the upsetting of
a dray. A R.A.A. man with a wife and nine
children on soldier’s pay ! Which recalls an inter-
view which a N.S.W. Commandant had some
years ago with a full private in the R.A.A. The
man had a wife and three or four children, all
being “on the strength of the regiment.” The
Commandant was retrenching, and the full pri-
vate’s name and family and the amount drawn
monthly —about £12 —came in front of him. The
general sent for the private, and having eyed him
over, bluntly said: “Blanlcy, blanky, I can get
three good men for what you and your family
cost. Take a month’s pay and, damn ye, get
out! ”
* * • *
Why are Judges so addicted to “emotion”?
A few days ago Justice Williams (Vic.) threw a
chaffing remark about a “Jew’s harp” at ex-
Attorney-General Isaacs. Isaacs took no notice,
but next day Williams explained, in a broken
voice, that he hadn’t meant the joke as any reflec-
tion on Isaacs’ religion. Nobody supposed he
had, but who, except a judge or an ex-actor,
would feel called upon to have a break in his
voice over a small thing like that ?

HEAVYWELIGHT WIDOW: “ Phwat —would yez have

anither chop?’”
The sinful foolishness of the White Aus-
tralia doctrine has been explained to the complete
satisfaction of Wodonga (Vic.) Shire Council by
Rev. Bickford. The gentle shepherd in question
appears to have been exploring North Queensland
on behalf of the Victorian and Tasmanian Metho-
dists, who love the far-off nigger either as a
brother in the Loard when he is converted, or as
a brother in urgent need of conversion to the
Loard. Starting off with the conviction that
niggers are good things to have in the Common-
wealth for missionary purposes, Rev. Bickford
soon convinced himself of the utter “impossi-
bility” of substituting white labor for colored
labor in the sugar-caneries. It can’t be done—in
a Rev. Bickford way of speaking. The white
man contracts tick disease. The cane cuts his
hands and damages his body. It would cut
his head off if he were to use it as a saw for that
suicidal purpose. Moreover the white man can’t
live within the tropic of Capricorn. He does
actually live and work there, but only because
he’s obstinate. If he were a respectable Metho-
dist and an anti-White Australian he wouldn’t do
it. Although it was the middle of winter when
Rev. Bickford and his Christian friends visited
Townsville, “ none of them could sleep in the
town, and had to repair to a mountain several
miles away to get rest.” Which proves conclu-
sively that the warmest parts of Australia were
never made for unctuous tourists. The oil which
exudes from the extra good man when he is hot
unfits him for climates where the man of muscle
rather than grease can flourish.
• * • •
“ C.A.R.0.” on a matter of modesty :
This is purely a yelp of disgust, it is meant for nothing
more, and it is at the expense of the women bathers who
frequent the morning breakers at Manly. Many of the
bathers dispense with anything over their gowns, both
to and from their bath ; and the sight of dripping figures
in shrunken skin-tight suits, making their way home-
wards, is too common to shake the nerves. Have seen
girls and fellows talking together at the top of some of
the streets with about as much clothing between them
as would keep a decent-sized dog shivering. It is need-
less to say the girls had good figures. One morning a
woman had the bliss of creating a little sensation—she
sauntered down to the water in a white muslin gown ! It
was very pretty, but as soon as it was wet! From a
distance that gown did the disappearing trick, and men
swam tirelessly past her. She was eventually remon-
strated with by a few Philistines, and left the beach in re-
luctant disgust. One lady became well known by divest-
ing herself of her wet and tight garments with the summer
house for background, and her dressing gown, into which
she somehow manoeuvred, held between her and the
men’s bathing ground. Side views were permitted. One
morning some girls removed her clothes to t’other side of
the shed, but she trotted them back again, remarking
she “ liked the sun."
* « * *
Parson C. E. Curtis, a right-thinking
preacher, got up in his pulpit, t’other Sunday
night, and gave the inhabitants of the unclean
town of Tamworth a piece of his mind re the pre-
sent typhoid outbreak in the town —and Tamworth
certainly wants a large, violent piece of some-
body’s mind. Its alleged river is a great open
cesspit nowadays, banked on either side by evil-
looking refuse drained and deposited from the
town. The time when Tamworth smells like the
bottomless pit in its worst state is when the
streets are being watered with the fluid from this
melancholy stream. The town has been incorpo-
rated for 27 years, and the local alderman’s idea
of drainage still consists mainly of “ chucking ”
things into the river. The slow-going Aus-
tralian river doesn’t lend itself to that kind of
treatment. It doesn’t hurry away with the garb-
age, but deposits it gently on its sandbanks and
its mudflats and its bars, and leaves it there in the
sun, and comes back again in a few days to see
how it is getting on. Tamworth, as the result of
this sore of thing, has got typhoid badly. It is
difficult to see what else it could expect to get.
• » # *
It was with reason Justice Walker, of
N.S.W., spoke resentfully the other day about
a common legal practice. A divorce cb-re-
spondent declining to pay costs may be
gaoled for six months, but if at the end of
that term he sequestrates his estate he may
be released. On April 20 such a co-respondent
was released by the sheriff; on April 22 a belated
application for his release turned up before Justice
Walker, who did well to be cantankerous at having
his time wasted and his horsehair wig turned into
a mockery by his being asked to order that the
thing which had already occurred should happen.
The “ barmaid ” question seems to be
almost as awkward a rock to run up against as the
Sunday banana. There’s a large and holy section
of the community which professes an abhorrence
for both, and by persistent effort scares up an
occasional agitation against them as sinful. But
the ultimate result is always the same —barmaid
and banana emerge in their old position of proud
security. Magistrate Isaacs from the Sydney
Bench the other day remarked, or was reported to
have remarked —
Barmaids are a curse to our social life. lam sure a girl
cannot engage in such a profession and come out of it
with a clean mind and clean hands.
Which libel had been in print but one day when
Magistrate Isaacs thought fit to withdraw and
apologise, with the explanation that what he
really did say, or meant to say, was—
The employment of young and inexperienced girls as
barmaids, such as the accused appears to be, is a curse to
our social life. It would be a hard matter, in my opinion,
for such to pass through such an ordeal with clean hands
and pure mind.
Which is a little less absolute, and brings Mr.
Isaacs nearer to the more moderate of the holy
section, who would allow any lady over 70 to
become a barmaid. Yet it is wholly illogical, for
if there are to be barmaids, there must be a time
when they are “inexperienced,” if not young.
The only way to become an experienced barmaid
is by being a barmaid. But it was the only hole
in the fence, and Magistrate Isaacs gob through
it with all possible grace and velocity.

The habit that Anglican Bishops in Aus-
tralia have of capturing as an alias the name of
their see (on the absurd pretence that they’re
lords over the territories in which they bishop
at small salaries) works out humorously some-
times. Charlie Bathurst and Willie Melbourne
look right enough, but a recent begging letter to
the Sydney press was signed
GEORGE H. NORTH QUEENSLAND.
Later on, one may expect “Thomas D. Gulf of
Carpentaria,” or “ James G. British New
Guinea.” And what’s the matter with “John K.
Cannibal Islands ” ?
