“ H.P.” re “no encum-
brance ”: —
The employers of labor are not alone
in barring children. Landlords in tak-
ing tenants generally give the pre-
ference to those fortunate (?) enough
to have no olive branches. I wanted
a house the other day. The landlord
had other applicants. I knew this,
and approached him cautiously. His
first question was : “ Have you
any children ?” "Yes.” “Ah ! how many?" *'Four."
“ Hum !—what are their ages?’’ "Ten, eight, four, and
one.” “Oh! What sort of children are they ?” “The
best in the world.” “Yes; well, I’ll 6ee you again.
Good day.” He did not see me again. Next day I
passed the house I was after. A newly-married couple
was moving in. They got the house for Is. a week less
than 1 was asked. One consolation is, they will have to
pay more later on—probably.
• • • •
The female stowaway gets a much better
time than the male ditto. That handsome woman
of about 35, who was found on the Orontes at
Adelaide, was comfortably lodged and boarded at
the cost of the Orient Co. and returned by their
next finer to Melbourne. This sort of thing is all
very good while the woman stowaway is new. But
she may cease to be new, and then things may be
different—very different. The man who finds
her very ill and exhausted among the cargo will
simply take her by the waistband and carry her
up, like a collapsed carpet-bag, for presentation
to the captain, and he will speak severely to her,
finally telling her that she may lie insensible in the
till she begins to feel better, and then
the steward will give her some salt horse and let
her work her passage to the next port, where she
will be given in custody with the utmost rigor of
the law.
* * * *
When the big double murder was com-
mitted in the Mackay lock-up yard, a few weeks
ago, by a Kanaka, two white men were in the
lock up under committal on a charge of cattle
stealing. Against these two men the evidence
was not particularly strong, yet they were both
kept in close confinement (which was probably
fortunate for them, under the circs.). The Kanaka,
on the other hand, who was under committal upon
a capital charge, and against whom the evidence
was of the strongest (lie had confessed to the
crime), was allowed to remain in the yard
at his own sweet will and tomahawk his fellow,
prisoners in a wholesale fashion. The lie abcut
the gentleness of the Kanaka was, in this case,
responsible for two deaths.
Somebody have been making nasty
remarks about the warlike spirit of Ikey Mo.
lately. The Jewish Hekald resents these base
insinuations, and proudly cites some striking
instances calculated to knock them into ole clo’ !
“ There is,” it sajrs, “ a good deal of Jewish fight-
ing material running to seed among certain of the
less known of the Hebraic clans in the remote
districts of North-western Africa. Few, probably,
are cognisant of the fact that a large number of
the so-called Riff Pirates, the last and worst, per-
haps, of the old corsair-men ofthe Barbary Coast,
are really Jews, in whom the old fighting lust
of the Hebrews has taken this unusual mode
of manifesting itself.” These are the gentle-
men who have been making things so un-
pleasant for the Spanish garrison in Melilla
lately—by way of wiping out the old Jewish score
against Spain, perhaps. They are picturesque
ruffians, but sadly in need of education. Another
interesting item from the hook-nosed contem-
porary: “Curious is it to note that one of the
first commodores of the newly-constituted Ameri-
can navy after the finish of the struggle with
Great Britain was a Jew, Mordecai Manuel Noah.
He was probably the only commander of a war
vessel the Jewish race ever produced.” The
position was, most likely, given him on the
strength of his last name. The other Noah was a
good deep-sea sailor, and the oldest of admirals.
* * * •
The female housebreaker of Melbourne is
apparently going to be a permanent institution;
lately two more of her were captured in one night.
One of them showed great energy and fought
several rounds with two ladies who attempted to
detain her. In the end she not only got away,
but took the plunder with her ; but she was pre-
sently overtaken by a policeman. This new form
of competition is a serious matter for the male
housebreaker, for the amount of valuable, un-
guarded, portable property is always limited, and
the more people that are after it the less each of
these people is likely to get.

“ Do ye sweat much, Lady Clarke? ”

The British aristocracy furnishes a very
steady supply of utterly squalid scandals. But
even it will nob easily beat the record of the
divorced Countess Russell, who married a coach-
man named Brown under the impression that that
plebeian impostor was a son of the Emperor of
Austria. Small romantic nurse-girls and such-like
do fall into traps of the sort, but the ex countess
would be naturally supposed to know the ear-
marks of emperors and other small deer better
than that. Brown, the coachman, doesn’t seem
even to have builb up a plausible tale by assuming
the style of a real prince whose dignity could hear
looking up in a book of reference. He called him-
self Jobson, Prince of Greece, or the Jook
d’Moses, or something about equally uncon-
vincing.

A section of the N.S.W. licensing law
authorises a fine of £2O on any publican who
doesn’t produce his license when called upon by
the police or Other proper authority. And, as a
rule, he can’t it, because the brewer, who holds
a mortgage ovifer his body, pub., and soul, has the
document. Therefore (as a remedy for the “ tied-
house ”)'the brewer might be fined £2O every time
the license isn’t forthcoming.

Same as Kings and Gov’norsget ;
Tompkins in the fashion quite is,
Envied by his social set.
Tompkins’ state of exaltation
Poet’s fancy cannot paint;
Only men of rank and station
Suffer from this New Complaint.
Tompkins has appendicitis;
O, what joy to hear the pain t
Uncontrolled his wife’s delight is—
Tompkins has not lived in vain!
Tenderly his hand she seizes—
“ When you’re stronger, dear old pet,
You must catch some more diseases,
Same as Kings and Gov’nors get.”

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 28, 1903.
My Dear Arini.—
Have you ever detected and arrested the funny
little fancies that scurry through your sensorium
whilst you are manicuring your nails or doing
your hair ? At such occupations you may become
intensely absorbed with a score of idle fancies
which, registered, would make interesting reading
—perhaps!
A week ago, whilst I was getting my claws into
good mousing condition, I caught myself thinking
how nice it would be to live away out from the
noise and dust of this, the noisiest city in the
world (except Glasgow, I am told), on a farm with
fine shade trees and fish-ponds; a farm where the
calves never grow to be cows and the goslings
never turn into great flat-footed geese, but always
retain their golden down and sweet, squeaky
notes. To feel a calf’s horns budding is an intel-
lectual intoxication, I meditated (and the calf does
love to have them scratched with a clothes peg—
the peggy end).
Then there floated before my introspective eyes
a Tasmanian memory of a group of giant
tamarisks. Through their foliage, light against
the opal sky as asparagus fern, shone a half-faced
moon; yet it Was not night. Between their
stems a blue panel of sea. Without a ghost of
a connecting link I was next thinking of the
horde of flying foxes in Federal Government
House garden on the night of the Tennysons’
ball. Drought has driven them to the coast in
great numbers this year. They rustled to
and fro in the foliage, quarrelled, and tried
to spoil the waltz music with their unhappy
noises. From that to the women at the ball —a
section of the women—the Woman of 50. She
has been, this season, prominent at the race-
meetings, the balls, the civic reception at the
Town Hall, at the afternoon teas and first nights.
She was, collectively, beautifully dressed, her
decollette gown showing her fine polished
shoulders, exquisitely corseted and coiffured, and
she kept her eyes swivelling and her lips smiling,
and she talked, talked, always—to the man at
her side. Rarely was the man middle-aged, or
old. And he gave no sign of wishing to wander
to others 30 years younger.
A recent mag. article on the fifty-year-olds is
illustrated with portraits of Lady R. Churchill,
Mrs. Burton Harrison, Adelina Patti, and Bern-
hardt (these last two are about 60), Brown-Potter,
and Langtry, as representing the brilliant women
of 50 or more who are at the very zenith of their
powers, with “ripe beauty,” “ripe views,” “dis-
ciplined intellects,” and who stand for a great
deal jn their particular worlds. Contrasted with
these; portraits are others showing women of 50
half-a-century ago, a maiden aunt of former days,
and a girl of 16, 50 years ago. They look a sad
group. No doubt they spent their lives trying
to please this or that man, and the writer says
“there are few objects to whom a man behaves
so indifferently as the woman who breaks her
neck trying to please him.” The writer argues
that modern women are 20 or t5O years
younger than their grandmothers of the same
age because they have decided not to grow old,
it being a matter more psychological than physical
till a certain bourne is reached, beyond which is
collapse. Portraits of the smart 50-year-olds,
who make the very debutantes jealous in our
Commonwealth society, would form a fetching
gallery.
L.-Mayor T. Hughes has “struck” on indis-
criminate mayoral receptions of visitors. One of
the aldermen desired to welcome pianist Mark
Hambourg as a distinguished visitor, and was
quite ruffled When the Mayor declined, and referred
to “ his mistakes of the past” in this direction.
He has everyone’s approval for striking against
the use of the mayoral reception as a means of
advertisement, but everyone regrets that he has
struck at a man of music instead of a man of 100
horse-power legs. Sandow and cyclist Taylor
were received as distinguished visitors—there is
almost something Cardinal Moranish in the blun-
der of shutting down on Hambourg. Why didn’t
Mr. Hughes have the sense to wait for the next
muscle man before taking up a firm stand ?
Mr. and Mrs. B. R. Wise returned to Sydney
last Friday, and left next day for their house at
Port Hacking, accompanied by Mrs. Fitzwilliam-
Curtis (Miss Fattorini). Mrs. Wise isn’t changed
by a day or a pound (avoirdupois), but she is
weary of the blown wet face of the sea, and feels
salted like a herring She spent much of her
time with her son at Harrow, where she also
skated. It was 20 years since she had on a pair
of skates. It was a beautiful winter in England,
clear and bracing. What a new thing to hear an
English winter get a good character! At
Marseilles, Mrs. Wise met Madame Blanc,
the wife of a former Sydney manager of the
Messageries Maritimes, and found her quite
up to date regarding Australian affairs through
reading our papers. Asked about her stay at
Madresfield Court with the Beauchamps, Mrs.
Wise had something to say as to the charm and
attainments of the bride, and much to marvel at
in Lord B.’s continued interest in Australia—in
its painters, writers and singers. He also asked
after scores of Sydneysiders by name.
Mrs. Wise returns a member of the Ladies’
Empire Club (the Forrest dame is also a member),
but she didn’t club heavily, and knows nothing
of the uniform which the body is said to affect.
Someone, who was possibly giving me toasted
cheese in a trap, described an Empire Club uni-
form of paralysing brilliancy. After getting the
salt out of her system, and the albatrosses and
porpoises out of her dreams, Mrs. Wise will come
back to town and start house-hunting.
Mr. S. W. Childs, an American construction
engineer with something of the fortunes of the
Republic in his personality, who has fixed up the
electric tramways in Kalgoorlie (W.A.), Was a
few days in Sydney before leaving for a run
through M.L. His destination is China. The
Kalgoorloons parted with him most unwillingly,
but nope to pay him a call in a body and borrow
tram-fares when he’s wearing the yellow jacket
and peacock’s feather in the Forbidden City.
Those who heard tenor Lloyd in his prime say
to those who hear him to-day, “Ah ! you should
have heard him before he retired, ’’ etc. Had he
come out in the summer of his voice, instead of its
autumn, he could not have got a more spon-
taneous appreciation. His singing of “ Come
into the Garden, Maud ! ” last Saturday night
made the audience look and behave in such fashion
as might cause a Master in Lunacy to take action.
Lloyd, junior, who accompanies his father, is a
blond, bland young man, who owns up to being a
farmer. He looks more like an aide-de-camp, and
you can’t imagine him hoeing turnips, except in a
comic opera furrow.
“The King dined with Captain and Lady
Sarah Wilson at their house in Grosvenor-square
on Monday evening.” Think well on that, look
at it in a proper spirit, and the world will recede
and disappear, leaving the announcement blazing
across the universe, just as Heine saw the name of
his favorite girl. What may happen to the son
of a good Riverina sheep-owner when he marries
a duke’s daughter !
At the recent Investiture. Mrs. Fitzrudder-
Jones in a surprised tone to a gentleman wearing
an order: “Is that you, Mr. R. ? ” “ Certainly ! ”
was the reply. “Well, lam glad to see you, Mr.
R.; will you please send me another dozen of
that nice hock you sent me before ? ” “ De-
lighted,” said the gentleman ; “do you mean that
at Is. 9d. per bottle ? ”
A muster of maimed, halt and convalescent
infants, ranging in age from three to six years,
from the Children’s Hospital, with nurses in
charge, filled the front chairs of the stalls at a
matinee of “Dick Whittington” last Saturday.
They were Mrs. J. C. Williamson’s guests.
Dick’s “ Cat,” the doll’s apron worn by the big
Martha Mixit (the man-cook) and the Buttercup
and Daisy ballet engrossed them to forgetfulness
of their plaster-of-Paris-encased limbs, and even
of their hostess’s milk chocolate. A granny of
four-and-a-half had so strong a sense of decorum
that she protested loudly (to the orchestra)
against the ballet “ showing its ‘ panties ’ when it
went ring-a-ring-a-rosy.”
Marie Narelle’s success in Dublin and London
will be an encouragement to the tribe of Aus-
tralian singers whose eyes are turned Smokeward.
Marie says she got an offer of £25 per week from
the Empire, but is doing so well at concert work
that it was declined. Her Irish ballads have
made Miss Narelle’s chief success.
A compliment to Longstaff is the proposed
issue of the portrait of Marjory Williamson as a
Xmas supplement to the Graphic. By the way,
Longstaff will now probably get on with his
portrait of the Queen, ordered for National
Gallery by some Sydney women. Last week
L.-Mayoress Mrs. T. Hughes presided at the final
meeting of the portrait fund, and cabled £302
some odd shillings and pennies to the painter;
so the fund was a trifle over-subscribed.
On May 7th a concert, organised by Miss Mary
Robinson, will be given in the Victoria Hall,
Manly. Among the voices to be lifted up in song,
and the other sounds, are those of Maud Dal-
rymple, Beatrice English, Albert Grau, and W.
A. Robyns.
The landscape photograph which took the
world’s amateur championship at the Royal Photo-
graphic Salon two years ago has only recently
got its medal and ribbon. In the interim it has
won over £lOO in prize money at English and
American exhibitions. Picture is in rich red
brown tones. It was photographed close to
Jamberoo township, Trees, sloping ground, and
cattle going to drink at a water hole. The
amateur ’grapher, Philip Caro, stood for the best
part of a day waiting for Strawberry and White-
foot and Lightfoot to pose gracefully into the
Jamberoo setting.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Our old friend, Wally, grows amusingly
patronising towards his old employer. In a
private letter, he is reported by the Argus as
writing: ‘ Poor Lord Linlithgow is, I fear, very
ill. He has now gone to Bavaria to try a new
doctor, but I confess (forsooth, he does) that I
don’t like the accounts I have received (ahem !)
at all.’ This from the obsequious-nosed Hopetoun
secretary of old ! The ‘ better-not ’ attitude is
for ever gone. The above letter-tone is one of
pure good-chappie concern.
“ The life of a Bohemian in Melbourne is not
all beer and skittles. It has its ups and its downs.
One of the fraternity, whose fine moustache and
massive proportions are well-known to frequenters
of the Block, has, or had, a comfortably furnished
suite of rooms at the top of a tall building, 79
steps up. The other Saturday he went out, lock-
ing the door behind him, and leaving his little
spirit-stove burning, ready to make a fragrant
oup of tea, or rather two cups, all set out on the
table with bachelor-like neatness. During his
absence, two men were observed to come up like
wolves on the fold, and proceed to hand things
through the window. Chairs, carpet, table,
spirit-stove, cups, and many accessories followed
one another swiftly on to the landing, ; while
these dread, silent men breathed never a
word. Soon after their departure from the now
bare room, the unsuspecting tenant came toiling
up the 79 steps. He was attended by one of his
Block visions. He had invited her to lunch a
deux, and, as he gaily unlocked the door, he mur-
mured, ‘Now, we shan’t be long.’ Then came
the shock.
“The well-advertised wedding of Miss Mary
Clarke and Captain Robert Lindsay attracted a
record attendance to the Anglican Cathedral and
caused nigh a deadlock in the traffic. The defunct
Rupertswood Battery was revived to parade for
the event. Down the aisle their aggressive spurs
clanked feebly, and there was no excuse in their
knock-kneed numbers to warrant the resurrection.
Newspapers spoke of a cheer at their appearance,
or the bride’s arrival—one or t’other. There was
a cheer—the result of a one-eyed tramp being
saved from violent contact with a spirited horse.
“ The bridegroom boasted the sylphic waist of
the up-to-date English ‘Johnny’—a very de-
cidedly attenuated one which would do wonders
as a corset advertisement. He is a fair sample of
the English brass-button dandy who waits upon
vioe-regals.
“ The white chiffons and scarlet mountings of
the bridesmaids provided the sight of the day,
for, whereas brides look all alike at a distance,
three pairs of bridesmaids in three degrees of
height crowned in scarlet blossoms are a novelty.
Miss Vera Clarke and Miss Le Souef (of the Zoo
Director’s family) carried their basket hats and
their straight scarlet sticks with an air of com-
mand to suit their inches. The second pair—
Misses Ivy Clarke and Erica Hughes—substituted
a show of chocolate hose and shoes for the long
chiffon trains of the advance guard. Haughty
young Misses these. The smallest two held the
great train, and were mostly lost to sight.
“ The feature of the Clarke bride’s wedding
toilette was the costly English Honiton lace,
woven for the present Princess of Wales (then
Princess May Teck), when she was booked to
marry the late Duke of Clarence. The bride-
groom-elect died, and the bridal-lace, sold by
auction, was bought by the late Baronet W.
Clarke. 111-omened lace, seems to me ! Another
uncanny thing about that ex-royal lace is that its
pattern is May-blossoms. Weddings and the
month of May, or anything belonging to that
month, are best apart. The crush of marriages
in April is not only owing to the fact that Lent is
over, but that ‘ unlucky ’ May is to come.
“ Dowager Lady Janet was dressed up to the
occasion, which means much. Her dove-grey
outfit, her sprightly figure, her inky and luxurious
coiffure made her impressive. Mrs. Cruickshank,
in gorgeous floral ribbon and lace-striped gown,
and Mrs. Charlie Ryan (whose dress-mounting
was of costly fur, with interposing lace fans) were
the two smartest dames in the family pews.
“ Surpliced greybeards rushed in where mem-
bers of the Force feared to tread to keep the aisles
clear of curious females. The most energetic was
that city magnate and veteran of authority—
F. R. Godfrey. He dashed across the corns of
the most important guests (the more important
the guest the greater the corn) in his determined
efforts to clear first one side aisle and then
another. A hoary member of the local Upper
House, in his desire to see the show, mounted the
pulpit, and his bald pate gleamed upon the pro-
ceedings like the pale benediction of a bladder of
lard in a small-goods shop.
“The one inglorious incident of the wedding
was the going-off. Word arrived at ‘ Cliveden ’
that a great rally of gaspers were lining the
Spencer-street station, so the newly-married pair
arranged to go on board at North Melbourne.
Arrived at the station of call, news was conveyed
that the train was late, and would not be in for
35 minutes, though an urgent message had been
sent to Bent to come and roar behind it. The
Jehu was bidden to take a few turns in the near
roads. He did, and the English bridegroom had
a view of Swamp-road and the prairie across
which the odors of the bone-mills blow. Truly a
sweet spot! When, at length, the train turned
up, only a few factory hands and railway gangers
were about, and these did not concern themselves
about the long, lean stranger in the skin-tight
suit, or about the Grey Lady.
“Governors and aides showered stationery
effects on the ‘ Cliveden ’ bride. Bachelors mostly
gave books and glove-stretchers. There must
have been quite a dozen copies of Tennyson, an
exquisite edition presented by the Gov.-General
heading the list. Archbishop Carr sent his com-
pliments and silverware to the bride in his suave
way, although he had sternly refused to sanction
a fair member of his flock acting as Miss Clarke’s
bride-help in ‘ the other Cathedral.’
“On the day of the Clarke wedding Judge
Madden and his liege lady opened the Chrysan-
themum Show in the Town Hall. They were
upholstered to match in two shades of grey. Her
pale-grey cloth (smooth, not hairy) was banded
with darker tones of same shade; his delicate grey
binding had deeper facings, and a color-splash in
the shape of a red tie. On the platform, the
Waxellency palavered pleasantly, as usual, excus-
ing Gov. Clarke’s absence by surmising that that
nice, clean little Person (just arrived from Sydney)
was ‘in his bath at this very minute.’
“Director Tate of the Education Department
is dubbed by some critics a wild enthusiast, and
an aesthetic humbug. But one tangible effect of
the Tate curriculum appeared the other day in
the commendable form of cut flowers, exhibited
by State schools pupils at the Chrysanthemum
Show, displaying the gardening skill of the
youngsters. Much tender care had been bestowed
on the blooms. Most of the exhibitors, I hear,
were boys. Now the male cottage-gardener
seldom turns the hose on the mate of his home,
or strikes her with the trowel, or punctures her
cheek with the rake. The training of boys in
flower ways is morally and physically beneficial.
Therein let Director Tate be applauded.
“ Another marriage of the week was of Mel-
bourne’s ’cellist, Herr Hattenbach, and Dr. Hef-
fernan’s daughter Eileen. They are twin-souls in
’cello duetting and musical rhapsodies.
“ The Italian mail brings news that Randolph
Bedford is showing his loyalty on a bed of sick-
ness. The letter runs—
‘ I went down to Florence expressly to see Randolph
Bedford. Found him ill, very, with appendicitis. But its
worst stage was mastered, and old habits prevailed—the
tongue or the pen went at express speed. Age cannot
wither or sickness stale his gusty confidence.
“ Lady Silverlocks had an idea of planning a
golf links on some of the ‘ Stonington ’ property.
She has often swung a cleek on an English course.
But a croquet green was set while she deliberated
about t’other. There she beats all opponents.
The Clarkes hear constant rumors of a change of
residence —and, being obliging and pleasant per-
sons, are ready to move for’ard according to public
desire, should the will of Parliament be so bent.
“I joined the Panama hat procession one cold
damp day this week to have a look at the
Aquarium and Annette Kellermann for my
self. It wasn’t diving-time, and the diving’
lady was curled up in a corner of the gallery
reading a book. There were some minutes to
spare, and we plunged into talk. She likes
the dip well enough, but ‘I don’t like them
crowding up,’ she grimaced with a jerk of the
head towards the Panama hats in the distance—
‘but they don’t know me, in this get up. They
often stand about after the performance watching
I stroll lazily out of the passage, pass them and
leave them looking for Annette the Diver ! ’ She
laughs, stretches herself, but keeps a thumb in
the book. ‘l’m a beggar for reading. And
I feel the cold here before I get in the water
The sea is soft, yielding, warm—so different. There
are two ponds—come and I’ll point out my pet fish
This fish pond’s my favorites, I confess I don’t
like the “gummy” (a species of shark) so the
man takes him out before I come on the scene
People wonder how I can plunge in among ali
these fish. But how much cleaner than the dirty
women in some of the sea baths? I’ve rubbed
shoulders with some rare old human dust-heaps
down at the seaside. They put fresh water in
here every day ; besides, I never swallow a drop.’
A bell is rung. Pa Kellermann shouts out the order
of the dips, and the plump, complacent girl, with
the fine brown eyes and the soft mouth, and the
drawl, saunters off to disrobe.
“Then into the show-case of fish shoots the
brown figure of the diver. She swims round-
scattering eels, bream, pike, porcupine to the four
corners of the case. Round and round her beauti-
ful body moves—swiftly, rhythmically, the brown
limbs taking sure, graceful side-strokes. A fine
performance. Tricks follow —plate-gathering, a
marine meal, and other popular items. Then a
detour is made to the open pond, where the diving
of the day comes off. The wet girl in her brief
leopard-striped gown runs along a passage, past
some stragglers, skips inside the rails, and gains
the spring-board. There, standing, till the
tarradiddle concerning length and breadth of
the pond, difficulties of compressed water,
and other tedious details is done, Annette poses
like a well-modelled figure in bronze. The legs
are fine, the feet neat, the toes prettier than
the fingers. She stands at tension, then shoots
out into the water. Fancy strokes—a variety-
exhibit those smooth limbs at different angles, in
enticing curves. There’s an assortment of dives,
too, which prove Annette something of an acro-
bat. A honey-pot double spring is the greatest
novelty in this line. But the physical charm of
the girl comes before even her skill. The Panamas
admit that.
“ ‘Midsummer Night’s Dream’ fairies 'are not
indoor, dressed-up, dolly frauds. Not a sprite
but stands for either a flower-fay or an insect-
midget. Shakspeare evidently thought of ‘fairies’
as the souls of grass-stems and dew-drops. Note
the four he mentions: Pease-blossom, Mustard-
Seed, Cobweb and Moth Titania, as seen on the
Princess stage, suggests a"*white-and-gold butter-
fly. All her attendant fays are gentle, little
gauzy midgets, ‘wee bits of blossom,’or lilliput
insects. The most formidable is a purple thistle,
who stands guard over ‘ the bank where the wild
thyme blows,’ but flees at the first glimpse of in-
truding Oberon. The latter (Miss Rignold) is
tall, ‘ slim as a willow-wand, dark-haired and
pensive.’ The get-up calls to mind a glorified
green dragon-fly; and Oberon’s elves are greeny-
gold and sheeny, like lizards. Oberon, singing
‘ I know a bank,’ in a soft contralto, sounds like a
‘ shepherd boy piping as though he should never
be old, ’ while Isabel Webster’s ‘ Over Hill, Over
Dale’ blends equally well with the glamorous
atmosphere.
“The Melb. Shakspeare Society celebrated
last week the birthday of the great William, who
conveniently economised dates by dying on the
anniversary of his birth. Chief-Justice Madden
—who brightened visibly on being again called
‘Your Excellency’—said a few words with cus-
tomary fluency, recalling old days when he had
Elayed truant and scaled school-fences to see
hakspeare played. (Incredulous applause.) He
deplored the tendency of the present day, which
cared for nothing so much as light music, no plot,
plenty of high-kicking, and good legs. (Blushes
by society.) Finally, he hoped romantically for
the establishment of a theatrical temple for
William alone.
“Malcolm M'Eaoharn has brought back a
miniature Noah’s Ark of furred and feathered
pets from Japan to swell the menagerie already
marking ‘ Goatlands ’ (suitable name that) as an
abode of much live interest.
“ Miss Watts-Phillips, the grotesque dame of
the marigold wig in ‘ Mistakes will Happen,’ is as
merry a soul off the boards as on. Her uneven
features bear the stamp of character—lots of it—
and form a prop for the lady’s line of good humor.
And her smile ! The average tape-measured
grin is small, trivial, mean, stupid beside that
extensive break-up of face.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Pa Brunker, whom many people think
should be leader of the N.S.W. Opposition vice
Cocky Carruthers, entered his 72nd year on Tues-
day.
Speaker Holder, the cadaverous, whose
business is politics and who finds his recreation in
preaching, will end his 53rd year on Wednesday,
May 13.
Passed out in Bendigo Hospital lately, as
an old-age pensioner, J. K. Scott, who in the old
plunging days was reckoned to be worth on paper
something like a million.
Newtown (Sydney) police-court supplied a
novel assault case recently, when a wife was
charged with “spitting in her husband’s ear”!
It cost her £1 15s. lOd.
Ebenezer Ward, a vanished S.A. politician,
has commenced canvassing his long-promised book
of reminiscences. Adelaide papers have been
constantly mentioning that book for the past three
years.
After all, death was most likely very kind
to the distinguished old diplomat and Empire-
slave, Dufferin ; for had he lived he must almost
certainly have stood now alongside Whitaker
Wright.
The Premiers who attended the late
conference in Sydney all spoke of the press as
their natural enemy. Yet three of them are
direotly concerned in the running of newspapers,
and the others are occasional leader writers.
ONE LADY TO ANOTHER.
“ I was never more insulted in all my life, and,
if it 'adn't been for soilin’ my ’ands I'd a
stoushed ’er in the jaw !”
Yes-No Reid in the Australian Common-
wealth had a prototype in the English Common-
wealth. John Morley, in criticising one of the
most abject crawlers of the Cromwellian period,
says: “Savillewas no edifying character, but a
politician who would fain say both Yes and No.”
A relic of the old military days of N.S.W.
was buried in Balmain cemetery one day last
week. Mr. John Taylor Finlay was a color-
sergeant in the 12th Regiment (Colonel Bloom-
field) which flourished and flogged and generally
held forth in Victoria Barracks in the long ago.
John O’Connell, the out-west (N.S.W.)
pioneer who went to bed one night lately with
unimpaired vision and woke to find himself blind,
only survived the shock a few weeks. He was a
straight-goer, known on many tracks, and owned
in his time a few among the best of the back-
country horses.
Melbourne Bishop Clarke at a recent
meeting :
Friends in England told me that I would soon be back
begging for money. “ Never,” I exclaimed, “if it comes
to that, I resign.”
They are beginning to know the Australian bishop
and his ways in England.
Mrs. Charles Bright asserted from the
Unitarian Church platform that her husband gave
up an assured income of £1250 per annum to take
the freethought platform. She added : “His
friends said he was mad.” Mrs. Bright, by the
way, has expressed her intention of continuing
the work in the Hyde Park church.
A late issue of San Francisco Call gives a
three-column interview and photo, of that very
dreary personage, Macdonald Paterson, who mis-
represents Brisbane in the House of Reps. The
Call has a strangely erroneous idea that the
M‘Pat is the wit of the House ; also that he twice
refused the Premiership of Queensland.
A live Sultan is coming to Australia—
—probably the first of them to wander this far.
Ibrahim is his name, and he comes from Johore,
in the Malay Peninsula. Also he is a K.C.M.G.
—all the same as John See and Alec. Peacock.
Just before he left (he is now dawdling in India)
he went out before breakfast and shot a tiger 9ft.
long.
Died in S.A., a week or two ago, one
John Barrow, who claimed to have taught Judge
Boucaut how to drive bullocks. Rather an in-
teresting bit of news, as Boucaut, being a modest,
unassuming individual, did not boast his know-
ledge and never drove bullocks in Rundle-street,
and so people didn’t know that he could drive ’em
—if he wanted to.
The “ spooks ” at Melbourne have resur-
rected Julian Thomas (“The Vagabond ”), whose
alleged spirit, at a recent seance, promised to
supply copy to any journalist who would attend
and take shorthand notes. Unfortunately, when
the spook of Tennyson is called up it can t pro-
duce verse, and Bacon’s spook is ignorant of prose,
and the„ghost of Columbus knows nothing of navi-
gation. Worst of all, a Sydney medium who
used to call up anybody at a minute’s notice, once
roused the spirit of Spinola, and found that
but confused Italian soldier under the impression
that he had fought at Waterloo. The writing
ghost also knows only the same language as the
medium, and her.
Dr. James Pryoe Phillips, who died in
Adelaide on April 21 at the age of 82, had lived
next to the Adelaide Club, on the North Terrace,
ever since the beginning of the recollection of the
middle-aged men of the city. He got his diploma
in London in 1843. He and Dr. Wylde (still
living at the Semaphore) were in St. Bartholo-
mew’s Hospital as fellow students in 1840, and
three years later Dr. Phillips left for S.A., whither
Dr. Wylde followed him in 1853.
The Dowager Lady Cook, Tennessee
Claflin, whose writings on sex subjects used to be
sent in deluges over the arid newspaper back-
country of Australia, has been cast in £5OO
damages, in London, for slandering the lurky
person whom she employed to watch her husband
(Baronet Cook), when he was still alive, to see
that he didn’t carry on with some other lady. The
British aristocracy seems of late to have been
almost unanimous in “ carrying on.”
A correspondent to Sydney News tells a
humorous story of the Owe’Sullivan. He was
invited out to a picnic, and part of the proceed-
ings was a schnapper fishing party. The Owe
found the sea unsympathetic, and it insisted on a
loan of his dinner. The sea-sick personage there-
upon, it is alleged, commanded the boat s skipper
to return, and threatened him with the loss of his
certificate if he didn’t obey. And the skipper
took no notice, and the Owe had to suffer for a
while. On returning to land he recovered, and
apologised for any wild remarks made whilst
indisposed.
The little weekly Scrapdish, issued from
the Sydney D.T. office, published the other day
what it called a “character sketch” of Car-
ruthers. This was evidently intended as eulogy,
but reads like malignant irony. Extract:
Bacon said that his province was mankind. The pro-
vince of Carruthers covered every nook and corner, every
road, every lane, every park, every public and semi-
public building in his electorate. As the day for depu-
tations recurred, Carruthers, with the three other mem-
bers in tow, and with a line of mayors, aldermen, members
of school boards, respected townsmen, filled the passages,
blocked the entrances, awed the officials in every depart-
ment. When the Colonial Secretary had been attacked
and a reluctant promise had been wrung from him for a
new park half-a-dozen yards from a couple of other
parks, the deputation fell into irregular order and laid
siege to the Postmaster-General until he had promised a
tower to a post-office, or a telephone to a developing
suburb.
And this determined mendicant, this local-pump
politician, this blustering beggar, this political
footpad with a petition in one hand and a club in
the other, this shameless solicitor of Government
favors for his constituents, is held up as the
heaven-sent statesman who will pull New South
Wales out of the slough of despond and set her up
better than new ! His worst enemy could not
have penned a more damning indictment of Car-
ruthers as a mere legislator, leaving statesmanship
aside as being absurdly irrelevant.
No writer sticks up for the poor husbands
who are victims of genius—like worthy drysalter
John Tayloy, who had to yield to John Stuart
Mill; Bardi, who married Dante’s Beatrice ;
Imhoff, who made way for Warren Hastings ;
Ruskin, with the conquering Millais ; Roland,
whose intellectual Madame was captured by
Buzot; Caux, ousted by Nicolini. Bub
genius, like the champion pie-biter, has a
hand like a whale’s ear, and when it is
wafted towards a pie, that is genius’s pie,
you understand.
Humphrey Oxenham, who is again bound
England wards, made his first rise by carrying
an enormous pumpkin on his head from Duke’s
Hotel, Bathurst, to the Melbourne Hotel, Kelso, a
distance of about two miles, for a £lO wager. One
condition of the wager was that the weight-carrier
should not put a hand near the pumpkin from
start to finish. H.O. won easily, and he has been
winning easily ever since.
“F.” writes:— ‘
Apropos Professor Halford recently mentioned in
Bulletin. Another reminiscence which the octogenarian
can give is that in connection with the execution of
Christopher Harrison for the murder of his partner,
Marsh, at the Public Works Office, Melbourne, in Feb.,
1864. They were contractors. Marsh delayed certain
payments, and Harrison considered his reputation ruined
and shot his partner. Great exertions were made to save
him from the noose, and they would have succeeded but
that Woods and Carver were to be executed the same
morning for the attempted robbery under arms (and
shooting) at the E.S. and A. Bank, Fitzroy. Premier
James M‘Oulloch argued that the bank robbers had
killed no one, yet they must hang, therefore Harrison
should hang also. Halford and many other medicoes
believed Harrison insane. By some means the brain of
Harrison was taken away after the post mortem examina-
tion and submitted to a caucus of medical men—Halford,
demonstrator—in a room over the shop of George Robert-
son, bookseller, in Collins-street This little arrangement
leaked out, and M'Culloch sent the sheriff, Claude Farie,
to demand the brain. When Farie arrived he found a
number of doctors handling portions of the brain “ like so
many oysters." The sheriff demanded the portions of
the deceased as being the “Government’s brains,” and
they were handed over, wrapped in paper. Farie placed
them in his coat-tail pocket (he wore the old-fashioned
claw-hammer coat) and walked up Collins-street towards
the Treasury buildings with from 4 to 19 dogs—the num-
ber varied as old dogs got tired and dropped out and new
dogs joined in—straggling at his heels and nosing his
pocket;. To this day it isn’t certain whether that pro-
cession annexed the gruesome relic or not.
William Pemberton, a Newton (N.S. W.)
patriarch, discourses eloquently about Premier
Parkes when the latter was farm-laboring for
Jamison on the Nepean. Parkes was, ac-
cording to Pemberton, pugnacious at that
period, and more than once settled a sheep-
shearing difference in primitive style. He
was then aged about 25 years, and was
reputed a hefty fighter among hisclass.
According to London advices,
Mrs. Mayorick, sentenced to death
in August, 1889, for poisoning her
husband, and afterwards ordered to
be imprisoned for life, will be liber-
ated m July, 1904. There was al-
ways a prejudice in England against
hanging a pretty woman, and much
was made of the medical evidence
that the quantity of arsenic found in
the deceased’s body was not nearly
sufficient to cause death, and that its
presence might be explained by his
habit of taking arsenical tonics.
This is the sort of twaddle the
English people get about Australia.
Home Chat (22/11/’O2) says: “Lady
Tennyson, the wife of the Governor-
General of Australia, has spent many
Christmases in Australia, her hus-
band having been Governor of South
Australia before he became the
official head of the federated States.
Her position has by no means been an
easy one. Society in South Australia,
for instance, is very'different to what is
meant by the same thing in England.
On one occasion when up country she
kissed the child of a working man."
One prominent N.S.W. Labor mem-
ber, like all his county-men, loves a good
fight, but considers that, in his position as
an M.P. and a J.P., he should discounten-
ance any breach of the peace. The other
day, in a mining distriot, pay-morning had
been celebrated by a huge drunk and then a
scrimmage between two miners, who,
stripped to the waist, were punishing the
air severely, and occasionally knocking small
splinters off one another. The member was
on the spot at the time with another M.P.,
“ organising.” “Aw reckon we oort to stop
this,” he said, climbing up a fence to get a
better view. “Eh, wull hit, lad, wull hit,
grand—as a J.P., not to speak of a legisla-
tor —oh ! get in, lads, get in ; doan’t be
a-sawing of the air !’’ And so on to the end.
King Edward’s performances are always
excellently stage-managed. The Kaiser would
give something to know how the dove was made
to hover over the royal yacht in the Mediter-
ranean last week. Was a string tied to its leg ?
Old Capt. Randall, the first man to take a
steamer up the Murray—he antedated Capt.
Cadell by some weeks—still lives at Gumeracha,
which he represented for six years in the S.A.
Parliament. He completed his 69th year on
May 1.
PASTOR BEVAN.
Passed away, at Timaru (M.L.), a 40
years resident in Captain J. H. Sutter, aged 84.
Prior to the gold discoveries, he traded to the
West Indies and Singapore ; was connected with
the China tea trade, and afterwards engaged in
the Arctic seal and whale biz. He was one of the
first to take a steamer into the Arctic ice. For
seven years Sutter represented Geraldine in the
M.L. House of Reps.
A FAMILY WEAKNESS.
“You seem very fond of cats, Miss Chivvy."
“ Yes, we both adore animals, only my sister pre-
fers puppies .”
A well-known Adelaide business man was
angling at Port Elliot, and the fish were biting
freely. He had landed a dozen and was half-way
through the second dozen when a boatful of gay
and giddy girls came round the bend and pulled
in his direction. Full of devilment, and despite
his remonstrances re frightening the fish, the girls
stopped the boat on his fishing ground. He
coaxed, argued and finally stormed at them, but
they refused to move and began to pass derisive
remarks about his side whiskers. In desperation
he took off his coat, then his vest, the ladies
watching him with interest. Then off came his
shirt and still the females moved not. At length,
minus all save trousers, he shouted, “Women, are
you going ? ” A chorus of laughter and cries of
“ No.” Then that business man, his courage
giving out, put on his clothes again, and after
making some severe remarks quitted the premises.
Concerning a peer, writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent :
Apropos the Norfolk Island peer, a peculiar incident
happened in Sydney. The original registers of births,
marriages, and deaths in N.S. Wales up to 1856 are not in
existence as far as can be discovered. An old gentleman,
named H. Kerrison James, who was connected with the
diocesan registry, had certified copies, made by himself,
therefore his private property. Young Pery was sent to
Norfolk Island under Colonel Anderson, and married
there. A son was born in 1840, who in time came- to be
Earl of Limerick. When he wished to take his seat in
the House of Lords he was blocked, as-he could not pro-
duce a certificate of birth. The Earl wrote to Colonel
Bloomfield, in Tasmania, to obtain the certificate regard-
less of cost. After much hunting the Colonel found- that
Norfolk Island was a part of N.S.W. in 1840, and sought
in Sydney what he wanted. He learned all about Mr.
James, and peremptorily demanded the birth certificate.
James replied that he could have a copy for 20 guineas.
The Colonel blustered, and James, not wishing tbkecp
Pery-Limerick out of his inheritance—and a poor peerage
it is—sent the copy to his agents in London, and wrote
to the Earl that the copy was with his lawyers, and he
could obtain it on payment of fees. The Earl, on receipt,
at once sent his solicitor to James’ agent, when the fol-
lowing occurred : “ You have a certified copy of the Earl
of Limerick’s birth ? ” “We have.” “ Will you show it to
us?” “Yes, here it is, duly endorsed by Mr. James, ‘ The
enclosed is a true and faithful copy of the birth certificate
of the Earl of Limerick at Norfolk Island, a dependency of
New South Wales, in 1840. (Signed) H. Kerrison James,
Registrar.’ ” The Earl’s solicitor took a pencil and copied
this. He made an affidavit that he had seen the certifi-
cate of birth in the handwriting of the Registrar, and the
Earl took his seat next day without paying Mr. Jathes a
red cent. The N.S.W. Parliament afterwards passed an
Act under which James’ registers were and
became the property of the State.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— In the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.

“ Cicada ” : The so-called Cape gooseberry
(Physalis peruviana) is not a gooseberry, and
does not come from the Cape. It belongs to the
same natural order as the tomato, and is a native
of tropical America. It was originally described
from Peru, whence it was introduced into Aus-
tralia many years ago. Through the agency of
fruit-eating birds it was carried into virgin scrubs
far ahead of all settlement, and is now widely
scattered all over the country.
Dear Bulletin.—“ B.” says : “ May not
be generally known that the common black-and-
white magpie is excellent eating, many preferring
it to the bronzewing pigeon, which it somewhat
resembles. ’’ I say : May be not generally known
that the common black-and-white magpie is pro-
tected by law from the attacks of ignorant new
•hums and callous brutes; and rightly so, for
“ maggie ” not only does much practical good in
the destruction of insects, &c., but her unique
warble is a source of continual delight. Let “B.”
lift his head from his feeding-trough and listen to
it, and then ask a friend to kick him. If “B.”
will give his name and address, the writer will
have much pleasure in drawing the attention of
the local police to his habits as implied in his par.
—H.P.

“ Bobrid ” : A final word to “A.8.T.”
See no reason yet to alter my opinion re “squat-
ting-color ” theory about rabbits. I write about
what I know, not what 1 think I know. The
bush, as I know it, is grey; it may have other
colors, but a lot depends on the whisky you
drink ! According to “ A.B.T. ’s” theory, a blue
rabbit should “squat” under a “blue”-gum—
which isn’t blue either ; and green rabbits should
be the Spring variety, gradually growing grey as
the grass dries off! What next? Have been
hunting rabbits with dogs, with guns, with traps,
with poisons, with everything which fools have
invented to knock out bunny, and never saw a
rabbit yet perform the antics described by
“A.8.T.,” when he had a straight run to his
burrow. When his retreat is cut off, bunny has
to resort to strategy to get home, but his color
doesn’t worry him then ! He hasn’t time to think
about it. Again, rabbits don’t go too far away
from home in daylight, neither do they feed in
daylight.

“ Wiljar ” : Concerning the inhospitable
cookie (“ Deeby,” 8., 11/4/’O3). In Victoria, the
further down you get the worse you’ll find him.
Whereas in the Mallee a man “can’t go wrong”
for a feed (though it may only be damper and
bunny), he must be satisfied with a smell among
the well-to-do wheat-growers south of the Divid-
ing Range. The cold-stare response of the Gipps-
land farmer, when asked for a meal, has become
proverbial. Was talking to a St. Arnaud ox-
owner who, when in the Western district recently,
called on nearly fifty agriculturists same day to
fet grass for his bulls at a reasonable charge.
aid he started out early in the morning, and
didn’t taste a bite till he got back to camp —about
11 p.m. “Caught 20 per cent, of ’em red-handed,”
he said, “and got asked inside; but they seemed
to think a blanky man was all legs and brisket,
with no belly at all 1 ”
“ T. 8.” : The callousness of the bush-born
Australian to animal suffering is, to some extent,
explainable, if not excusable.
Nature in Australia is a harsh
stepmother to the flocks and
herds foisted upon her,
and, as custom begets
toleration, the callous-
ness of the bushman
is akin to the indif-
erence of the hospi-
tal nurse. In the
vicinity of bush
townships are to bo
found plough-horses
working with shoul-
ders raw and fester-
ing from top to point, the
sores in some cases even
forming pockets partially
overgrown with skin. In
most cases these sores
are due to ill-fitting or
worn-outcollars. Many
bushmen, when they
find a collar does not
fit, simply cut it in-
stead of lengthening
or shortening the draft
of the harness; and I
have seen collarsafterbeing
_ cut padded with chaff-bags
literally saturated with blood.
But of this the police see nothing, because when
•>ne of these unfortunate animals is driven into
the bush town a good broad pad is
worn under the collar and a sharp
look-out is kept for the traps.

“PetramoB” from an up-country
S.A. town:—
An artist here, everlastingly on the hunt for the realis-
tic, got an idea into his brain to paint a picture entitled
“ Delirium Tremens,” and sauntered forth into the local
street to find a model. During the quest, the idea
became surrounded with many drinks, but, despite this,
the artist managed to drag in and tie to a bedpost in his
“ studio ” a magnificent specimen of the spent-cheque-
seer-of-visions. The more the artist sketched the more
visions the model saw, till at last it became even too
much for the artist, who began to nerve himself up. And
so the sketching and nerving up continued till counter-
lunch time ; then till past dinner-time ; and it was not
till late in the afternoon that a friend dropped in and
found artist and model fighting for the possession of an
imaginary centipide, and each declaring that the other
was trying to steal his jim-jam. (This seems to me to
settle the long-vexed question whether, when two men
are equally drunk, they can see the same jim-jam or
fantod.) The picture lay face downwards on the floor.
It represented something that looked like a blue Governor
with horns, opening a new gridiron in Sheol.

“ David Harum ” : Re yarn of the newly
made J.P. (8., 11/4/’O3) who provided a man with
the wherewithal to get drunk, so that he might
have a case. I knew a country J.P. who did the
same, but the man, whilst intoxicated, got braw-
ling in “The White-Eyed Dragon,” floored the
landlord, smashed sundry bottles of good booze
and a window or two, resisted arrest, put a police-
man’s eyes in mourning, and behaved badly in
quod. As luck would have it, the Commissioner
of Police happened to pass through the town at
the time, and turned up at the court-house. The
long list of charges was read, and the prisoner
pleaded guilty. “ Give it to him hot,” whispered
the Commissioner ; “gaol without option.”
“TJm!” grunted the J.P. ; “ he’s got a wife and
family. A fine would do him more good.”
“Well, make it pretty heavy, then.” “£5 and
costs,” said the J.P., with an inward sigh, and
left the court, reckoning how dearly he had paid
for his dignity. The perfectly new J.P., when
he feels he must sentence something, had better
take a chair out into the bush, and there sit in
state and solemnly order the native bear to be
imprisoned for six months, and fine the “go-
hanna ” 10s. 6d.
IN THE DRY WEST.
Sketch outside a pub.
‘ ‘ Riverina ”: A few peculiarities of various
horses:—Knew an old bush racehorse which was
travelled round the Riverina as a “take-down.”
He was a hard-looking old case, with whips of
pace, and his owner made matches and money
easily. But the old nag had his little peculiari-
ties. He would finish like a coward unless his
rider leaned forward and cooeed madly in his ear.
Once he heard the cooee he stretched out to it in
a manner that made onlookers suddenly grow
sober. Doesn’t this suggest the rattling night
ride from pub. to hut with enough on board the
rider to make him view the world as a grand old
institution ? Another old bushman told me his
uncertain-looking nag could be caught only by
approaching him on the offside and waving a hat.
Have an idea this gentleman was only “pulling
my leg ” as regards the hat; but about the offside
he may have been speaking truly. Own a pony
myself which I always approach from offside.
Can’t s catch him then.
• • • *
Just before the western Q. heavy rains
arrived a “willie-wagtail,” “shepherds’ friend,”
“Australian nightingale,” or whatever other alias
the vivacious little black and white bird is known
by, built its nest in the rain-gauge glass at a
station homestead. Then the owner said
gloomily : “That settles it! I had some hopes
left for rain, but they’re gone now. That dashed
fowl knows the rain-gauge won’t be wanted this
year.” And saying this he packed his bag,
mounted the bones disguised in horse-hide, and
left for the south. Yet three days later the rain
came down so suddenly that the wagtail barely
escaped out of the gauge with its life. “ Signs of
rain” are mostly failures. “Signs of drought”
seem to be mostly ditto.

“Tonbur” : Struck a tent recently in
N.Q. and found a man gesticulating violently to
what I thought was another man, but which
proved to be a dummy rigged in coat, trousers
and hat. The semi-lunatic explained that the
effigy represented Bob Philp, and that that was
the usual way he aired his eloquence. Living
alone, off the main track, this man never saw a
white, save when he went to the station for
rations.

Notice in a Queensland paper :

No., &c., &c., that, unless a certain Bull-dog, or mongrel

* * » 9
After waiting months for rain, a Corop
(Vic.) cockie had five acres of sown ground
washed away, seed, subsoil, and all, by a passing
storm, a sort of thing hitherto unheard of in the
locality. Everything seems to come in a heap,
when it does come, in Australia.
Dear Bulletin, —“Matchbox” (B. 17/4/’O3)
to get off his squib of “ humor,” has, perforce, to
be inaccurate. “ Why ! that sand was distributed
over the Wycheproof farmers’ land by the wind,”
says “Matchbox,” with mirth. Really! If h e
will look up the mirth-provoking par again he will
see it stated that, “in those parts where the storms
had distributed the sand thinly over the land, the
soil had retained moisture in so remarkable a
degree as to embolden the cookies to try some sand
distribution on their own account.” Why don’t
men read with their brains ? Also, in these days
of horse or steam power for everything, no up-to-
date cockie would think of a “No. 4 shovel ” as a
means of distribution, and from the fact that such
a method was the only one which suggested itself
to the mind of “Matchbox” it would seem
that he has been dead for some time, say, half a
century. Had “ Matchbox ” ever seen a few
fertilising undertakings in the U.S.A. and Canada
he would see nothing wildly humorous in the idea
of a Vic. cockie fertilising his insignificant 100 or
500-acre kitchen-garden patch even with manure.
—Vul. (writer of the par).
ON THE HOB.
NEW CHUM : “ Will this horse— aw—stand fire."
GROOM: “Stand fire! Why, blime, he was foaled
in a kitchen!"
“ Riverina ” : There are people who say
the bush youngster is totally devoid of humor.
Let such people listen: My mate and I pulled up
at a selector’s place one day and were just pur-
chasing a few pounds of meat (the missus was
serving us) when a kiddy, aged about eleven,
came rushing round the corner yelling joyfully,
“Billy! Billy! come ’ere quick! the old mans
just tumbled down the well head bloomin
fust!” And Billy (also seeing the humorous side)
enthusiastically went.
0 * * *
Another curse of the thoughtless. The briar
plant was imported into the Glen Innes (N.9.W.)
district by a shepherd in 1864. Now, according
to the local Examines, thousands of acres are
covered with briar —in some cases so dense as to
defy the approach of man or beast. Shepherds
coming from briar-infested areas should be washed
and shaved by some public official.
MANANA
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The thriftless, lazy Portuguese has only one answer
to all remonstrance—“ Manana ” (To-morrow).
The land of Manana is virgin and new,
And it waits for the hand of a master ;
Its prairies are boundless, its people are few,
Yet they ceaselessly wail of disaster ;
For the land of Manana it breedeth a host
Of dawdlers, who languidly lean on a post,
And who always give in till they give up the ghost
To the tune of their anthem—“ Manana I ”
The land of Manana it liveth on loans,
And its battle-cry ever is—“ Borrow ! ”
The usurer picketh the flesh from its bones,
And bids it beware of the morrow ;
Each wight in that land is a weariful loon
Who has just as much “ go” as the Man in the Moon,
And who standeth in pawn from his hat to his shoon,
As he warbles for ever “ Manana 1 ”
The land of Manana is yearning for men,
For men who will battle defiant,
To level the mountain and dry the broad fen
And laugh the brave laugh of the giant;
For never a State in the ages ago
Was reft from the desert, the flood, or the foe,
Or won a fair name as a nation, I trow,
When its people were wailing “ Manana ! ”
The land of Manana is not very far,
You can hear its high bells in the steeple,
Its rulers are prating of “ Trust in our star,”
While the mortgages smother its people.
Its founders were tireless and gritty and bold,
Their hands were of iron—their hearts were of gold,
But the sons they begat were but feeble of mould—
Just hark how they prattle—“ Manana !”
The land of Manana hath lost the old seed
That ferried from Dublin and Dover ;
It needs a fresh strain of the bitter, big breed
That swept the high seas as a rover ;
It pines for a race that is sturdy of thew,
For a race that has faith in its energies true,
And it pines to get rid of a spiritless crew
Who murmur for ever—“ Manana ! ”
P. LUFTIG.
THE BUSH M.O.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twas at the pub at Boggy Pools
The Bhearers gave a ball;
And Bill the Ringer wrote the rules,
And stuck them on the wall.
Of ladies there were only three
(Not counting squint-eyed Nance),
But “ buck ” sets are no novelty
At any shearers’ dance.
The fairest damsel in the throng
Was new from Sydney town ;
Her skirt was short, her fringe was long,
Her hair was golden brown.
The Ringer ran her good points o’er
And marked her for his prey ;
He ambled down the ant-bed floor
In quite the city way.
With courtly grace he bowed his head
(A gallant man was he),
And to the city maid he said,
“ Miss ! wot price you an’ me?"
The maid, with icy dignity,
Remarked she’d not been used
To “ blokes wot chatted her when she
Had not been interjooced.”
Outpoured the shearer’s language red,
Outpoured his language blue ;
No words could paint the things he said
That city maid unto.
And when she’d heard right to the end
His cursings wild and crude,
She gently murmured to her friend,
“ Oh, Liza, ain’t he rude ! ”

AN EXPONENT OF LUNACY.
FATHER MURPHY : “ But I can't get you into the
Hospital for the Insane, my man; you're not
mentally afflicted."
THE CHRONIC SWAGGIE : “ But, yer see, sir, if yer

police ’ud be bound to run me in for bein'
insane an' wanderin’ at large. I leant a spell,

“Ne’er-do-Weel”: “ D. 8.” (Bulletin
21/3/’O3) writes re sufferings of horses out back
through flies—he says nothing of the sufferings of
humans. I have had nearly 20 years’ experience
in the Queensland bush, and have noticed that
flies become troublesome after rain and a flush of
grass; why, goodness only knows. We had fair
rains in December last and immediately were
afflicted with a fly pest, to which the famous
plague in Egypt could only have been a circum-
stance. When it commenced a decent night’s
sleep was out of the question—intense heat and
mosquitoes ensured wakefulness during the first
part of the night, and with dawn came the flies,
driving the unfortunate sufferer into a dark room.
They were everywhere in myriads, and the only
hope of escape was a dark room. Eating became
a task, only to be undertaken when forced by
absolute necessity, and then the greatest skill and
sleight of hand had to be exercised to avoid swal-
lowing a few hundred flies with every mouthful of
food. Every dish on the table was kept constantly
covered—removal of the covers resulted in the
immediate conversion of the gravy into fly hash.
Once I stupidly asked for soup, but before I could
raise a mouthful to my lips, flies were struggling
in it as if sprinkled from a castor. I passed, but
retained tiie plate near me to act as a trap. I
was not long over my meal, and before leaving
counted the flies in that soup—ll 7 ! Had the visi-
tation caused only vexation and annoyance it
would have been bad enough, but the bush was
strewn with cattle, sheep and horses in various
stages of decomposition, and the flies, when tired
of battening on these remains, rested on living
humans —on the face whenever possible, in the
corners of the eye for choice. Result, blight for
nine out of every ten. Poor little children suffered
horribly, some were permanently injured ; adults
suffered to a somewhat slighter extent. Also,
food and drink were contaminated by what is now
known to be the best common carrier of bacteria ;
result, epidemics of typhoid, dysentery, &c. I
never wish to put in such another two months as
I did in January and February, 1903, on the
Balonne. “D. 8.” draws a comparatively mild
picture of the sufferings of horses—cattle do not
seem to suffer to the same extent. The leather fly
veil is good, no doubt, but Balonneites went one
better. They sewed bagging completely round
the heads of their working horses over the eyes—
you can see the idea in your picture of the horse
by “ Hard Riding” out of “Poverty.” The
bagging did not blind the horses, as might be
Upposed. They could see through the covering,
and horses thus hideously but usefully adorned
were both ridden and driven on the Balonne.
Flies could not get through, and horses when
turned out were able to feed. Basil fly veils were
used by men whose work in the bush rendered a
mosquito net bag an impossibility. Whatever
hardships were put on the Israelites, I consider
Moses was altogether too rough on the Egyptians,
• • • •
“ Scutum ” :
The sunburnt side of a tree or a weed is a good guide
to the approximate north in open forest or plain country.
But in such places as the Oomboyne, Don Dorrigo and
Richmond brushes the sun never penetrates sufficiently
to have any effect on the trunks of the trees, and the
ground is covered with “ vegetation rank and rotten,”
which never “ feels the cheering ray.” There you have
to trust to cedar-tracks and luck. A few years ago our
surveying-party included a cook who professed to be able
to find his way anywhere by instinct. We were working
in the dense brush at the head of Camden Haven River.
One morning cook accompanied us to work, and about
4 p.m., in the thickest part of the brush, he said, “ Well,
boys, I’m goin’back to get tea ready.” “ Where’s the
camp from here, Peter?” he was asked. “ Straight in a
line with my finger,” said he, pointing to a scaly beech
Bft. through, and off he went. We were in bunk that
night when Peter reached the camp. His “ language ”
woke us. When chaffed about it afterwards he growled :
“ Well, I found the camp in the dark, didn’t I? What
more d’ye want?” But somehow he didn’t seem too
proud of his achievement.”
“ 0.P.” : Concerning the bush spieler
Amongst the peculiar
animals that Australia is
evolving the bush spieler
is one of the most in-
teresting. He frequent*
outpost towns and cen-
tres of sheep-raising dis-
tricts, seeking how many
large - hatted, shy - eyed
bushmen he may finan-
cially devour in a small,
mean way as they come
in periodically to water.
He usually runs about
five to the pub, but, like
the wallaby on the scrub-
lease, his full numbers
are not evident, except
at the watering places at
dusk. His appearance
in the tailor-made suit
and cheap jewellery is
much after the style of
his city brother with
the affluent uncle in Fiji
—even to the one large
wrinkle across the back
of the neck, made by
an incessant dropping of
the head to avoid the
disrespectful glances of
passers-by. Most of him
have “ done ” the bush
at some time or other,
and have come back fully
determined to “do” the
bushman instead. He
is to be found at various
stages of professional
progress, but is almost
invariably of the one
branch of the “ strong-
weakling ” division of
Australians. Loose - jointed,
active, muscular, but weak of
heart, lungs, and kidneys,
feeble-jawed, and with a Oham-
berlain nose, slightly bulby near
the point. He is invariably a bache-
lor and a strict individualist. His
subjects or objects of contempt are—Public subjects,
family life, religion, work, police, moleskins, and dull
conversation about sheep. He often owns a lOth-rate
racehorse or a “johnnycake” book, with which he
takes down the public. The police do their best
to make him earn his living, and often drive him to
a little shearing or droving. But he is brief, half-hearted
and unreliable at the business, and usually escapes the
painful thing by going on circuit to shows and race-
meetings, whereat his three-cornered eye may avail him
much. His methods are still of the old order—“ pogue-
diving,” “ nob-ringing,” and double-banking lone-hand
simpletons in play. I don’t know whether he has any
line of logic to justify him, but his hatred of the police is
evident enough, and his one unprintable epithet for the
common person who works and steals not is always to be
heard where two of him are congregated together. Bad
breeding, bad rearing, the abominably coarse, poorhouse,
lag system still in vogue at stations, and the dull, un-
profitable monotony of bush-life in general, are the main
things that are evolving him. Breed and environment
turn him on the world lazy and vicious. The prospects
of the bush frighten him; and, knowing no trade where-
with to earn a living in a township, he decides to let
others earn it for him, and, 10, he falls. This is a double-
barrelled argument in favor of improvement in bush-
workers’ accommodation and extension of the technical
education system.

“Land Crab”: Have seen “poowong”-
worms 7fb. long, and from an uprooted tree I
have collected dozens over sft. Have seen two
ducks get hold of a huge poowong, one at each
end, and after swallowing a few feet each, get
hopeless of reaching the middle, and gjive it up
by running backwards —to begin again. This
worm lays a cigar-shaped egg, 2 to 3in. long,
with a tough skin, and containing milky stuff
and a young worm about 6in. long. This is no
M'Cooey, but hard fact.

“ Polstan ” : Re large mouse families (B ,
4/4/’03); mice often share one nest among several,
and the crowd that “T.H.’’ happened across
would very likely be owned by about ten mothers.
Cannot explain the reason of the amalgamation,
unless it is to save trouble in the cooking and
washing up.

hard work and common sense, though the paper currency
is a valuable idea. Canada, by reason of its very heavy
Protective tariff (about four times as heavy as ours), pays
away less than half as much for imports as we do, and
manufactures more than twice as much. And these

THE GENTS UPON THE ROAD.
Now, all ye chaps as ’as ne’er been
Out a-humpin’ of yer ‘ bluey’ on the track,
Just yer ’old yer bloomin’ throttle
And a pace or two take back
When yer meets a ‘ gent upon the road.’
’Cause yer sport a ’at that’s shiny
And a shirt chock full of glue and patent starch,
Don’t yer reckon as you’ll never
Be a-found upon the march
With the ‘ gents who live upon the road.’
Yer can go to balls an’ parties
With a damsel that ’ud light a bloomin’ pipe,
And go kitterin’ round the ballroom
In amongst ther ‘ city tripe,’
But be ’spectful to the ‘ gents upon the road.’


AN ATTEMPT UPON THE AUSTRALIAN TREASURY.
THE STATE PREMIERS PROPOSE THAT THE AUSTRALIAN GOVERNMENT SHALL FLOAT A LOAN TO PAY FOR STATE PROPERTIES TAKEN OVER (PROPERTIES BUILT WITH LOAN MONEY) SO THAT A POST OFFICE WORTH £1000
SHALL HAVE £2000 OF MORTGAGES ON IT—THE £1000 WHICH THE STATE BORROWED TO BUILD IT, AND THE £1000 WHICH THE COMMONWEALTH BORROWED TO BUY IT. TREASURER TURNER REFUSES TO ENTERTAIN THE IDEA.

“Your Bile Beans have proved a valuable
remedy in my household,” says Mrs. E. J. Rumus,
of 1 Arundle-street, Forest Lodge, Sydney. “I
was sometimes troubled with debility, especially
during the hot weather, and when I feel done up
with a tired, listless feeling, I take a bean or two,
with beneficial results. As a gentle purgative
they are all that can be desired, and leave no evil
after-effects whatever. I can safely say Bile
Beans are unequalled for poor appetite, sleepless-
ness, and that out-of-sorts feeling so prevalent at
the present season.” Bile Beans have now a
world wide reputation for curing biliousness, head-
ache, indigestion, constipation, piles, debility,
female weaknesses, nervousness, bad blood, pimples
and all skin eruptions, bad breath, anaemia, loss
of appetite, sleeplessness, lumbago, rheumatism,
and, in fact, all ailments that owe their origin to
defective bile flow, assimilation, digestion, etc.
Bile Beans are obtainable from all medicine
vendors, price Is. l|d. or 2s. 9d. large box,

Business, Robbery, etc.
“J.A.C.”: “ Eko ” (8., 18/4/’O3) thinks that
the payment to a pawnbroker of 100 per cent, per annum
“ isn’t so shocking as it looks.” It appears tome to be
just as shocking as it looks. Passing over some of
“ Eko’s ” remarkable arithmetical problems, if one has to
pay 3d. per diem on a parcel worth 55., deposited in a
railway luggage-office, that isn’t interest but a ridicu-
lously exorbitant storage fee, and one exorbitant charge
doesn’t justify another. I have paid half-a-crown to get
a dog, worth less than 65., put out of his misery, but
that didn’t necessarily represent 60 per cent, per day
(18,250 per cent, per annum) on the dog. I may inform
“ Eko ” that some pawnbrokers are not satisfied with 100
per cent. ; I have frequently known 4s. per annum to be
charged on a loan of 3s. Further, many establishments
advertise that they charge only 6d. per month in the £—
roughly, 30 per cent.—enough, in all conscience—and
then unblushingly charge the same amount for 10s., thus
doubling the rate. If “ Eko ” likes to reckon up how
many thousand per cent, some hard-working women,
who, from sheer necessity, pawn a skirt from Friday
till Saturday, have to pay, the result might sur-
prise him. The fact that many pawnbrokers don’t get
rich has no bearing on the case. If there were a suf-
ficiently great superfluity of pawnbrokers, they wouldn't
get rich even if the rates were put up to 10,000 per cent.,
simply because the 10,000 per cent, might not happen
often enough. A great deal depends on the frequency of
things. If a man picks up Is. and spends half-a-minute
in doing it, for that half-minute his income is at the rate
of £15,024 per annum, even if he only works eight hours
a day and knocks off on Sundays. Yet I have known
men who didn’t get rich even at that princely rate.
There was too much competition in the business.
• • • •
Dear Bulletin, —“J.F.D.’s” assertion (8.,
11/4/’O3) that there is skill in playing “ two-up ”
is unadulterated bosh. The back country is the
home of the “ two-up ” school and the “ two-uppers.”
Every possible means and way of getting home on
the other fellow is tried and practised by the
bush-spielers during idle time between shearing. They’re
driven to, if possible, acquire skill, not for the mere
love of it, but out of grim necessity, because “nobs,”
“ greys,” and “pigeon-hole kips” and other take-down
paraphernalia are now so well-known as to be almost
entirely played out. “ It’s this cursed Education Act
that does it," once said a well-known out-back spieler.
Have known scores of men trying hard to learn the art of
regulating the spin of the coin for months ; but in vain.
Were it possible at all, these flercely-earnest individuals
would accomplish it. Practising in a room would be
more hopeless than ever. ‘ ‘ Two-up ”is mainly an open-
air game, and there are no ceilings to be guided
by, and the eye alwayt gauges the amount of muscular
strength required for certain actions. Besides, wind and
air-currents would upset the indoor rehearsal. Likewise
“J.F.D.,” in same Bulletin, conveys nothing new or
startling when relating the story of the cards and his
neighbor’s grind-stone. To extract the jacks or any re-
quired cards out of a pack and grind down the edges of
the rest is as old as “The Wrinkler’s’’ great grandfather’s
dog jlie grind-stone of every large shearing shed out-
back could sing that song. If there should be others in
Innocent Sydney besides “ J.F.D.’s ” guileless shopkeeper
who do not know what the thing is done for let them be
enlightened by a bushie. Cards extracted from a pack
during the grinding-process naturally remain a trifle
wider than the rest. In consequence their edges slightly
protrude when the pack is squared on one side. They are
thus easily located and extracted when “stacking” is
necessary to acquire your opponent’s boodle. The
cleverest loaded-dice-men, card-sharpers, two-up spielers,
coin-ringers, “endless-strand” and “ belt-trick’’-men,
thimble-and-pea-gents., and others of that ilk, are met
in the large shearing-sheds out-back where schools are
high and flourishing. It’s a common fallacy to think that
the clever division lives only in the cities. Bush-spielers
have whispered to writer that their easiest prey are the
“ think-they’re-clever-city-fellows.” “J.F.D.” and his
Druitt-street “ heading-’em ” genius, I imagine, would be
fine fat pigeons to pluck at a big Yanko, Dunlop or
Bowen ©owns’ gafflng-sch<M»l.—“ 6 x 8.”
* • • •
A metropolitan softgooda firm held an exami-
nation for of “walker” in its establish-
ment retentljC jChere were between 40 and 50 candidates
—all of them dressed faultlessly. The examination was
conducted in the firm’s board-room, and consisted of tests
in a multitude of movements and brief speeches. Among
the preliminary tests were walking the full length of the
room, bowing to imaginary persons, saying, with a smile
and gentle wave of the band, “ This way, madam,” and
placing chairs for invisible visitors. There were tests in
smiles, in modulations of the voice, in arching the eye-
brows and casting down the eyes. Most candidates
passed in these exercises, and were then confronted
with what were called “specialties.” In these customers
of all grades of temper were imagined, and the candidate
was expected to prove his resourcefulness in most trying
and peculiar positions. And such is the beginning of a
great man of the William M'Millan and Robert Reid
class!
* * • »
Vic. shire official, who combines the clerical
duties of a cabbage-growing shire on the fringe of the
metropolis, with those of poundkeeper, struck a sheaf of
troubles t’other day. In a bad fit of retrenchment the
local council decided to cease taking the Government
Gazette, as a little-read, useless organ. Directly after
this decision two horses were impounded, for trespass,
* nd j? 10 ordinar y course of affairs the secretary-cum-
poundkeeper posted the usual impounding advertisement
to the Government Printer for insertion, as required by
law. But, since the municipal fossils had discontinued
taking the Govt, organ, the day for receiving lost horse
advertisements had been altered ; consequently the
poundkeeper’s notice did not appear till a week later than
intended, and the sale of the two horses which followed
was illegal for lack of sufficient published notice. About
this time the owner of the lost animals—two valuable
polo ponies—traced them, and also traced the illegal sale.
Then there was trouble. It took a £5O bonus to persuade
the buyers to return the horses, and the council sorrow-
fully paid this, though whether it can be legally taken
out of the rates or not hasn’t been ascertained yet. Per-
haps that will mean more trouble. Anyhow the Govern-
ment Gazette has been ordered again.
• * • •
Some suggestive monuments of the boom still
adorn Melbourne suburbs, especially those that Bent
wants to encourage by giving free railway passes to house-
builders. Two such are Blackburn and Glen Iris. Black-
burn mostlycomprises a pub., a smithy, and a crescent.
Latter consists of a dozen gorgeous, two-storey brick
mansions, each in a half-acre allotment. Glen Iris pos-
sesses neither pub. nor smithy, but boasts a gaswork,
and, on a hill beyond, a squad of mansions as before.
These tenements are, and have been for the last 10 or 12
years, occupied by a poverty-stricken, skimping, scraping,
contriving race. The unfortunates bought their places
in the joyous boom days—on the instalment plan—and,
having paid a portion, were loth to relinquish when the
spree collapsed. They are now mostly getting their titles
—just as the mansions show signs of senile decay—and
they have paid from £lOOO to £2OOO for properties that
are to-day worth barely £5OO. Wherefore they look upon
the new “free pass” builder as a jump-up—almost a
pauper.

Religion up-to-date : The Scientific American,
of Feb. 21st., in its list of “ Recently Patented Inven-
tions,” includes the following : “ Chaplet and Shrine of
the Holy Rosary.— T. Sault, New Haven, Conn. The
purpose of the inventor is to here provide a new and im-
proved chaplet and shrine of the holy rosary designed for
devotional purposes, and arranged to enable a person to
successively display pictures of a religious character one
at a time and in proper order, according to the designated
devotional exercise.”
