“ Scotty the Wrinkler” to “J.F.D.”: You’re
at it again, are you? And “ you know a man and have
seen him practise for hours,” &c. Well, whether lam
flush or not is no matter. I’ll stake £5 down with any
appointee of The Bulletin [The Bulletin hereby ap-
points Rev. Dill-Macky], to be made £lO on naming of
date and hour of trial, that “ J.F.1).” does not produce
the man he mentions, to go within an inch of a 10ft.
ceiling 19 times out of 20 — i.e., without touching. I’ll be
generous: I’ll make it 18. And if “ J.F.D.” lays odds
I’ll make it 15 and 2 inches. I shall come to Sydney,
even risking my life at the corner of Druitt-street, any
day, from one to three months from now; and, as the
apparatus can’t lie, my camera or “ J.F.D.’s ” will record
the shots. And the eight times running, heading or
tailing, is a palpable lie, just as “ Temora Tommy’s ” was,
unless (always an “if ”) the Druitt-street man is an idiot,
or of independent means. I bet he hasn’t decent boots,
and I state that he is a fool to show his hand, just as
Ginger used to show his game at billiards. Your rabbit-
ing man never came that tenner to clear 640 acres by a
bag of pollard and two bobs’-worth of poison. But if
“ J.F.D.” wants to make a quid or two, I’ll pay his and
Druitt Cornerman’s fare and board up here if they’ll let
me edit them, and I’ll make £5O out of them as a show —
i.e., if they can do their trick, and if they will recoup
me should they fail.
UNEMPLOYED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The early bird, awak’ning ere the dawn has lit the blue,
Awaits the tardy urchin who conveys the paper round ;
Then, hustling through his humble meal, he scans the
column through
Which specifies where work, or something similar, may
be found.
His patched and saggy seediness is roused from its
despair
By cautious brush and artful fake and titivated sham ;
And tattered crease-worn ref’rences are gathered up with
care
To share the handy pocket with the mid-day bread and
jam.
The morning sheet is pencil-ticked in likely-looking spots,
Increasing hesitation as to which he’ll tackle first;
For memory is counting o’er the many painful lots
Of pilgrimages made so oft with unsuccess accurst.
Selecting one to start with, off he rushes straight to where
A hungry crowd dejected swells increasingly anon ;
And residents adjacent gaze and spitefully declare
There’s certainly a murder or an auction going on.
A weary string along the kerb, a throng around the door—
Their number is adjacent unto fifty-six or so
Assorted types of manhood, with additions more and
more,
Like flies in steamy weather when the meat begins to go.
Some plunge in tangled arguments on hazy politics,
Old mutilated maxims are with new confusion fraught;
They know where all the pegs are loose—know all the
little tricks,
And what some legislators did, and didn’t do, and ought.
He takes his turn in single file, which gradually brings
Him to the sanctum where the boss awaits the interview—
The boss who asks in accents full of wrath—and other
things—
If this procession’s got an end, and when that end is due.
The brief examination ; how he struggles to impress
The fact that he is all he represents himself to be ;
The same old dead finale—“ Yes, well leave me your
address,
And if you suit I’ll let you know.” Alas, it’s all U.P. 1
But time is on the wing—both wings ; so off he strides
and hunts
For other jobs ; while thinking what a chance he might
expect
If he could only spread himself round everywhere at once,
Like something in a riddle that he can’t quite recollect.
Then out beyond and back again, down here and over
there,
To find in each successive visit disappointment more—
Some lucky wretch installed. Until he’s painfully aware
The widely-scattered crumbs have all been swallowed
hours before.
With appetite developed that he’d rather do Without
He homeward crawls; while ghostly hopes in sympathy
still cling :
To-morrow may be kinder—yes, well that is just about
The daily summing-up of life. What will to-morrow
bring ?
J. DORGAN.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Imagine the Premier of all Australia
addressing the Commonwealth from such a hole as
Dungog, and a hole with such a name !
The British War Office is doing something
for its returned soldiers. It has formally decided
that it will not supply the address of any one of
them to his creditors !
Some grave charges of want of accuracy
are being made against the Victorian census; and
in one department at least the figures are to be
submitted to a fresh investigation.
The S. Aus. Labor party is building a
“threepenny election fund.” That is good. But
to build a really national and comprehensive plat-
form would be better. The funds would come
then.
After all, the idea of filling the Jew-
hannesburg mines with Chinese labor has not been
dropped. An agent is in China at this moment
fathering information as to the possible supply of
luddhists and Confucians.
John Norton, M.P., has been regretting
in his electorate that N.S.W. Parliament “ had
deteriorated since the introduction of payment of
members.” Under the circumstances, the state-
ment seemed to carry its own proof.
ONE OF THE SIGHTS OF SYDNEY.
Seating Accommodation (?) at Public Library.
The Tory reaction in N.S.W. ! Things
that the daily press now hirt at : Repeal of the
Old Age Pensions Act and the minimum wage
regulation ; disfranchisement of the civil service ;
and settlement of Chinese on irrigation areas.
Though Charters Towers (N.Q.) suffers
from a severe outbreak of typhoid fever, some
of its numerous muddle men oppose the taking
of a Referendum to decide whether the filthy and
insanitary cesspits shall be abolished. Proputty
sees a little extra cost looming up when improve-
ment is suggested.
Cabled that the Canadian surplus for last
year is between £2,000,000 and £3,000,000.
Wanted—a leading article apiece from the
Sydney Telegraph and S.M. Herald as to how
it is that Canada and Maoriland, the two highest
tariffed States of the British empire, are also the
most flourishing of all British possessions.
On the Victorian railways nowadays,
cleaners often work as firemen, firemen as drivers,
porters as guards, repairers as gangers, and so on,
but without getting the pay attached to the
higher position. If, however, a fireman is put
temporarily on at cleaning, within three seconds
of his being made a cleaner his pay is put down
from that of a fireman to that of a cleaner. But
this rule doesn’t apply all round. Bent, for
instance, is an M.L.A. temporarily put on to be
a Minister, but he doesn’t draw the pay of a mere
M.L.A. —not much.
The polling for Musgrave (Q.), a typical
Bquatting electorate, where a vacancy lately oc-
curred through the death of Lands Minister
O’Connell, looks as if the Continuous Ministry
were losing ground. In 1893, the Government
majority was 342; in ’96, it had a walk-over; in
’99, its majority was 250; in 1902, 209; in 1903,
only 29.
“ Bobrid ” writes :
The Australian Government must take over the old-age
pension business. T’other day met an aged man who
arrived in Victoria 53 years ago. Ten years ago he came
to N.S.W. with £3OOO to invest in land. The drought
left him without a cent. Too old to work, he cannot now
claim a pension in N.S.W., because he hasn’t been 25
years in N.S.W., and because he has been 10 years in
N.S.W., he cannot get assistance from a State where
he lived over 40 years !
Up North it is better and cheaper to be a
colored alien (when you are murderously inclined)
than to be a common white baker leaving your
employer without due notice. At Cairns, N.Q.,
a riotous Japanese, who had split a white man’s
head open in a row and was a notorious brawler,
fot fined £5 and costs. In Brisbane, same week, a
aker, who left his employer at 24 hours’ notice,
had to pay £7 or go to gaol.
The “knowing” aspirant for a portfolio
“in the next Ministry” in N.S.W. now takes
care that the daily papers shall interview him
when there is a “ political crisis.” The half-dozen
or so men who are privileged by the reporters to
talk at large on the sins of the Government on
such occasions become naturally known to the
public as the leading men of the Opposition, and
are just as naturally sent for when a Ministry is
being formed.
At the half-yearly meeting of the Per-
petual Trustees’ Association, held the other day
in Melbourne, old Bill Zeal almost wept at the
alleged way the income-tax was “ driving money
out of the country.” Query: Does the British
income-tax, which is heavier than any income-tax
in Australia, drive money out of Britain ? If not,
why doesn’t it ? Is Britain burst ? If it isn’t,
why isn’t it ? Will these poor, whining persons
kindly explain T
“ San ”
on the
population
question—
if there is
one:
It is of no
use to discuss
the problem
of the restric-
tion of popu-
lation while
economic
c o n d i t i ons
remain as
they are. The
worker (who
is, of course,
by far the
most prolific
source of
population)
knows that
if he brings
up a large
famil/, each
member of
that family
can be used,
in youth, to
compete with
him, and re-
du c e his
value in the
labor market.
It is also use-
less to take
the high
ground with
him that
every child is
a valuable
asset to the
State; it
might be so
under a bet-
ter social ar-
rangement,
but at pre-
sent every
child is, up
to a certain
age, a dead
weight on his
s h o u 1 d e rs,
and after-
wards an
active indus-
trial enemy.
What wonder
then that the
working man
is beginning
to have smaller families. It has long been an axiom
of political economists that the law of supply and
demand is the only one governing the labor market.
Well, the working man says he is going to regulate the
supply. There are, no doubt, grave reasons against such
regulation, but immediate self-interest appeals strongly
to everyone, and to the worker it looks like a matter of
self-preservation.
It is expected in S.A. that there will be a
considerable inrush of tenders for the construction
of the Ninety-Million-Aore-Steal private railway
to Port Darwin. The Bulletin has some doubts,
but if there is an inrush of tenders it will probably
be followed by a violent rush of the tenderers to
get out again when they find that the promise of
ten years’ exemption from taxes is a mere confi-
dence trick, seeing that the State of S.A., which
makes the promise, has no power whatever to bind
the Commonwealth Parliament not to levy taxes
on the syndicate.
Federal Treasurer George Turner, giving
the “ straight griffin ” to the State Governments :
We are not going on to the London market to raise
£10,000,000 or £12,000,000 to hand over to the States, and
are certainly not going to hand over Commonwealth
bonds for works which were practically constructed out
of loan money. If we gave the States bonds, there is
nothing in the world to prevent any needy Treasurer,
instead of going on to the London market and borrowing
for new works, simply putting our Commonwealth stock
on the market and selling it. So far as the Common-
wealth Government is concerned, payment by either
cash or bonds is absolutely out of the question. I would
not agree to it under any circumstances.
BENT AND THE BIVALVE.
Vic. railway employees refuse to separate from
Melb. Trades’ Hall at the dictation of T. Bent.
TOMMY BENT: “Out you come!"
THE WINKLE: “Not if I can help it. I’m not
eager to be eaten.”
The National Ass. of S.A. will make a
great effort to shift Senator McGregor at next
elections. McGregor would have gone down to
Tasmania to help in the State elections but that
he has been unwell. His blindness rendered it
necessary for him to be constantly in his place in
the Senate during the last long session, for he
cannot read the debates, and the trying ordeal
told considerably on his strength.
The publicans who supplied the late Tommy
Moore, of Ramsay’s Bush, with the liquor which,
allegedly, led to a gruesome murder, explain that
they are not amenable in this matter to the local
liquor laws. The prohibition as to the selling of
liquor in N.S. Wales to aborigines applies only
to natives of N.S. Wales. Tommj was a Queens-
land aborigine, and, if sober, could demand
whisky, same as any “ white pfeller.”
Dry Dog Reid, in his recent speech at
Deniliquin, urged that the Immigration laws
should not be enforced against people from
England for fear we might aggravate a country
that we want to borrow money from. We are
Setting down pretty low in the matter of servility.
ome day, when Bull will lend us no more, and
we are trying to get loans in Paris, Dill-Macky
will have to turn Roman Catholic in order to
pacify our new creditor.
As the result of another controversy as to
N.S.W. Labor party secret caucus, Arthur
Griffith discovers that the difference between the
Labor party and the Carruthers party method is
this: —
On any important point the L.P. holds a secret caucus
and after discussion instructs its leader how to act.
In the same circumstances Mr. Oarruthers decides how
he is going to act and then holds a secret caucus of his
party to affirm or reject his idea. If they reject it, pre-
sumably he abandons it.
Forecast ot the Federal election results by
a man who thinks he knows:—Westralia, things
will be about as they are; the Labor party may
win another seat. Tasmania, ditto. South Aus-
tralia, the Government may lose one seat. Vic-
toria, the Government will hold its own ;
the Labor party should win two seats. N.S.W.,
the Government may lose two seats to the Oppo-
sition. Queensland, the Labor party will pro-
bably win two extra seats and the Government
one extra.
The Tasmanian Government has been
‘ ‘ approached ” by an American syndicate which
says in a shadowy way that it is willing to invest
from £loo,oooto £500,000 in the island “on con-
dition of receiving from 500,000 acres to 1,000,000
acres of land.” For really first-class cheek this
reads like a record. The present writer would do
as much as that. He would undertake to invest
(for his own profit) £lO,OOO in Sydney on condition
of receiving the General Post Office —land, build-
ings and all.
The tendency of the affluent Australian
Tory to lean abjectly on the State was curiously
illustrated the other day. In the old, settled
Pennant Hills district, near Sydney, where the
fruit-growers range from comfortable to really
well off, flying foxes are troublesome, so these
fine, solid landowners decided to ask the Govern-
ment for cartridges to shoot them with. The
“backbone of the country” will soon be asking
for a State subsidy to enable it to stamp on its
own caterpillar.
N.S.W. Cabinet seems to be taking its
usual cowardly, shuffling, yes-no attitude over the
Wyalong sectarian row. There was something
very like a riot at Wyalong through the fostering
of militant sectarianism, and a score or so of the
actual offenders (alleged) were arre&ted and fined
ss. each with costs. Now the O’See Ministry has
suspended collection of the fines whilst it considers
the question of remitting the costs ; and it pro-
bably will remit those costs. Thus there will be
a sop to all parties. The Protestant sectaries
will be grateful for a prosecution of the other
fellow without any attempt at sheeting home to
them their share of the trouble ; and the “ other
fellow ” —when the State pays the costs —will be
oomforted with the thought that he had his fun
cheap, anyhow.
The Maoriland Cold Tea crowd
is apparently going to fall in. The
trouble arises over a number of
Local Option polls which were de
clared void by reason of informality'
lhe law makes no provision for
holding a second Local Option poll
if the regular one is declared void
It says that no new licences shall be
granted till the people have decided
at the poll, either for reduction
abolition, or for leaving things as
they are ; and when the poll is de
dared void the people don’t decide
any of these things—therefore no
licences can be granted. The law
also says that if none of the three
proposals—reduction, abolition or
the leaving of things as they ar’e-is
carried by the requisite majority the
existing licences shall remain’and
when the poll is declared void none of
these propositions gets the requisite
majority, so all the existing licenses
must be granted again. Being faced
by these two irreconcilable proposi-
tions, the Crown Law officers decide
that licences remain as they are till
next poll three years hence, and un-
less this decision is reversed Beer
wins, with Cold Tea a bad second.
Australian politicians occasion-
ally say hard things of each other
but they seldom reach the height of a
recent orator in the Hungarian Par-
liament when he called the Premier “a
hangman, a murderer, and a bloodhound.”
Where are the stalwarts who used to grace
the Australian military before the Boer raiding ’
At the Vic. encampments it was remarked that
quite a different stamp of “men” were now
presenting themselves for training. Taken all
round, a weedier lot of striplings had never been
seen except at a cadets’ camp. Some of the more
solid class fell in the war; others learnt the arts
of stealing and loafing; the remainder, presum-
ably, are disgusted with the sort of thing they
met with “ out there,” by oourtesy called War.
A Maoriland correspondent :
On the eve of the last Maoriland general election it was
announced in certain newspapers that Cardinal Moran
had declared, while in Ireland, that Premier Seddon had
shown the Irish leaders how to solve the land problem
and that instead of disloyal attacks on the Empire they
should copy the vast wisdom of King Dick. Now
when the British Government proposes to tax the
people of Ireland in order to buy off one set of land
monopolists in order to create another, Maoriland is told
through the leading columns of certain Seddonian news-
papers that Bun Tuck’s advice has had the desired
effect—in other words Seddon has shown the Irish how to
deal with the land problem. Seddon has posed as many
things. Now he poses as the regenerator of Ireland
among the rest.
Another week finds the Victorian Govern-
ment still wondering what to do next as regards
the railway workers. It ordered them to sever
their connection with the Trades Hall, and did it
so forcibly that it must take some notice of their
refusal. To sack them all is impossible, and to
make a general cut in wages by way of punish-
ment would be a declaration of war against trades-
unionism in general, and that would be a very
large order. The Irvine Ministry has several times
been sorry it spoke, or, rather, it has been sorry
that Bent spoke. This time it is so sorry that it
wishes Bent were dead.
Melb. Argus brays loudly for “discip-
line” in the Railway Department, and refuses to
print letters from disgusted railway employees,
on the plea that, in voicing their grievances, the
employees are infringing a regulation forbidding
them to seek redress in any other way than
through their superior officers. But the same
paper prints columns of letters from teachers bur-
lesquing the Director’s new programme, and
makes these letters the basis for a leading article,
in which it expresses its deep sympathy with a
small minority of unprogressive cranks, who are
also infringing the regulation.
It doesn’t look pretty for a State Treasurer
to be branded as a spreader of slander, and that is
what has happened to Raven Shiels, of Victoria.
He stated in the House lately that various old age
pensioners at Smythesdale spent their pensions in
drink, and in a violent attack of just indignation
he cut off the supply and ordered their claims to
be re-heard. The result was that in every case
the Ballarat P.M. confirmed the pensions, it being
shown that all the tanglefoot the old folks got was
a very occasional beer shouted by a friend. But
for “privilege” these old people would have a
good libel action against the Raven.
Mr. Spruce Myth seems to think that
women will swallow any amount of ponderous
inanity. Slab from a recent lecture of his:
Women must learn to disconnect the heart from the
head in creating ' enforcing legal rights and liabilities
by Act of Pari! nt. The heart and the emotions were
designed for higher and nobler work, and ought not to
be dragged into the political mire.
There is surely nothing more important in civic
life than the exercise of the franchise, and why
should the “high” and “noble” capacities be
kept out of that work? And why should Mr.
Spruce Myth refer to the field out of which he
gets his living as the “political mire?”

Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Dear Sir,—
I have very great pleasure in testifying to the value of
VITADATIO as a cure for Hydatids. I was taken very
ill towards the end of 1899, and found it necessary to call
in a doctor. He, after carefully examining me, stated
that I had Hydatids, gave me medicines to take which did
me no good, and at last I was informed that I would have
to undergo an operation before I could be cured. This. I
was determined not .to do, and decided to immediately
commence a course of VITADATIO. I commenced it in
January, 1900, and I was then in a very weak and low
state, and I am very pleased to say that after taking it
regularly for three months I was completely cured. I
have not taken any VITADATIO or other medicine since
April of the same year. I hope that others by reading
this testimonial may profit by it, and I would say to those
who take it that after taking eight bottles I felt much
worse than I had done for some time and was advised to
discontinue its use and again call in a doctor, but I am
thankful that I continued with the VITADATIO, and
found after the eighth bottle, each one I took made me
feel much better, until at last I was completely cured.
You may make use of this letter if you wish.
Yours truly,
CATHERINE F. SMITH.

A CURIOUS MISTAKE.
DESPITE the advanced teachings of The
Bulletin, many of its readers are like the
old woman who only listened to one side of an
argument, because she could make up her mind
easier. Now don’t be like the old woman, and
make the curious mistake of thinking that,
because Queensland is smaller than the Southern
she cannot compete successfully with
them; B.ut b o f°re making up your mind, write
to CHARGES Gilbert, the leading Cash Tailor,
Brisbane, for patterns of New Winter Suitings
(including a special line of soft twill finish pure
ludigfi Blue Serge at 50s.—the suit to measure,
carriage paid) and Self - Measurement Forms.
Then compare the values. You will think
differently.
If you are not wise, be pompous ; if you
are not witty, be malicious; if you are not learned,
be tedious; if you are not well-informed, be dog-
matic—most people will never know the difference.

.Noble Wilson, who died at Ballarat re-
cently, aged 72 years, went to the Statued City in
1854, and remained there until his death. He was
many things, including photographer, journalist,
land and estate agent and founder of the Ballarat
Trustees, Executors and Agency Co., of which he
was managing director at his death. He never
boasted of having been in the Eureka Stockade
trouble, December, 1854, but he was a full private
in that disturbance.
King O’Malley is posing under his huge
advertisement-hat as the champion of poor dear
alleged Senator Reid. That pompous softgoods-
man, as K.O’M. pictures him, is “held legally
responsible for the technical blundering of his
employees, but nevertheless is known to be in-
ca,pable of doing anything which could in the
slightest degree reflect upon his honor.” King
O’Malley gazes in admiration at the martyr Reid,
who, “ starting as a boy, poor and friendless, has
successfully climbed the highest peak of the com-
mercial mountain. This is sufficient to account
for the envy, hatred and malice of some who
strive to blacken his character while not fit to
blacken his boots.” Looks as though O’Malley
had bought his big hat in Reid’s shop at a great
reduction. This yearning to blast the character
of Robert Reid’s untried junior clerk, as a mis-
creant who habitually did large swindles for his
boss’s profit without his boss’s knowledge, is a des-
picable kicking of an obscure person who is
possibly an orphan.
It has been discovered by the lawyers,
who are always making useless or obnoxious dis-
coveries like that, that the N.S. W. Early Closing
Act may be made null and void because, though
it orders a man to close his shop at a certain hour,
it doesn’t order him not to re-open it immediately.
Now, a somewhat similar hole has been found in
the Vic. Factories Act; it orders that waiters
may not be worked more than so many hours per
week, but doesn’t mention waitresses, and a court
in the partially-dead town of Geelong has decided
that, therefore, waitresses are not protected. Yet
the iaw against stealing a horse ropes in the man
who steals a mare right enough.
S.A. State Treasurer Butler made a most
disastrous remark the other day :
Probably (said Mr. Butler), no one knew better, than
Mr. Edmund Barton that it would have been simply im-
possible for the States to have paid for public works out
of revenue.
There are two ways of paying for public works
out of revenue—by building them out of revenue,
or by building them with loans and afterwards
paying off the loans out of revenue. S.A. Trea-
surer Butler says that both things are im-
possible. Does Treasurer Butler think he is
doing any good to Australia by stating that it
can’t pay its debts? Also, how far does he
reckon this sort of language is separated from a
declaration of bankruptcy ?

The Inky Way.
Some reporters are absolutely devoid of humor. For
instance, the Age (Melb.) recently sent a man to “ do ” a i
mining catastrophe, and he sent down his copy with the
final head line :
ODOR OF DECOMPOSING BODIES.
[FROM OUR OWN REPORTER.]
• • * •
Maoriland has developed something new in boycotting.
From the Taihape Post : “ In consequence of the ungen-
tlemanly conduct of Mr. S , exhibited at a social, in
abusing the editor of the Post, when entrusted with the
privilege of responding to the toast of ‘ The Press,’ we
have decided that in future this paper will not publish a
report of any function or meeting which Mr. S is con-
sidered good enough to attend."
• • • •
The Bulletin known now why John Norton is going to
the West. Into his hands has evidently fallen what is
labelled “ a journal of bright, chatty literature,” and he
wants to see the man who runs it. The Bulletin gets
only three pages of it ; but even in that small space are
packed the following bright, pleasant, chatty remarks
regarding a Perth citizen:—“Ratty . . . destructive mon-
ster . . . unscrupulous . . . impudent, absurd . . . self-
adulator . . . vicious . . . wave of infamy . . . syco-
phantic, fawning, political imbecile . . . silly little
bantam . . . your cut out of the blood-money was one
hundred . . . dodger . . . poltroon . . . liar . . . whited
sepulchre . . . impudent sausage . . . apoplectic swine.”
Then the purveyor of this bright, chatty literature
remembers his own “ too generous nature,” and passes on
to make a few benevolent remarks about a certain
“ Fenian cat ” mayor.
* • * *
Something like a record mixing up of things is this
from a Gisborne (M.L.) newspaper, which ran together a
cablegram about Hector Macdonald and one about how
Edward VII's moke, Ambush, didn’t win the Grand
National through falling at the last fence, and produced
these results :—
“London, March 28.
“ The Daily News declares that there is a feeling in
Edinburgh that the Crofter soldier (General Macdonald)
had* been huuted down by a big, systematic society
attack, and that in the ‘ Grand National Ambush ’ he fell
at the last fence, when badly ‘ winded.’ The King
witnessed the race.”
Then the Gisborne editor spoke with grave severity after
this fashion :—
“ It was a well-known fact that General Macdonald
cared not for the silliness of so-called society, and he had
a wholesale contempt for that class which faw'ns on a
name, and it is quite possible that this contempt did lead
him into trouble. But it cannot for a moment be believed
that an ambush was deliberately laid for the purpose of
luring him to destruction. The statement that the King
was an onlooker, too, stamps the tale as absurd, for what-
ever may be the faults of the King they do not run in the
direction of seeing his best officers sacrificed at the behest
of fashion’s favorites.”

Philip Wirth, the circus-man, tells of how he experi-
enced a great shock of joy in a little, one-horse Argentine
town when a touring Britishah produced a red-covered
journal and said, “ Like to read The Bulletin ? Beastly
clevah Austwalian papah.” In all the larger towns of
South America it is easy to get The Bulletin.—Advt.
* • • •
It is by a mere accidental shuffle of cards that the
editorship of S.M. Herald goes to a layman. The Rev.
Arthur Galton, now a polemical rector in Windermere,
England, then a secessionist from the house of Duff, had
the chair under offer during a weak health interval in
the career of Rev. Curnow. Gurnow revived and Galton
departed. By the way, Curnow was entitled to a pension
under the Act which abolished State aid to religion, but
the pension is paid contingent on the cleric sticking to
his church duties. If he did not accept a pension from
the Herald, some lawyers hold that parson Curnow
could go back on the clerical pension fund.

Stated that an exalted naval officer of the
Australian squadron stopped, with a frown, on
encountering a blue-jaclcet squirming abjectly on
the deck with both hands clasped over his solar
plexus. “Damme! what’s the matter?” he de-
manded. “I do-o-n’t like to say, sir,” faltered
the boy of the bulldog breed. “ What’s that ? ’
roared the officer; “answer me at once. What
are you making those grimaces for?” Between
his groans the sailor replied, “Something the
matter with mybowels, sir.” “Bowels! bowels!”
cried the exalted one, indignantly. “ Bowels are
for the officers. Yours are guts 1 ”
THE FORLORN AUTHOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met a long, gaunt, shiv’ring being ;
So deep his woe, my heart’s core bled ;
His feet’s en_..sements’ base had fled,
Nor did his batter’d hat his head
Prevent the weeping cloud from seeing.
When sympathy on’s plight had touched,
“ I am an author, sir,” he said,
And aching were his eyes of lead ;
He heavily sigh’d and stayed his tread,
Then spake, and at his vitals clutched—
“ Sweet youth was mine, and exultation
That did unto my brain a seat
Among immortals give 1 yet meat
Years past I’ve lack’d : all journals greet
My screed, but pay—on publication 1"
N.B.W.
VOLOAN.


A Groundless fear.
A Woman Afraid of Herself.
Walking along a country road in the dark,
one often imagines that every tree or post is
some human being bent on mischief. Sometimes
one is afraid to even look back, so fixed has the
idea become that someone is stealthily following
them with intent to do harm. The darkness of
the night and loneliness of the way is accountable
for such a state. But what can be imagined of a
woman who, during all the day, was afraid of
even the noise made by her own footsteps?
Nervous, irritable, and depressed ; afraid to move,
fearing to see even those nearest and dearest to
her. Such was once the condition of Mrs. Lillie
Johnston, of 142 Crown-street, Sydney, before
she was restored to perfect health by Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. "‘My nerves were entirely
run down,” she says, “and everything I wished
to do seemed a trouble. In neither my home or
surroundings could I take an interest. Indiges-
tion first brought on my illness. From this
rapidly followed loss of appetite, accompanied by
frequent sick headaches. The slightest exertion
completely prostrated me. I became low spirited,
depressed and irritable, and my life was indeed a
burden. At seeing the most harmless things I
would start with fear, and oft I would be put into
a complete tremble for nothing at all. I attended
a hospital but their treatment was of no avail. A
friend suggested I should try Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. Before I had taken many my condi-
tion began to improve marvellously. With their
continued use I became completely cured._ I am
now a happy woman in the brightest spirits and
the best health. My work is a pleasure to me,
and I am only too pleased to testify to all the
wonderful good Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
have done me.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
are a perfect blood purifier, and a positive cure for
biliousness, indigestion, constipation, headaches,
sallow complexion, liver and kidney troubles,
piles, pimples and blotches, and for female
ailments. Put up in amber bottles and the full
name blown thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
How long is this farce of closing the betting-
shops going to continue? The proprietors now simply
stand outside and carry on their business as before. The
punter suffers, of course, for he has a difficulty in seeing
lists of scratchings, and the average "Mo" has no
unworthy scruples about laying “dead ’uns."
• • • •
There is a curious trouble over the distribution
of the lOOsovs. set aside for the breeder of the winner of
the Onkaparinga Steeple, S.A. Mr. Wm. Crozier, to
whom the lucre was popularly supposed to belong, won't
claim it, and says it should go to JR. Barr Smith|; while
O. G. M'Mahon, who originally picked out The Drummer
as a promising animal, says his idea is that A. H. Pegler
was the breeder.
• • • •
At Charters Towers’ bicycle sports on Good
Friday, Yellerman got home again in the William
Goat Race, beating Baden Powell and a select field.
A bumping race brought four goats down. Yellerman, a
veritable Carbine among the racing goats of the north, is
trained privately, and is always backed with great confi-
dence by the stable. On one occasion a well-known
bookmaker was persuaded to open a book on the goat
race. ** What price Stinker ? ” said small boy with
cigarette. _ “ Four to one." “ Right—four half-crowns."
“ What price Stinker ? ” “ Four to one.” “ Right ; four
bob to one me.” Clerk kept busy booking bets against
Stinker, finishing up with the owner, who languidly took
the odds to half a quid. Nothing else was backed, and
when Stinker baa-a-ed in on his own, bookie’s feelings
got the better of him. “Only one dashed g-g-goat in
this race besides Stinker," he stuttered —“and that’s
nu I Even the dashed b-boys t-t-ake me down."
• • • •
Long succession of M.L. turfite G. G. Stead’s
appropriations of O. J.O. Champagne Stakes was broken
by Treadmill’s win, t’other day, and same owner (Geo.
Clifford) “ got home ” with Windwhistle and Quarryman
same day. For 10 years previously C.J.C. Champagne
Stakes had been closed on by Mr. Stead, who has won
it 13 times altogether.
• * • *
Development last week of Newcastle race-
course scale-manipulation scheme was discovery, a
few days later, of a similar swindle on Wallsend
racecourse. Floor of weighing-room there had been
laid little more than two weeks previously. Method was
exactly the same as at Newcastle excepting that a 21£lb.
bag of shot took the place of the lead. Sensation was
intensified, Saturday, by the arrest and charging on sus-
picion of trainer and owner (Kingsley) and Rider (Moran)
of nag Gentleman Jim, who won the Pace Welter at
Newcastle, Monday week. Prior case to that at Moore-
field occurred at Brisbane, not at Warwick Farm, as
previously stated.
• • • •
Winner (Airloch) of City Tattersall’s Youthful
Stakes at Randwick, Saturday, beat a field of 16, and
started 8 and 10 to 1. Why she wasn’t a hot favorite is a
mystery, for her load (8.3) was much lighter than thatwhich
she easily carried through at Moorefield a month or so
since.
* • • •
Announced that imported Lacrosse threw a
fine colt foal to English sire Martagon at Motto Farm
(Newcastle) t’other day. This youngster is of the bluest
of blue blood. Lacrosse is a beautifully formed mare, by
Derby winner Ladas from St. Simon-Luoy Cross. Mar-
tagon is full brother in blood to the greatest nag
(Ormonde) the world has known, and is by Bend ’Or from
Tiger Lily—full sister to Lady Agnes, the dam of
Ormonde.

“It’s an ill wind,” &c. Insurance money paid
through destruction of its grandstand by fire put
Murrumbidgee Race Club very much in funds; it
squared up with the bank and still has £4OO of the
amount left.
* • • •
City Tatt.'s meeting at Randwick wasn’t a
success from any point of view, owing to rain falling in
the morning and threatening till after the programme
opened. Seventy-two of 130 entrants failed to turn up.
Four of the five races listed were soft for the winners,
and a good percentage of those most fancied by the
public were palpably not over eager to win. Outsider
Ambulance secured the big thing (Tatt.’s Handicap)
easily, with equally long-priced Silver Forest second, and
favorite Naphro third. Only one first choice got home,
and he, Tatterdemalion (by Lochiel out of Tatters, and
consequently fully related to Old Clo, a good performer
here and in England), started heavily backed for Club
Welter, and fairly romped away from his field, leaving
them at the finish as if they were standing.
• * • •
“Le Chasse”: Freedom’s fall in the Steeple-
chase at Randwick (N.S.W.) was due to his shying at the
yelling crowd sitting on the rail near the jump. Some
years ago I lost a good stake, and went near to parting
with my life also in the Mudgee Town Plate through a
fool-headed woman suddenly opening a flaming red
gingham right under my horse’s nose as we led into the
home turn. That part of the course should be kept
perfectly clear, and anyone found trespassing there
jugged for attempted manslaughter.

J. Barden, W. H. Smith and A. Richardson
each rode three winners at the V.R.O. Autumn meeting.
N.S.W. is well supplied with jockeys just now.

Seahorse, the handsome Maorilander that was
expected to do great things in England, has won a
maiden hurdle race.
* • * •
In a Reefton (M.L.) hurdle race five started.
Two fell, one breaking its neck ; then the first and second
horses were disqualified on technicalities, and as the
third could not weigh in, it was declared no race.
* * * •
Tote investments at South Australia’s popular
Onkaparinga meeting:—lB99, £24,577 ; 1900, £22,705 ;
1901, £22,062; 1902, £22,417; 1903, £21,228. Which
completely knocks out the ancient superstition that,
once the tote was established, the poison would creep
and crawl through the whole State until in a few years
it would spend its last copper and pawn its last hymn-
book to bet on the accursed machine. In S. Aus. there
practically isn’t any agitation against the tote now.
• * * •
Old C. Hutchins, who steered the Assyrian to
victory in the Melbourne Cup 23 years ago, in a terrific
storm, thereby upsetting the pot of many a punter, has
taken to riding ponies at Melb. suburban unregistered
meetings. A short time ago he effected a coup on Sweet
Agra, a rank outsider, winning the “Newmarket”
Handicap by a head.
• • • •
“ Dagonet ” Sims says an association has been
formed in England with large capital to operate on the
turf by the Marconi system.
* * • •
“Bias ” : Was much amused at the big family
picnic Victorian representative bowling team made of
their recent visit to Sydney—they brought along ladies
and children representing three generations, and all
swarmed to dinners, picnics, drives and the like, and at
the entertaining N.S.W. Bowling Association’s and clubs’
expense. Victorian bowling teams have made it a prac-
tice for years. Strange to say, N.S.W. bowlers never
“get back” by swamping Victoria similarly when it is
their turn to visit.
• • • •
George Giffen, who as far back as 26 years ago
was at the head of the S. Aus. Cricket Association batting
list, finishes up this season with third place; also, barring
only Travers, he took the greatest number of wickets.
Seven times in the past 26 years he has headed the Asso-
ciation batting list, and he has been at the head of the
bowling list as often, though not in the same seasons.
• • • •
J. Noel, who has been in S. Australian senior
cricket almost as long as George Giffen, has probably
seen his last active season. This year he has gone back
six times without scoring, and he hasn’t been able to
get any wickets either.
The recent visit of (absent) Lord Hawke's team
to Maoriland was a huge boodle-raking advt. The differ"
ent members of the team —and particularly Captain
Warner—unearthed a “ born ” cricketer in almost every
village, and the undisguised hint was thrown out that the
gifted one, with coaching, would develop into a world
paralyser. Thus, as a sequel, Maoriland is threatened
with a plethora of professional coaches to teach all and
sundry the .intricacies of the game. Also the team
boasted of a few would-be journalists, and the sport-loving
book-worm will be afflicted with a dozen books entitled
“ Cricket in Australasia."
* • • •
Whilst in Australia the Association and League
football threatens this season to absorb publio interest,
in Maoriland it is the opposite. In the latter place the
Association game is falling very flat and leading players
are joining the ranks of the Rugbyites. The inter-
state fixtures have a deal to do with that; for while,
on the one band, there’s always a trip to Australia every
few years sticking out for a really good Rugbyite, the
Association player must still show his prowess to his own
countrymen. In N.S.W. the newly-introduced Australian
game is knocking lumps off the Rugby crowd in the
matter of management, and the proposal to have a num-
ber of paid “ field umpires ’’ should ensure for the Aus-
tralian game that pith which has always been lacking in
the opposition branch of the sport.
• * • •
The proposal of Maoriland Rugby Union to send
a team of 25 to Britain next year is strenuously opposed
by Rowland Hill, the sec. to the English Rugby Union.
Hill suggests a smaller team, and at least five matches in
a fortnight. A purely English tour is estimated to yield
£2OOO for the Maorilanders, but, if the tour were ex-
tended to Scotland, Ireland and Wales, more would
probably accrue. M.L. is chswing the matter over.
• • • •
In Christchurch (M.L.) a club has been formed
to play Victorian Association football; the Rugbyites go
down in shoali to see what kind of an animal it is, and to
pass disparaging remarks, and proffer kind atttentions
with the feeding bottle.
• • • •
Maoriland is in the throes of Prohibition. It
is everywhere—and Otago Rugby Union has caught the
infection so badly that “no hotel advertisements, or
advertisements relating to the sale of alcoholic liquor”
shall henceforth appear in the “Annual” published by
the Union.
* • • •
“ Uppercut ” on the solar plexus : It is a spot
which should be barred in prize-fighting on account of
its sacred associations. Holy Writ is eloquent on the
“ bowels of mercy, compassion," etc., and the yearning
thereof. The ancient anatomists called it the S.P.
because they regarded it as the sun of the body. African
savages regard it as the vital spot; the Chinese declare
that it is the seat of the soul. The chief M’Tse said to
Stanley before his particular Nosey Bob started the day’s
work that the killing hurt his belly, and later on he in-
formed the Buccaneer of the Congo that the same part
of his anatomy was torn with sorrow at the death of his
favorite wife. M’Tse was right. It is the place where
the good things of life are appreciated, and the centre of
emotion, and in the Fat Man’s anatomy takes equal place
with the “ bullion.”
* * * •
Result of fight between Cripps and soldier
Thompson for 11.4 (middleweight) supremacy of the con-
tinent, at Sydney National Sporting Club last week, was
a big set-back for most people. Betting ruled sto 2 and
2 to 1 on Thompson, the then holder of the title, but,
though gritty and forceful from end to end, he never
looked a likely winner, except in the sixth round. Both
were equally aggressive. Thompson led the right often,
and each time with his left swinging low —leaving himself
open enough for a cart to back in ; but Cripps seemed
content to step out of distance without taking advantage
of the opportunity. Truth is, he didn’t know enough.
While Thompson’s right rarely landed, Cripps’ was sel-
dom unsuccessful. Jolts occasionally staggered the
soldier, and crosses floored him twice. Only grit of the
finest brand brought him to the perpendicular again.
Almost total absence of anything indicating that either
had been punished much was remarkable, and was prob-
ably due to the extra big gloves used. Thompson hadn’t
a mark, while Cripps bled a little from the lower teeth.
Though ’twas a tough, exciting struggle—fast and fierce
every movement—Cripps finished the 20th round a good
winner. He is a cool and undemonstrative fighter, and
this husbanding of his powers accounts for every demand
finding him equal to it.
* • « *
“Pug”: Because Cripps whipped Thompson
he shouldn’t be rated a wonder—he has a lot to learn yet,
and, by appearances, will, if a tutor worthy the name can
be found. Still, tutors are as scarce as high-class fighters
nowadays. Boxing as taught by Larry Foley and Peter
Jackson is as much a lost art in Australia as it is in
England. Thompson’s reputation was mainly built on
his defeat of “ Snowy” Sturgeon, who never beat a first-
class man at any period of his career, and had a long
spell of good time before meeting Thompson. Latter, just
before tackling Sturgeon, fought old Bill Jennings (fat
and slow through being laid aside for years)—when half-
a-dozen times it looked soft for the ancient, had he just
a little strength left to make the most of advantages ;
and Jennings in his prime was never anything to rave
about.
• • » •
Success of latest middleweight champion pug
Oripp3 has been remarkable. In Bananaland, where
pug material isn’t near the quality N.S.W. boasts, he won
an amateur tourney. Sydney men who faced Cripps till
that fight last week with Thompson weren’t first
flighters by long chalks ; his toughest go was with ball
puncher McCarthy when both, Mac particularly, were
badly pummelled and the match declared a draw. Betting
in Cripps’ fights has been mostly on t’other man ; latest
saw odds 5 to 2 on soldier Thompson whose friends
summed it up a good thing for him. Cripps is 23 years
old, muscular, strong and at the top of good health ; he
fights as if it were an ordinary everyday thing and can
take heaps of thumping without showing that it troubles
him. Cripps was prominent as a footballer in Brisbane
and is so in Sydney now. Probable that the new
champion will take on “ Snowy ” Sturgeon and after-
wards, perhaps, heavyweights Scanlan, Doherty, Felix
and even McCall—provided he keeps on winning.
• * • •
Big novice bantam tourney at Sydney Golden
Gate Club induced 64 entrants, and extended over eight
nights, concluding Saturday. Poundage limit, 8.4, was
strictly adhered to. Winner, Martin—a 16-year-old boy
—gave weight to 63 fellow competitors (he scaled little
more than 6.10, fully clothed), and scored through sheer
downright cleverness ; his showing promises well. It is
remindful, to an extent, of ex-waterman pug Johns and
Tim Hegarty—among the cleverest at the bruising game
Australia owns just now.
• • • •
’Murkan bruiser Jim Scanlan and heavyweight
champion Billy M’Call will meet a third time at Sydney
National 5.0., Tuesday next week, for £450 (purse and
stake) and premiership of the continent. There’ll be a
heap of betting on the struggle, with M’Call, of course,
the favorite, but many think the Yankee will turn the
tables this time, and their view finds much support in
the masterly way Scanlan polished off giant woodchopper
Heckenberg a month or so since. Scanlan’s preparation
has been on lines calculated to fit him for M’Call’s force-
ful rushes; he has had daily “rough ups” with the
soldier Thompson brothers and some thewy and brawny
Australian artillerymen. M’Call has prepared as usual,
and is reported fit as ever. It ought to be a fight that
will cause D.T. Sunday-school reporter to faint right
away.
• * • •
Probable M.L. and N.S.W. amateur champion
boxers will contest Australasian supremacy in Sydney
about October, by which time premiers of each country
will be defined in local competitions. Jack Fitzsimmons
(nephew of famous Bob) will be M.L.’s stand-by in heavy-
weight class, and it is anticipated that he’ll give the best
man N.S.W. owns a severe shaking. Fitzsimmons affects
the Yankee crouch now favored by many professionals, and
relies considerably on his remarkable capacity for taking
punishment. M.L. Boxing Association, formed a year or
so since, is making big strides and has thriving branches
in most of the State’s centres. Same body has declared
war against the professional pug. owing to his question-
able methods in the past, and has him shut out from
appearing in any portion of the land. By and bye the
Maoriland B.A. will handle the pro. as well as the
amateur.
• • • *
Ancient head of billiard table makers, Alcock
and Co., was 50 years in Australia last week. He arrived
in Melbourne, April, 1853, and began as a cabinet-maker
and timber merchant. Old man Alcock was responsible
for the coming to Australia of old man Roberts in 1864,
and, later, other crack ’Ome billiardists —including Cook,
Mannock, Dawson, and Roberts, jun.
• • • •
Prevailing impression that greyhounds lose
much weight during a course, especially if it be a trying
one, wasn’t altogether borne out at Kensington C.O. last
week, when dogs were weighed for the first time in Aus-
tralia. Two rounds of Puppy Stakes were decided,
Monday, and every youngster bar two weighed the same
on each occasion. One of the exceptions was ljlbs.
lighter, and t’other 3£lbs. heavier the second time ; latter
was beaten, but nobody apparently evinced the slightest
curiosity regarding his big accumulation of weight during
the spell. Second day of the meet saw some of the dogs
a pound or so heavier, while others were that much
lighter, but the majority carried what they began with.
Winners of the three stakes never varied much. Narrow
Escape weighed 491bs. in the first round, 491bs. in the first
ties, 48flbs. in the second ties, 48£lbs. in the third ties, and
491bs. in the deciding course. Gold Bar scaled 591bs. in
the first round, 59£lbs. in the first ties, 59Jlbs. in the second
ties, 59£lbs. in the third ties, and 58flbs. in the deciding
course. What Odds weighed 611bs. in the first round,
621bs. in the first ties, 61ilbs. in the second ties, 611bs. in
the third ties, and 60jlbs. in the deciding course.
* • • •
Just-created Kensington Coursing Club, at its
opening meet last week, showed how the thing should be
done, and promises to make the game as popular as 'tis
possible to be. Among many acceptable innovations was
Yankee idea of weighing contending hounds. Ikeys
operating were better class than those mostly seen at
coursing meetings because of restrictions and increased
fees exacted, consequently more money was available and
owners could back their dogs to win fairly substantial
amounts. Coin added to each stake, and fact that usual
10 per cent, deduction would not be made from sweeps,
served as additional inducement to enter, with the result
that every event overfilled considerably. Winner(Narrow
Escape) of puppy event is a beauty, exceptionally fast,
and a close worker who, all going well, will gather some
of the season’s big plums. N.E. hails from Victoria, and
comes of an old Australian strain—Young Heyfield by
Heyfield, brother to Hedley. Another Victorian crack
(Gold Bar) won the Opening Stakes like a champion ; he
is a second season dog and only figured in a course once
before—at Moonee Valley back end of last season. Gold
Bar is by Goldsmith from Song Bird. Last year’s
Waterloo Cup winner (Master Licorice) was run out in
the initial round, and so were whilom forefronters Lamp-
lighter and The Hermit.
• • • •
“ J.F.D.”: Amusing to read of pigeon slaugh-
tering records at match between Comte de Neiva and
G.R. MTntosh at N.S.W. Gun Club. Both shooters shot
46 out of 60 birds—a few of the missing eight fell dead
out of bounds. The day was stifling; the birds, packed in
crates from the night before, could not rise, and the
shooters covered them in every instance before they made
an attempt to get wing—in fact, a smart Rocks pushite
could make records as good with blue metal. I have
seen trained pigeons shot at—birds that had been swished
through a dummy trap before being sent to execution,
and 40 per cent, escaped our best shots. Present pigeon
shooting rests on quickness to pull on the right trap and
fire before the bird gets properly to wing. A good second
barrel kill when the bird is properly going shows more
skill.
• » * •
A Parisian sporting paper prints this :—“ Fly
killing is the favorite sport of Australians, and even the
Frenchmen who reside in that country take to it after
seeing a few matches. Teams are chosen, and each
player is allotted a certain space in which to kill the
insects. The man who has the biggest bag at the end of
an afternoon wins a substantial money prize. The record
up to the present is 149 flies in the hour. Seven have
fallen at one swoop.” That scribe’s ignorance of the Aus-
tralian fly is pathetic. When The Bulletin staff and the
staff of Dill Macky’s organ had their last match on Fenian
Hill, many of the contestants killed two barrow-loads of
flies, and the record was a truckful. Any new-chum
jackeroo, when sitting down to Irish stew on green grass
in Queensland, can stiffen 149 flies at one swoop. He
often swallows that many in his breakfast.

There’s trickery in all branches of sport! This
time the “toney” amusement of golf is affected, and
players under N.S.W. club rules on their own links have
shown such superiority over visitors, that the desirability
of providing some means of lessening the effect of the
local knowledge, and adopting a new method of scoring
in such matches, is strongly recommended. Hitherto, it
has been mostly the rule for visiting golfers to meet
with almost annihilation, and, in consequence, the
pastime threatens to fall very flat.

Definitely announced that saintly colored cyclist,
Taylor, will visit Australia again next year in search of
boodle.
• • • *
Major Taylor told an Adelaide interviewer,
when leaving by the French mail-steamer, that his
earnings in Australia had been greatly exaggerated, and
only came to £lOOO profit after travelling expenses were
paid. The dusky one and his wife paid a visit of
sympathy to Walne, still laid up in a private hospital,
and they finally faded out of Australia without any
cheering or other formalities from the local Wheelmen—
at which the Major expressed pained surprise.

“B.B.”: The desire of the cycle crank to hang
a series of record-scalps to his belt furnishes faster and
more furious riding each season. But a few years since,
the performance of 30 miles in an hour by J. Michael was
regarded as a wonderful achievement; now, the world’s
fastest paced hour ride stands to the credit of French
bikist Contenet (48 miles 781yds.). The fastest paced
mile, without artificial shields, is also his in 1.16 l-ssec.;
but the actual fastest, with fitted shields to help by suc-
tion to drag him along, belongs to Yankee flier Charles
Murphy, who, behind a train, did the mile in 57 4-ssec.
Still, marvellous as such pedalling has been, the work of
the motor-racing fiend is a good deal swifter and
though the mile record has been lowered time after time’
Henri Fournier, far the most skilful autoist in the
world, expects this season to cover a mile in half-a
minute IHe may succeed—and there may be a funeral.
Big amount of money for cycling in Australia
is shown in the squaring-up of the season’s accounts
Don Walker has scooped £7BB 10s. (34 firsts, 13 seconds
and 9 thirds), Yankee visitor Hopper got £766, Major
Taylor (19 firsts and 2 seconds) £560, Aunger (4 firsts i n .
eluding Austral, 3 seconds and 4 thirds) £549, and’ R
Morgan (15 firsts, 16 seconds and 11 thirds) £364. Then
come hauls from £224 down. Bike season this year lasted
eight months.

Maoriland still takes an interest in axeman-
ship : 7000 people rolled up in the one-horse, wet, windy
city of Invercargill, where the streets are two’ chains
wide, and the wind howls around all the corners at once
at Easter, to see T. Pettit, of Tasmania, the “ world’s
champion," win the single handed sawing, worth £l5
and a gold medal.
That frantic person Yic. Railways Minister
Bent announces that, though the tender of the
Government railway workshops for the building
of 20 locomotives is the lowest, yet unless
the men employed at the workshops leave
the Trades Hall, as he has ordered them to do
and thereby save his personal vanity from being
damaged, he will deliberately waste the public
money by giving the work at a higher price to
private workshops. Another of Bent’s frightful
breakings-out of spite and cantankerousness is
unsympathetically reported after the following
fashion even in the strongly Tory Sydney Tele-
graph :
Since Judge Molesworth recently, in the Insolvency
Court, expressed sympathy with railway employees who
SOUGHT THE PROTECTION OF THE COURT AGAINST THEIR
creditors, the Minister has decided to issue an order to
provide that on and after the 14th May no uncertificated
insolvent shall be permitted to continue in the employ of
the department.
Molesworth merely said that he regarded the rail-
way man with a large family, who nad his pay cut
down from 48s. to 40s. a week, and who went
broke for a small amount after having sickness in
the household, as more deserving of sympathy
than the man who went broke for a large sum by
reason of share-gambling. And even these gentle
remarks seem to have roused the fury of Bent,
and made him wade in to show the railway men
that any kindly remarks made to them will only
increase the wrath of the bulgy official persecutor

Ex-member Walsh, of Tamworfch (N. S. W.),
attributes his insolvency to neglecting his business
for politics. He is far from being the first poli-
tician who has come to grief that way. Yet there
are men who do well out of politics. Alexander
Stuart was dead-broke when he entered political
life, and left £60,000 at his death, after being in
Parliament exactly 10 years.

Gov.-Gen. Tennyson (or his secretary),
writing to some little parish council of a little
island off the little island of Britain, remarks
with grandiose stupidity—
His Excellency begs to thank the Parish Council of
Freshwater cordially for their, kind congratulations and
good wishes. He is proud to fill this high and important
office, because the Australians are loyal to the backbone,
and because the work of Governor-General is full of
absorbing interest and grave responsibility. Lord Tenny-
son feels sure that the Federation of Australia is the
prelude of the larger Federation of Great Britain and her
great self-governing colonies.
The backbone, when you come to think of it, is
very near the surface, unless “loyalty” begins at
the other side—the stomach.

Sydney Telegraph evidently thinks that
the stench of the Robert Reid and Co. case is a
bygone odor, as it starts to publish sub-leaders on
the “ Customs Administration.” The yell now is
that the Minister should not bring into court
“respectable traders” for mistakes where ob-
viously no fraud is intended. The D.T. doesn’t
quote any case where the Minister has brought
such a case into court, but it does mention a case
where a prosecution, commenced by a subordinate
officer, was withdrawn at Kingston’s command
because it was plain that the offence was a mere
error. This trick of howling to a man to clo what
he is already doing may be skilful journalism, but
it’s darned dishonest.
“Lazarus ” : Regular meals are the death
of us—when we get ’em. Look at the healthy
animals, fossicking. Eat when you’re hungry;
but don’t drink when you’re thirsty.
“ The kangaroo has the head of a rabbit,
the body of a deer, and a tail like a bed-post.”—
Sydney Smith.
The vessels used in the communion service
on important occasions in St. Philip’s Church,
Sydney, are for the rest of the time deposited for
safe keeping in a Sydney bank. They are a gift
from George III.
Adelaide bookseller, A. T. Hodgson, who
recently joined the alleged majority, was the
third member of the bookselling firm, Slater,
Williams and Hodgson, which flourished in Mel-
bourne, with branches in other parts of Victoria)
in the 50’s. The only surviving member of that
long extinct co-partnery is tenor singer Williams,
a young-looking man of 70 odd years, whose voice
grows flutier every day.

A medical paper :—
The New Mexico Territorial Board of Health may be
congratulated on having found a use for patent medicine
literature. In a recently issued circular on the preven-
tion of consumption, among other things, the board
advises that “ every person so affected should spit into
some receptacle, and should see that the sputum is soon
destroyed by fire. About the house there is no better
way than to spit between the leaves of patent medicine
almanacs —to be had freely at all drug stores—and after
half-a-dozen or more spittings burn the book.”
Victoria still wants money in order to
avert a general land-tax, so Bent’s bellow grows
louder and Treasurer Shiels’ harassed look gets
more intense every day. The serious state of
things brought the Raven and the Minister for
Breakdowns into consultation t’other day, and
Bent now states that there will be no supplemen-
tary estimates for his department this year; also,
he has sent out instructions to the railway officials
to submit reports showing how the railway em-
ployees may be squeezed of another .£22,000.
Seeing that they have already been squeezed as
dry as a sucked orange; seeing, also, that the Vic.
engine now takes a rest of an hour or so every
couple of miles (when it isn’t so bad that it has
to take to its bed altogether), such a state being
due to want of proper care and nourishment, it is
difficult to see where that extra £22,000 is to come
from ; but Bent says it has to be got somehow.
Bent’s own screw has not been reduced yet.
Squatter E. H. Kater, who died in a Syd-
ney private hospital in MArch last, leaves £45*000
odd. He was a careful, quiet, easy-tempered old
soul, and knew every yard of the Macquarie
River. Unlike his brother, the M.L.C., he took
no interest in politics. Grass, or as he used to
call it “feed, was his great subject, and dis-
coursing about it and blood stock occupied all Ins
spare time.
The warm rains which have fallen through-
out the Commonwealth promise showers of
prosperity later. The two essentials to the salva-
tion of Australia are a good season and the wise
administration of D.C.L. Whisky. For the
wisdom born of D.C.L. is necessary to the proper
management of prosperity when it arrives,- also
that sound liquor hastens the arrival by inspiring
human effort to profitable enterprise. D.C.L.,
The Whisky of the Wise.*
“ Shaker ” : In two towns which I fre-
quently decorate with my presence—Charters’
Towers and Townsville —I know at least half-a-
dozen persons \\ ' (, in as many months, have lost
part of one or more fingers through the collapse
of deck-chairs. The chair slides. The sitter
involuntarily clutches the arms to prevent himself
falling. Then the chair shuts up like a knife, and
does the rest. Why people will take a razor out
on the verandah and sit on it after this fashion I
don’t know.
The only living grandson (in Australia, at
least) of John Batman—John Batman Weire, now
chief of the Vic. Old Age Pensions sub-depart-
ment—favors Melb. Herald with a few particu-
lars of his progenitor’s family. Old man Batman,
who, himself, died at 35, bequeathed the habit of
dying young to most of his children. His only
son was drowned at the Yarra
Queen’s Bridge now stands—at the age of nine.
Of the seven daughters four died ere reaching their
early thirties. The eldest married a Mr. Fennell,
an early-day squatter grazing where Studley Park
now is; they returned to county Tipperary in ’67
and nothing more is known of them. Others of
the girls espoused Daniel Bunce, a curator of
Geelong Botanical Gardens ; Wm. Weire, town
clerk of Geelong; William and John Collyer, but
all are gone now. Nothing remains to recall
Victoria’s founder and his works save his monu-
ment in Melb. general cemetery. Even the hill
in West Melbourne named after him was razed a
few years back, and Melbourne doesn’t put up
statues to those old pioneers,

A curious series of happenings : Tommy
the Nut, hawking tomatoes, stole a bracelet out
of a bedroom window. He planted it in the
grounds of the same mansion, to be called for.
The gardener found it, but said nothing. The
very next day Tommy got six months, as a vaga-
bond. His brother, Jack the Sailor, was coming
out of gaol in two days. Tommy begged that an
old Bible, in his effects, might be given to Jack.
The detectives consented, but impounded the
Bible, and found that, by pin-pricks in the Book
of Genesis, information was given where the
bracelet was planted. The detectives went to
find it, merely explaining, in the mysterious way
of the force, that they were searching for stolen
property, and the alarmed gardener, seeing them
at work, put the bracelet back. As it happened,
it hadn’t been missed. After a while the dis-
gusted police, covered with earth, explained
things to the owner of the mansion, and learned
that no bracelet had been lost, and that they had
been wantonly damaging his grounds. They were
shoo’d off the premises as suspicious characters.
Went back disgusted, and made things as un-
pleasant as possible for Tommy during his six
months. When he came out, he also went by
night and dug a large hole in the grounds. Then
a large dog came and chewed him. He got over
the wall hurriedly, and found nearly a dozen
police waiting for him. This time he got 12
months.
When they have completed the last short
section of the Brisbane-Rockhampton railway,
now in progress, it will be possible for any crank
who likes to try it to have a straight-away Aus-
tralian rail ride of 3295 miles. He will begin at
Oodnadstta (S.A.) and wind up at Longreach
(Q.); or if he starts at the other end he will
spend just as much money and get just as
satisfaction.
It is pleasant to reflect that almost every-
body’s funeral gives pleasure to somebody.
Old John Keats on parsons :
I begin to hate parsons ; they did not make me love
them that day, when I saw them in their proper colors.
A parson is a lamb in a drawing-room and a lion in a
vestry. The notions ol society will not permit a parson
to give way to his temper in any shape, so he festers in
himself; his features get a peculiar diabolical, self-
sufficient, iron, stupid expression. He is continually
acting—his mind is against every man, and every man’s
mind is against him. Parsons will always keep up their
character, but, as it is said there are some animals the
ancients knew which we do not, let us hope our posterity
will miss the black badger with tri-cornered hat. Who
knows but some reviewer of Buffon or Pliny may put an
account of the parson in the appendix ; no one will then
believe it any more than we believe in the phoenix.
A well-known Melbourne sporting judge
and two or three cronies, hurrying Gippsland way
to be in time for the opening of the quail shooting,
were all travelling second (smoking) to enable
them to keep their dogs in the compartment with
them. At Dandenong a seedy-looking customer
got into the carriage, nodded all around, dis-
coursed learnedly on the sporting capabilities of
the different breeds of dogs, and joined in an
animated conversation on the folly of the postpone-
ment of the quail season. After leaving Paken-
ham he became thoughtful, and on nearing Drouin
casually remarked to the man of law and his
friends, “Well, mates, you’ll oblige me very much
by shunting a bit. My ticket only took me as far as
Pakenham, so I reckon I’ll get under this ’ere
seat!” Which he did. The judge? Well, he
didn’t squeal, but tacitly joined in the conspiracy
to defraud the revenue. On a holiday one can’t
be severely judicial towards a man who knows the
good points of a dog—of your dog, in fact.

PAIN in the BACK. GOUT.
EIGHTEEN YEAR S SUFFERING.
WHAT WAS THE CAUSE?
Mr. J. Gleeson, of Bromham Place, North Richmond, Vic., flays— “ Twenty years ago I began to
suffer from a terrible pain in the back. I consulted several of the most eminent doctors in Melbourne.
One doctor said I had lumbago, and gave me liniment to rub on it, but it did me no good. I went to
another doctor, who, after examining my urine, said that the tissues of my kidneys were destroyed, and
that he could give me but a fortnight to live. You can tell the state of alarm into which I was thrown
by this verdict. I immediately consulted another doctor, and he, to my relief, pronounced my
trouble to be muscular rheumatism. He treated me, but his treatment did me no good. For eighteen
years I was under first one doctor and then another, and faithfully carried out all the instructions they
gave me and the treatment they prescribed, but still I obtained no relief from the excruciating pain.
About two years ago I was in despair, and in my desperation I determined to give up consulting
doctors and to take Warner’s Safe Cure, as I had heard and read of many similar cases to my own
being cured by it. After taking the first dose I got relief from the pain. In two days the pain and
ache from which I had suffered for eighteen years had entirely disappeared. I continued to take the
medicine for some little time, and can sincerely declare that it has absolutely cured me, as I have not
had a return of the pain for two years. I used also to suffer a great deal from attacks of gout, but
since taking Warner’s Safe Cure I have not been troubled in this way either.”
IT WAS URIC ACID POISONING.
Uric acid was retained in the blood instead of being expelled fro® the eyetem in a natural manner.
It was one of the many signs by which Nature indicates
KIDNEY DISEASE.
When the kidneys are working perfectly uric acid and other urinary poisons, manufactured in the
body by the continuous decay of the tissues, are naturally removed. The moment, however, that
there is any interference with the action of the kidneys, these poisons accumulate in the system, and
we feel the effect by suffering from Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica, Backache,
Anaemia, Bright’s Disease, Dropsy, Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles, Indigestion,
Torpid Liver. These are some of the many forms in which kidney disease manifests itself. Nine-
tenths of the pains and illnesses to which the human race is subject can be avoided if care is taken
that the kidneys and liver are in a condition to do their work efficiently. The reason that
Warner’s Safe Cure
cures all the disorders mentioned is simply that it possesses the remarkable property of being able to
restore to health and activity the
KIDNEYS & LIVER.
All urinary and biliary poisons are then duly expelled from the system. The food eaten nourishes
the blood and robust health is established.
If you have any reason to suspect that your kidneys are nob healthily performing their duty,
place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass and let it stand for twenty-
four hours. If it is then milky, or shows a sediment like brick-dust, or if particles float about in it,
the kidneys are deranged. No time should then be lost in commencing to take Warner’s Safe Cure.
Neglected kidney disorders will result in Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more
painful manifestation.
A Treatise containing full particulars and accounts of many remarkable cures effected by
Warner’s Safe Cure will be sent free, on application to the Australasian Branch, H. H. Warner and
Co., Limited, Melbourne.
