The unselfishness of the rich is evinced in
the freedom with which they confer the dignity
of labor upon the poor.
Told of the deceased Frank Lockwood,
Q.C.: Being about to defend a villainous-visaged
ruffian in the Criminal Court, the lawyer examined
his hopeless-looking brief, and then went over to
his client and advised him to plead guilty and
profit by the known olemency of the presiding
Judge. Looking him up and down, prisoner spat
contemptuously, leaned over the rails, and replied,
“You fuzzy-headed 1 Wot d’you
s’pose I pay you for? For me to do the work as
you can’t do? Go back to your seat and do what
you’re paid for.” F.L. went back, and secured
his client’s acquittal.
A Melb. sassiety dame, returning by train
from a suburban fete the other day, fussily
descanted on the function, but was greatly per-
turbed at the presence of numerous R.C. priests.
“ One couldn’t move for them, you know, dear.
It’s so unpleasant having to meet those Romans
wherever one goes,’’ and so on for the whole
journey. Up in the corner was an old Irishwoman
who gave every indication of an outburst, but she
reserved herself until she left the train at South
Yarra. Then she poked a savage Hibernian phiz
through the carnage window and exclaimed “ to
Hell with the lot of ye. You won’t find so many
Romans there, anyhow.” And she marched up
the platform with a delicious sense of having
broken up the sassiety religious discussion with
one well-moulded phrase.
The Miners’ Slow Poison.
In Charters Towers, in Bendigo, and in Westralia,
in S’Africa and in England, the indefinite, but
deadly, something which everywhere gets to be
known aa miners’ disease, is insisting on public
notice. It isn’t certain whether the disease is
worse than it was, because it isn’t certain how bad
it used to be; but it is known, beyond all doubt,
that it is desperately fatal and general to-day.
Most likely it really is worse than it was ; at any
rate, there are several reasons why it should be.
Modern hauling and pumping and blasting ap-
pliances have made deep sinking possible; and
deep sinking means, too often, bad ventilation.
The machine drill has also probably helped —the
machine drill which makes a steady output of
fine dust far exceeding that of the old,
slow hand-drill. Working in the hot air
2000 ft. or 3000 ft. from surface, the miner who
breathes this drill dust, and the dust that follows
the heavy charge, and the powder smoke, has
lungs already, probably, none too sound. For
mining is hereditary, and the man whose father
breathed dust most of his life (and probably died
of it) is generally handicapped to start with, and
the more or less vitiated air which he breathes all
his working hours (and too often all his sleep-
ing hours into the bargain) puts him still further
back in the race. The quantity of drill and other
dust which he can swallow without injury is
therefore less even than most men can do with.
Presumably how much he can swallow, and how
long he can go on swallowing it, depend upon a
hundred things ; and, just as the conditions vary,
■o the prevalence and virulence of the disease vary.
Always, however, in greater or less degree,
it is present on every field. It is at its
worst perhaps in S’Africa. Figures which
have been compiled there are, indeed, so
startling that they seem incredible. Careful
inquiry has, however, shown that in some mines
the average life of the miner is only two years !
Of a party of lusty young Australian miners who
went to Johannesburg in 1894, it was lately re-
ported that the last had just died. Australian
experiences are less tragic than that, yet they are
tragic enough. The vital statistics of Eaglehawk,
a portion of the Bendigo (Vic.) goldfield, were
lately analysed, and it was found that one out of
every three of the adult deaths for 1902 was due
to lung disease (that is to say, miners’ disease),
that every one of the male victims had been a
miner, and that the male victims outnumbered the
female by 13 to 1. There is no obvious reason
why Eaglehawk should be worse off than other
raining communities; on the contrary, it is
highly probable that an analysis of the figures
for other fields would make more gruesome
reading. And still hardly anything is being
done. Worse still, comparatively few people
seem to have any clear idea that anything
requires to be done. They know that some-
times a big slice of a mine falls in and
orushes the life out of a miner—now and then
out of half-a-dozen miners; and they applaud (as
they should) the true grit of the mates who go
to the rescue, and they pass laws and pay in-
spectors to see that it doesn’t happen again.
But of the victim of miners’ disease there is no
word in the papers. Usually he has been too
long sick to have left his widow the half-crown
which would buy a mention even in the “ Births,
Marriages, and Deaths” column. And yet, even
under favorable conditions, on every deep-sink-
ing mining field it can safely be said that
three times more men die the cruel,
wasting death of the phthisical patient than
the mercifully-rapid death which generally
comes to the victim of accident. Even the miner
himself only half-realises the truth. “ Poor Btll,
he’s gone ! ’’ he says, when, after a last struggle,
his mate has to “chuck it ” early in a “shift.”
He has seen it coming for months—the whitening
face, the gasp (and at every gasp another mouth-
ful of dust to hurry on the work), the cough and
the spitting, and the weakness; nevertheless,
more often than not, he looks upon it as all in the
day’s work, as all part of the game, and as some-
thing against which it is no use kicking. The
miner’s wife, with the woman’s instinct, is a little
wiser. Not that she thinks much of improving
the miner’s condition either; but to her own sons
she says nearly always, “Don’t you be a miner,
lad.” And of course the lad nearly always
disregards the advice —what else can he do,
where else can he turn ? So the task of
doing anything substantial to mend matters is
shockingly hard. Yet, in the face, of sucli
figures as those quoted, it is a task that cannot be
shirked. Three lots of people must be awakened.
First, the miners themselves—the whole body of
them. They must be got to believe that it isn't
all in the day’s work that they should be poisoned
any more than that they should be smothered or
mangled. Then the owners. If they can’t be
moved to, in all cases, provide good ventilation
and sanitation from motives of humanity, they
may be persuaded to do it on the solid ground
that it’s bad business not to do it. And, finally,
there’s the big, ordinary public—the crowd that
votes—which must be got to say that, if things
don’t improve, the mines that are death-holes
must be closed ; and if they say that as though
they mean it, things won’t be long before they do
improve.
One of Euclid’s lost axioms: “Things
which believe in the same things believe in one
another. ”
Since molasses came into notice as a cure
for cancer, it has been used for many and varied
purposes, and its possibilities appear to have a
wide range. It has at latest been taken up by Bill
Sykes as an effective agent for breaking and
entering. Poured into a paper bag, it is hurled at
a window, and the smashing is accomplished
quickly and noiselessly. The Lilydale (Vic.) rail-
way station office was burglariously entered by
this means a few days ago.
“W.8.M.”: The Immorality Act has
been so strictly enforced in money-grabbing and
syndicate-sharping Capetown (imprisonment for
the “ladies” and the cat for the bludgers) that
the evicted femininity of Capetown is camping
out at Ramps Bay, one of the very few seaside
resorts of Desolation Land. A township of tents
now nestles between the jagged shore and the
frowning and precipitous “Twelve Apostles.”
Here the rouged and powdered refugees riot and
revel to the roaring of the breakers on the
roughest bit of coast in creation. But no men
except the butcher, baker, carrier, and messenger
are allowed to approach nearer than telescope-
distance of the canvas settlement, and there are
policemen on the watch for the would-be tres-
passer. The “Ladies” have a lease of the pro-
perty, and any laxity of tenure means summary
ejectment, not to mention prosecutions under the
Immorality Law. The discovery of one Daniel
in the den of darlings would be sufficient to send
every refugee in the settlement to gaol for three
or more months. Of course they are waiting for
the Immorality war to finish. Meanwhile, they
tempt in vain the Twelve Statuesque Apostles.

rPHE Directors submit to the proprietors the Balance-sheet as at 13th October last, with the profit account for the
half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and colonial rates and taxes
and for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half year, including some recoveries from old account*''
amounted to £152,084175. Bd. ’

annum, or £2 4s. per share, free from income-tax. ' y

at the general meeting held in October last. There will then remain £14,648 ss. Id. to be carried forward to next
account.

Brown Hill Extended (W.A.), as here predicted,
shows an enormous falling off in production in March
compared with February, the deficiency being 3000 oz.
The rich patch between the 300 and 200 ft. is almost
worked out. One or two more crushings are left, but of
poorer stuff. The ss. dividend declared may very likely
prove to be the first and last, after eight years of weary
waiting. Total collapse seems inevitable. The eastern
lode, about which there has been so much futile talk,
is never likely to produce any returns, not to mention
divs. It has proved worthless in the Brown Hill as well
as in the Extended, and yet the directors of the Oroya
East, on the south, are sinking their main shaft to 400 ft.
with the view of developing its continuation.
• • • a
Queen Margaret (W.A.) has completed the pur-
chase of the South Gippsland, at Vosperton, as the
holders refused point blank to grant any extension of
option. The decision was arrived a£ after the clean up
of the third parcel, which returned 381 oz. from 150 tons.
As there are some 5000 tons of tailings, estimated to yield
a profit of £2OOO, besides all the reserves of ore between
the 380 and 180 ft. levels, theco. should at least get back
the purchase money (£3500), Ten head of stamps are
to be removed from the Bulong property to increase the
mill power to 20 head. After the few patches still stand-
ing in the Bulong property are worked out the leases will
be abandoned.
• • • •
Queen Margaret Central (W.A.), which, in-
cluding all equipment, was bought for £2OO by a party of
miners, may yet produce a lot of gold. Very rich patches
have been found on the leases north and south, and there
is no reason why the ground should be a blank. The
property has been opened to 400 ft., but, for unknown
reasons, no stoping was done. The new owners thus
start with 400 ft. of backs, and have arranged with the
Queen Margaret to haul and treat their ore. This is one
of the many Adelaide-owned cos. which, from first to last,
has apparently been worked mainly for market purposes.
• • * »
South Kalgurli (W.A.) loses Manager Isles,
who must be glad to quit such a thankless billet.
The treatment plants which have been installed here
have been a severe handicap,' but Isles probably
didn’t select them. The mine itself is one of the
patchiest on the belt, the daily assays in the same face
varying from dwts. to ozs. in every yard driven. Who-
ever Isles’ successor is he will have a big contract to
make the mine pay. Hamilton, in his special report,
suggests an expenditure of £15,000 to get the equipment
in order and put the mine on a payable basis.
* • * •
Ivanhoe (W.A.) is now opening up the Boulder
lode at the 500 ft. It it 7£ft. wide, and worth 23dwt. per
ton. It outcrops in the Great Boulder, and gradually
underlays into the south-east corner of the Ivanhoe, and,
at the 300 ft. level, shows a length of 220 ft. before passing
into the Great Boulder on the .north and the Horseshoe
on the south.

Latest London news with regard to the pro-
posed Mt. Lyell-North Lyell amalgamation is that the
North Lyell directors refuse the offer submitted, and
negotiations are, for the time “off.” But, unless the
people interested are imbecile, there must be amalgama-
tion—the Lyell’s pyritic ore and the North’s silicious ore
together forth a natural smelting mixture, while to
attempt to handle either separately means high costs
and low recoveries.
• * • *
Ravenswood (Q.) moves again. Grand Junc-
tions, paid-ups in which stood at about 75., bottomed at
190 ft. on 18in. of stone, believed to be the rich Sunset
lode, and reported to be heavily mineralised stone, assay-
ing as high as lloz. Anyway, shares jumped within the
week to buyers 265. for paids. Junctions were at the
outset thought so little of by Ravenswood holders that
they sold out paids long ago in the vicinity of Is., and
the mine is now principally Towers owned. As a result
of the strike, the Irish Girl, a £25,000 property near the
Junction, was floated off with a rattle in 24 hours, and
there is a material boom in all alleged Sunset reef pro-
perties. Ravenswood stone, though terribly refractory,
is rich, the trouble in most cases being to get enough
of it.
At Poverty Point.
“E.F.”: If the Chicago newspapers are not overstating
the case when they say that “Maud M’Carthy draws a
larger tone than any woman who has ever played here ”
it seems safe to predict a mighty celebrity for the Sydney
girl. When she visited Australia some 4£ years ago,
there was a certain thinness of tone in the beautiful
music she produced, as though she hadn’t quite outgrown
the infant-phenomenon handling of a bow. Otherwise
Maud was a most impressive violinist. Heard herplay to
a few people and hundreds of empty seats in a Town Hall
(Melb.) and thought her a saintly musical genius. She
made one feel pure and holy for twenty consecutive
minutes. Any first-class artist can get the audience
interested in Aerself or Aimself. Only the greatest per-
formers can make their audiences believe in fAemselves.
The dispute between a big Melb. daily and one of the
theatre proprietaries has ended in a climb down by the
latter .The strife originated through a well-known
pressman being refused admission on a first night, some-
body else having the paper’s seats, and the ordinary press
list having been set aside without notice. When the
theatre management refused apologies, it found the
supply of puff-paragraphs very much curtailed, and after
reading once or twice a week that its show was merely
“still running,” whilst the casual and despised leg-
demonstrations in little halls down Bourke-street were
described in terms of adulation, it hastened to assure
everybody concerned that the whole business was only
“a little misunderstanding with the doorkeeper,” or
something. This is equivalent to the politician’s excuse
that he was misreported.
Willoughby and Geach last week received a pathetic
letter from a Melbourne dramatic authoress, who has
long waited for the chance to achieve celebrity. The
lady, recognising that local talent must offer itself at an
alarming sacrifice in the first instance, proposed to let
W. and G. purchase her new and original four-act comedy
for 10s. 6d. cash' >Vlso, she will grant them her full per-
mission to make *4iat alterations they please in the plot
and dialogue of her work. For a modest half-guinea the
management can secure the manuscript and all rights
appertaining thereto.
At Wangaratta (Vic.) Show once a boxing amazon
offered to put on the gloves with anyone. The acceptor
was to put down half-a-crown, which was to be returned
with a sovereign if he succeeded in landing one blow on
the lady. A man put on the gloves, the Is. admission
charge was carefully collected from a pretty large audi-
ence, and the pair faced each other on the platform. But
before they could do anymore a massive stranger stepped
on the platform and bashed the male competitor right
across the tent, saying : “ You coward ; would you hit a
woman ? ” He skulked out. The crowd applauded and
thought hard. The gallant remained and helped to count
the takings.
Died at Secunderabad, India, on 12th March, of enteric
fever, Florrie West Devine; the niece of old J. P. West,
the well-known character actor, who occasionally plays
Deacon Skinner when not otherwise employed. She was
aged 23, and had been on the stage longer than her years
might suggest, having started in very small girl parts.
Thos. Holding, the Demetrius in “A Midsummer
Night’s Dream,” is not recognisable as the same Holding
who played Dicey (beastly polite fellow, Dicey) in “ A
Message from Mars” a couple of years ago. Holding
went back to England at the end of Hawtrey’s first
Melbourne season, and he has seemingly learned a little
about acting since then. Moreover, he hag had his hair
cut.

Mostly women and London.
Many good yarns are spun in the reminiscences of the
late Augustus Hare—but not nearly enough to justify
the infliction of six volumes on the public. One relates
to Hare’s great uncle, Lord Ravensworth. When that
unrighteous nobleman was walking one day in Portman-
square he met a nurse with a singularly handsome child.
He stopped her, and said : “ Pray, forgive me for stopping
you, but I can’t help asking whose is that remarkably
fine child ? ” “ Your own, my lord ! ” said the girl. And
that reply so flabbergasted the noble lord that he gasped
out: “ And—and—does my lady know about this child
also?” After all, it’s an unwise mother who knows too
much about her husband’s children.
YOUNG DEAD-HEAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young Dead-Head, lounging at the play,
Views all things w.ith a scornful eye ;
And should his neighbor’s mood be gay
He checks it with a weary sigh.
A heroine in mimic tears
Incites him to aggressive sneers,
He snarls at kll he sees and hears—
Young Dead-Head.
An opera which charms the town
Gives him no thrill of common joy ;
To smile, the same as Smith or Brown,
Would ill become the learnhd boy.
All shows displease him, more or less ;
It is his custom to profess
Contempt for any big success—
Young Dead-Head.
The sweetest songster cannot touch
The place allotted to his heart;
No mummer’s force can knock him much,
Though mummer play a striking part.
His verdict, all the time, is “ Rot!
The same old tricks, the same old plot I"
He knows it all, he knows a lot—
Young Dead-Head.
Blow, blow and blast, thou wintry wind f
Blow chilly on the showman’s chest;
No blowing can be so unkind
As that of pressman unimpressed,
Who finds all entertainments poor,
Flat, stale—in fact, an awful bore—
Because his name is “ on the door ”
Young Dead-Head.

Russia still continues the system—common
to medseval Europe but abolished now in all truly
civilised countries — of allowing religious persons
—priests and monks of the Greek church — exemp-
tion from the general law; they are punishable
only by their spiritual heads. The natural result
is that the criminal classes flock to the monasteries,
where they can pursue, without any real check, a
life of vicious indulgence. Such incidents as that
reported by cable the other day (of two young
English girls being kidnapped and taken to a
monastery to be subjected to outrage) are com-
mon enough in all but the nationality of the
victims, which has brought this particular case
under the notice of the world.
Canon Scott - Holland thus speaks of
“Merrie England”: “A country-side withering
into waste and desolation as if suddenly struck
with some blight; an increasing residuum of the
unemployable in the morass which is forming in
great cities; a population developing in over-
crowded streets and houses, anaemic, stunted, cut
off from the winds of nature and all visible mani-
festation of the workings of God —this is the
problem which confronts us. Nothing but some
great misfortune, the shock of unsuccessful war, or
a vast trade depression with starvation and misery,
will ever shake us from the kind of enchanted
sleeping walk in which we are at present en-
gaged.”

Sailor Bill Gets His Hand Read.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A palmist came—an’ lots of folk rushed off to
know their fate;
An’ every flat-foot Biddy got a squatter for a
mate. |
“I’ll have a hand,” thinks I, “in this;” and goes
and states me case :
“You’ll find,” I sez, “me lines not cast in any
pleasant place;
I’ve lost me friends—l’ve lost me hope, me happi-
ness an’ ease:
What have I got to part with next?” She sez,
“Five shill in’s, please.”
So I squares her with five bob, and she gets upon
the job,
An’ grabs me ’and —the one that got the anchor
on the wrist.
Wrist —on the wrist —yes, she gets me by the
fist;
She reads it up —she reads it down —she reads it
on the twist;
She reads it fore and aft, an’ she sez, “It’s done
some graft; ”
An’ she looks at every line and lump from finger-
tip to wrist.
Then she sez : “ From indications that I see it’s
very plain
That you’ve been a hardy sailor, and you’ve
ploughed the raging main.
An’ some harrowing experiences you’ve often had
ashore. ”
(“I’ve been had for all me gilt,” I sez, “some
twenty times, or more.”)
“You come from famous London town —you
haven’t any chicks.”
“Excuse me, mum, but in me time, I’ve been
accused of six.”
“You’re in poverty to-day. For you’ve chucked
your chance away.
You spend your money foolishly.” “By Jove,”
I sez, “ I do.
Do—yes, I do ; every bloomin’ cheque I blue.
I liquor up the lads around; I shout for Lord
knows who.
An’ I’m quick to help him if a blessed bloke is
stiff;
But when I let him chew my lug—it always
proves a ‘ do. ’ ”
Sez she: “You’re kind an’ honest—keen and
candid—hard to drive ;
You’ll love the booze —you’ll love the girls as long
as you’re alive;
You’d fight the great Fitzsimmons—for it’s all the
same to you.”
"Well, now,” I sez, “it’s strange, but every
word of that is true.”
“I have me trances, too,” she sez, “an’ if you’re
satisfied,
Ten bob will turn your future up an’ let you look
inside.”
Then I planks down half a quid ; an’what d’ye
think she did ?
She sits back in the big arm chair an’ simply shuts
her eyes.
Eyes—shut her eyes, while I looked in blank
surprise;
An’ when she gives a groan, I thought: “ Suppose
the beggar dies.”
An’ then I hears a snore : “ What, ain’t there any
more?
Is this the lot for four half-crowns? Well, dash
my bloomin’ eyes ! ”
Then she carried on in such a style I really
thought her sick;
Her nose a-twitching all the while, like a monkey
on a stick.
She looked so very comical I really thought I
grinned—
“ Is it rheumatiz,” I sez, “ brought on by this ’ere
cold south wind ? ”
But she never sez a blessed word—just lies there
straight and stiff.
I lights a match beneath her nose—but she didn’t
even sniff.
So off I starts for aid; when she sez “Don’t be
afraid;
It’s me sperrit overcomes the flesh —me sperrit’s
far too strong.”
“Strong?” sez I. “Strong? Have you patronised
it long ? ”
“But you don’t understand,” she sez; “you’ve
got me meanin’ wrong.
It’s me soul, so pure and bright, that soars clean
out of sight,
And sails amongst the things unseen.” “Be
Jove,” I says, “ that’s strong.”
“ Now,” she sez, “ I’ve had me vision an’ it’s very
clear to me
That a fortune’s coming to you—an’ ’twill come
across the sea.
Then you’ll wed with youth and beauty—though
you’re past your manhood’s prime :
She’ll break your heart with bitter blows—an’
love you all the time.”
“Ah, well,” I sez, “love’s bitter-sweet, and often
love’s unkind;
An’ if an old Plan weds a girl, he should be deaf
and blind.
But tell me this,” sez I; “Is it long before I die?
What! Twelve months after marriage ? Then
I’ll never have a bride.
For what use is a bride to a man that’s gone and
died,
So, missus, if you don’t object we’ll let that
wedding slide.
Why, an open grave I’d see betwixt me bride an’
me.
When the line of life has parted, what’s the good
of any bride ?”
N.s.w.

A Bush School.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It has often been remarked that the backblock
settler, when driven to seek a living in other
fields, can take up his rolled blanket and billy,
and start straight off on the pad, as if to the
nature born. Yet there is nothing to wonder at.
He began his training for the wallaby when he
started going to school; and, for anyone destined
in after life to follow the precarious existence of
the swagmau, there is no better institution than
the far-off, lone little building that stands like a
signal-box on Gum Ridge, or in the bend of
Wattle Creek. It is anything from two to six
miles from M‘Gee’s selection or Duggan’s farm ;
and Bill and Jim, with the tucker-bag containing
their lunch, and their boots—if they have any—
slung over their shoulders, their pants rolled up to
their thighs to escape the wet grass, start off at
daylight on a winter’s morning, and even then, at
times, have to sprint to get there before “school
goes in.” Their track is a winding streak, which
their own hardened feet have trodden through the
long grass, over hills and over gullies, round
swamps and through scrubs. In the rainy seasons
they wade through miles of mud and water, and
the grass-shoots prick like needles; in dry times
the caked black soil is hard and rough as a cobble-
bed.
Far on their way, they see the sun rise redly
above the fog-banks, and know if they are late or
early by certain trees, or particular points on the
road; they compare his progress thence with their
own, telling the time to a minute. They discuss
the intricate and skilfully-woven webs of bush
spiders, glistening from a thousand grass-spires,
examining the spiders’ work and methods; they
hunt for birds and eggs, climb after ’possums and
bears, criticise butterflies and grasshoppers;
everything living, whether bird, animal, insect or
plant, comes under the keen scrutiny of inquisitive
eyes. That track is part of the bush school.
In summer-time their load is supplemented with
a water-bag ; for as often as not there is no tank
or other means of obtaining water at school. The
shaded part of the establishment would remind
one of a soft-drinks shanty, or a curiosity-shop.
Thirty or forty bottles, of all sizes, shapes and
colors, stand in a row on the ground, while twenty
dinner-bags—covered with flies —hang on nails
above them. The scholars eat their lunch under
trees, a hunk of bread and beef in one hand and
the bottle in the other.
In the afternoon, during the short days, the
bush scholar has sometimes to run part of the way
to get home in time to chase the cows round the
paddock, to cut weed for the pigs, and put the
ducks in. After tea he husks the corn till 11
o’clock. On Saturday he carries water and wood,
and on Sunday he has a holiday 7 —following dad
round the swamps, carrying the game and
ammunition-bag- Poor Bill is always carrying
something.
Some of the youngsters, who live beyond walk-
ing distance, ride to school on horseback. Two
and three straddle the one moke, boys and girls
alike, and they bump along like a package on a
trotting camel. On the way home they give their
mates a lift, and the poor old pony is covered from
tail to neck. Sometimes the moke remonstrates,
and kicking up vigorously, deposits his load of
assorted boy and girl in a huddled heap on the
roadside. I knew a boy who, one evening, took
several of his mates on board, and then sent his
mount at a log to show them how he could ride.
Two deserted backwards at the take-off, and the
others all landed on top of boy Green on the other
side. The old horse kicked up again with delight,
and galloped home. ■ Green never attempted to
show off after that.
Those not compelled to hurry from school duties
to home duties have gay times on the road. It is
the time that little differences are settled at the
turn-off tracks, when coats are “ peeled,” and fists
perform unscientifically under the gum trees. All
manner of games are played on the way, from
“tippeny-catcher” to “kiss-in-the ring” (girl
kissing girl not allowed); wild flowersare gathered,
and forest yams dug up with sticks ; they probe
the hollow logs for native cats, and bathe in the
lagoon ; there is racing and hunting on foot and
kangarooing and dingo chasing on horseback. It
is all “experience,” all part of the bush school —
the main part, probably ; many of the scholars
can subtract a bandicoot from a log with greater
ease than they can bring pounds of corn to
bushels.
It was at Phegan’s Academy that I learnt to
make pot-hooks and hangers. It was a private
institution, maintained by several farmers, whose
corn-weights Phegan had to reckon up every
threshing and divide into bushels, and make the
bushels into £ s.d. ; and woe be to Phegan if he
erred to the extent of a “tray-bit.” The school fee
was a shilling a week, mostly paid in corn,
potatoes, pumpkins, eggs, and farm sundries;
und the class-room was in a barn between the
corn-straddles. There was probably more whack-
ing done there than in any other school in crea-
tion. The scholars couldn’t sit still for weevils
and ants; and when the farmer was threshing or
husking, Phegan, who was a bit deaf, couldn’t
hear the class reading, and the class couldn’t hear
Phegan dictating. Then the fowls and pigs
would come in, and now and again the cart-horse
made a tour of inspection. Occasionally the
brindle cow and the spotted steer offended, and
the class scrambled up on to the corn. It was the
duty of Bolem, the black boy, to hunt out all
wandering stock ; but Bolem wasn’t always equal
to the occasion, so the dog had to be sooled on.
When the intruders were driven out, the big boys
re-adjusted the furniture, gathered up the ink-
pots and pieces of slate—and we got on with the
pot-hangers.
Phegan had other troubles to contend with.
Some of the scholars were big enough to be mar-
ried, and they resented Phegan’s idea, of .moral
suasion by occasionally “plugging him in the
eye.” Phegan’s uninjured optic would then scin-
tillate on the smallest boy in the class, and that
boy daren’t scratch or wriggle for a week, though
a horde of weevils were torturing him. I was
one of the small boys, though old enough to
emulate the big boys in kissing the girls (I
always loved girls—got sentimental at a very
tender age); but it was over the letter R that I
got my first drubbing.
“ Say ar-r-r-ah,” said Phegan, one day.
“Ar-r-r-ah,” said I, imitating as nearly as I
could the peculiar quaver of Phegan.
“Don’t say —“ar-r-r-ah ! ” cried he. "oay
ar-r-r-ah!”
That beat me completely. I could hear no differ-
ence between one ar-r-r-ah and another ar-r-r-ah.
Still, 1 had another go at it, giving it a more
determined quiver—“ Ar-r-r-ah!” "'The sound
might have been compounded of tne burr of a
distant bull and the whirr of a crosscut saw.
Phegan turned purple. “Not ar-r-r-ah, blast
ve ! Say ar-r-r-ah ! ”
He meant me to give it the English pronunci-
ation, and probably had no idea that he himself
was rolling it. I didn’t “tumble” even then. I
screwed myself up, and shaking my head to giva
it the supposed necessary amount of quiver, I
rolled out ar-r-r-r-r-r-ah till I was red in the face.
Then I got it hot—on hands, arms, back and
shoulders.
“By me sowl, I’ll learun ye to spake English,
yer chatterin’ gorilla,” he cried as he flung me
back on the seat. “Now say ar-r-r-r-ah ! ”
A boy behind me whispered “Ar.” In an in-
stant I saw where the mistake was, and jumped
to my feet. “Ar! ’’ I almost shrieked.
“Arrah!” said Phegan, “I thought I could
knock it into ye, be gob ! ”
Phegan was a stern disciplinarian, and the
punishment meted out to delinquents at times
took a form which induces me to believe he must
have heard of Mr. Wackford Squeers. “You
sthop in, Charlie Pickles,” he would say, “an’
rub the shoots off av four dishes o’ spuds. They’re
in the skillion. That will learn ye ter count up
yer sums maybe.” Charlie would measure off four
dishes of spuds, and pick the shoots off the top of
the heap. “Phil Nolan,”said Phegan, addressing
another boy, “yer copy-book’s absolutely filthy.
Sthop in an’ husk 200 cobs o’ corn, ye dirty hog.”
One day as school was going out Mrs. Phegan
entered with a complaint that the water-butt was
empty. “ Halt! ” cried Phegan instantly. He
went along the line till he came to a short, chubby
lump of a youth with red hair and brown legs.
“I cot ye copyin’ terday, Jack Thompson. It
escaped me mimory till this minute,” said Phegan
sententiously. “Copyin’ is stealin’, Thompson,
an’ stealin’ is wan av the black sins. Sin must
be punished ter give honesty a chance av a sthand.
So go you now with Mrs. Phegan an’ carry 10
buckets av water from the river before ye ate yer
dinner. Left, right, march ! . . Halt ! . . Right-
about-face ! . . Sthand at ease ! . . Dismiss !
an’ (sotto voce) to the divil wid ye ! ”
About once a month Phegan had a spree, and
on the Monday following it was usual to find him
very ill. He would give us our lessons, and
then go to sleep. One day Reardon, the
senior pupil, got the clothes-line and leg-roped
him to a post. Threshing had been going on the
previous week, and there was a heap of corn-flour,
sieved out of weevily corn, in the skillion. We
were smothering one another with this when
Phegan woke. With a snort he scrambled to his
feet, and grasping a hoe-handle, sprang at an
inoffensive little boy who was looking on. The
leash wasn’t long enough, however ; and Phegan
landed on his stomach with a tremendous grunt.
For a moment he lay there, spread out like a frog
on a hot shovel. Then he upended very slowly,
glaring at us with murder in his bleared eyes.
“Fall in!” he roared. We fell in. “Atten-
tion!” We attentioned. “Now then, ye bog-
throtters ; spake the truth, mind ye, or be the
howly shmoke, I’ll be the death av wan av ye !
Is it a dirty cow ye take me for?” He shook his
tethered leg. “Who leg-roped me?” His eyes
wandered along the lines, while he brandished the
hoe-handle.
Silent and rigid stood the line, and Phegan spat
out some of the dust he had gathered up.
“Spake,” he roared, “or be the Power above
me, I’ll knock ye down, the whole row av ye ! ”
The hoe-handle was lifted threateningly. “ D’yer
hear? Who leg-roped me ? ”
Reardon’s hand suddenly shot up. W e antici-
pated a fight.
“Please, sir, it was Bolem ! ”
The instant the name was uttered the black boy
broke from the ranks and took to his heels.
“ Afther him, all av ye ! ” roared Phegan, making
a dart forward himself; but the rope jerked him
back, and the school floor left an abrasion on his
nose. “Catch him, ye divila !” he gasped, “or
I’ll murder ye.”
We spent the remainder of the school-time
chasing Bolem through the corn and sampling
melons. Bolem escaped.
Phegan didn’t reign long after that. He drank
more than ever, and got the sack. Then they
installed an eccentric old bachelor named Bowker.
He wasn’t a bad sort, and would light his pipe,
and sit in front of us for hours spinning yarns.
He taught us a lot about tramps and squatters
and the way to catch fish, but he couldn’t reckon
up corn. We got along very well with him till
the farmers heard of a little peculiarity he had of
making love to the big girls. They called on him
one day, and the subsequent proceedings were so
sultry that Bowker jumped into the river and
swam for new fields.
He hasn’t come back for his things yet.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
“L.W.”: Sydney Daily Telegraph is
breaking out in strange places : First of all it
went straight out for the Old Age Pensioners
then it fell tooth and nail on the Professional
Bruiser; and now it has spread itself on the
Professional Beggar in such a manner as to cause
the individual who takes his opinions ready made
from the newspapers to look upon the existence of
misery in the city as an impossibility, and every
destitute person who wails for assistance as an
ingenious impostor who is engaged in a lucrative
business. This writer has heard much of the
blind mendicant who has bought houses and
property out of the proceeds of playing a musical
box in the street; and the street singer who wails
dolorously in the hot dusty gutter by day and
lives in Oriental luxury in Woolloomooloo by
night is a well known myth. Then there is the
man who calls round at offices trying to sell trans
parent tape for sticking torn pages together and
talks sympathetically of his little ones, and who is
understood to be a gay individual with a harem
of six, all maintained out of his earnings in the
sympathy business. The wealthy blind musician
I knew personally, and the family lived in squalid
misery. The street-singer I accosted one night
and got her to give me her address for investiga-
tion purposes, and the poor creature was very
badly fixed, indeed. The transparent tape man
is an elusive individual, and as he is always
changing in height, size and complexion I have
come to the conclusion that he’s a transitory
person who takes to it while out of work. Any-
how, from careful personal investigation, this
writer is absolutely sure there is no fortune in
professional mendicancy in this country. There
was one long individual, with a skull cap and a
pointed beard, who used to whine round the streets
in a state of hideous uncleanliness, and stand to
windward of his intended victim, and keep there
till bought off. I think he’s in gaol now—any-
how I haven’t smelt him round any of the corners
for a month or two.

Autumn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O sweet, sad season, why should all your wonder
bring
The meditative stillness of your mellowed eves,
Only a vain suggestion of the vanished Spring,
Like a pale ghost that mars and mocks the
ripened sheaves
And lifts a loud lament amid the falling leaves ?
O thou for whom each leaf an altar burns,
Thou dark divinity of southern gypsies bred,
O wherefore is it that my fancy ever turns,
As to a time when she no longer shall be dead,
Toward thy blue-eyed, brown-haired sister sylph
instead ?
O dear, dead season, that preparest earth for Pan,
The god who seeks for summer-service love with
fear,
By what device of magic, lightening Autumn’s
ban
Do I again the gay or pensive piping hear
Attuned to Strephon’s mood, yet ever sweet and
clear ?
Have not the trees put on their raiment of red
gold
Matching the Sibylline mood of the mist-veiled
skies?
Does not the watcher by the wine-press oft be-
hold,
With afternoon’s mild sun-shafts falling on his
eyes,
The blue smoke from a thousand smouldering fires
arise ?
In this fair, sun-flushed valley by the fairer sea
The keen breeze ever like an eager clarion blows,
The wakened Ocean in uncoiled immensity
Along the shrinking shore-line like a serpent goes,
And backward proudly his coiled crest of salt
foam throws.
All the long afternoon the land low laughed, with
love
At large caresses of the sea by beach and bay,
Beneath the blue sky brooding like a mighty
dove;
The waters sang a frWilingslied of joy all day,
Woven of billow-crash and cadences of spray.
Yet purple vales, dream-dusked and twilight-
charmed, attend
The rising of the harvest-moon, and seem
Splashed with the splendor of the vintage’s rich
end,
And sunlit emerald and turquoise gleam
Turn to the smouldering opal of an Autumn
dream.
For this is Austral Autumn with her mask of
Spring
By day, and heart of Summer in the slumbrous
eves,
Whose morning laughter holds an undertone of
tears —
Season that like a fallen empress proudly grieves,
Shrouded in darling dreams dethroned, and rebel
fears,
Starlike in gloom where none finds heart to sing,
Dull-burning through the veils of evening.
N.S.W.
GEO. C. WHITNEY.
The Strad.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a narrow Sydney street, in a poor and not
necessarily respectable neighborhood. The Poet
dwelt in that street, not because he preferred
squalor and noise to wide avenues and engardened
mansions, but for the cogent reason that the rents
there were much cheaper. It had been a busy
morning with the Poet, busy because of a
bailiff whose dark, shadowy image flashed up con-
tinually in his mental cinemetograph and ob-
scured the painted pictures of his Muse. Wearily
the Poet leaned back in his ricketty chair. Just
then a barrel-organ broke into uncouth harmony
below his window and raucously stilled the imme-
diate uproar of the street. It was ridiculous, but
the rasping strains of the hurdy-gurdy threw the
man of song into a reverie. It seemed to him that
he was sitting, as he sat one day, by the side of a
Polish gipsy, whose sole possession, save the rags
that fluttered between him and nakedness, was a
genuine Strad.—his wife, children, home and
country, from which not even the treasures of a
Rajah could persuade him to part. From this
fountain of music the gipsy drew constant streams
of harmonies.
All human emotions, all earthly passions, sor-
rows and pleasures were contained in the belly of
this wonderful Strad.; in its neck it carried the
choruses of the celestial choirs; and below the
bridge were concentrated the tragic groans of the
damned.
When the musician was in waggish humor he
would let loose Earth, Heaven and Hell together,
and then, to those who listened, the laughter and
sobs of mortals, the hosannas of angels and the
shrieks of devils pained, pleasured and affrighted
at the same moment. Without doubt, some
wizard of the Past had imprisoned a Poet’s
soul in the Strad. The necromancer was dead,
but the spell endured for ever.
One day the Polish gipsy played upon the soul
of the Poet in the Strad a music story of the
gipsy life to the courtesy Poet in the flesh.
John Keats wrote in flowers; the Strad. spoke
in melodies. In such melodies the musician crys-
tallised the history of the world’s wanderers, and
for the first time the Poet understood the great
instinct of unrest, and knew why Bohemia is the
happiest country of them all.
As the strains of the magical violin rose and
fell he saw Macedonian minstrels marching ahead
of Alexander’s army, chanting to a golden lyre
praises of the beauties of Babylon and the plunder
of the Pharaohs. He heard laurelled bards in-
toning the prowess of Roman arms beside the
legions’ camp fires. He saw the moon glinting
on the lance heads of wandering medieval knights
whose horses’ hoofs kept time to valiant rounde-
lays carolled by amorous minnesingers. He heard
the guitars of Arabian warriors pleading beneath
the miradors of Moorish maidens. He heard the
rovers roaring down the Spanish Main, the
chanteys of East India sailorman bringing up the
company’s anchors, the shouts of diggers, the
laugh of cow-boys, the calls of Australian stock-
men, the cries of African hunters; and through
this queer medley rang all the while the words of
a song. . . . unwritten words of strange, familiar
meaning, but to hold which in the hollow of his
pen the Poet would have foregone the world.
As the tones of the Strad' deepened and more
familiar fell the tune he saw the Southern Cross
silvered through darkened pines; heard the she-
oaks telling wild stories to a great southern moon ;
saw long reeds by far lagoons bowing to the sun
as it climbed over rugged hills ; caught an echo of
surf on Eastern beaches, and from plain, hill,
gully and riverside saw a thousand beckoning
arms and heard a thousand voices calling.
The strains of the Strad. softened.
The musician no longer played upon its strings,
but upon the heart strings of the imprisoned
Poet, even as playeth the angel Israfel in Para-
dise.
As he hearkened, the Poet understood why
Anthony perished for Cleopatra, why Paris lost
all to gain Helen, and why there are only two
things in the world and neither one of them is
new.
The long-drawn notes were caresses, the short
ones kisses.
The love-songs of all the world were being sung
by a chorus of women’s voices, and although each
song was in a different tongue, the Poet readily
understood them all. Keeping time to the
melodies were a score of sweet instruimncs,
Spanish guitars, lutes, banjos, harps, mandolins,
Japanese cymbals. Through a rosy haze of
vision, Nautch girls in Indian gauzes swayed volup-
tuously ; island women, with wild, natural move-
ments of rounded brown limbs, danced their
inviting native dances; the sing-song girls of the
further East chanted monotonously; black eyes
flashed electric fires from under black lashes ; blue
eyes melted in amorous tears ; brown eyes yearned
trustfully; eyes of grey confessed boldly their
burning passions; and moon eyes stared languidly,
disdainfully, but in soft complaisance withal.
As gently as the butterfly hovers over the
flower, so hovered the musician’s bow over the
strings of his enchanted instrument. Babbling
fountains began to dripple down the hillsides.
Earth laughed and Ocean echoed her. The
nymphs were singing in the streams, and the mer-
maids whispered love-legends in their deep sea
caverns.
The Poet saw his own country —the Lady Aus-
tralia, fertile, glorious—spreading westward in
the sunshine.
He saw her girdled by tropical seas, arcaded by
azure skies. Amethyst and hyacinth, emerald
and topaz, she glittered in her glory.
Bound by purple mountains, crowned by amber
plains ; her hand on empire, and her feet upon
the clouds, Art her sceptre, and Beauty her foot-
stool. He heard the clear voices on the ranges,
the voices of women and men, the Voice of
Nature, the harmony of birds, the eloquence of
flowers ; and his Soul said to him—“ Romance is
not dead. Neither is chivalry forgotten. The
red Dawn still rides in her chariot of roses, and
the Sunset still sleeps on his couch of gold ; the dew
yet glistens, and, after rain, springs the freshness
of the earth ; honey hath not lost its sweetness,
nor wine its flavor. Romance is not dead, but
sleeping. She dreams among the foliage of this
Southern Land. The ways of the older world are
grown narrowed to her feet, but here, in Nature’s
unhorizoned dominions, she reigns Eternal Queen.
The thirsty Spirit of Adventure,” continued the
Voice “which, as old as human longing, is ever
new, doth not drink at street fountains nor halt
upon the trodden ways of men, but in the saddle
and on the sea, on boundless highways; un-
surveyed, by tracks untravelled, by farthest camp-
fires, through forests virgin and plains primeval,
in the open where skies arch nobly, by the un-
polluted source of waters, by the grey sentinel
hills, in the Outer Land, in the Distant Land, in
the Land of Over-There, will ye find her.*’
The dream-music died in smiling promises.
The hurdy-gurdy had shut up with a soul-
destroying screech, like the squeal of a minor
devil suddenly cast on the red hot bars of an upper
octave.
As the Poet was collecting his strayed wits the
Italian proprietor of the machine looked up and
perceived him. Mechanically he took off his hat
and held it out to receive his largesse.
The Poet felt vainly in his pockets for a coin,
and shook his head.
The Italian adjusted the strap of the organ and
moved off, followed by a small juvenile audience
of extreme personal uncleanliness.
And once more arose the sordid noises of the
poor but not necessarily respectable neighborhood.
E. J. BRADY.
An ex-Labor member makes this com-
plaint :
When I sat in Parliament my electorate colleague was
McThousand, the wealthy merchant, who resided in a
mansion at the top of my street. Constituents who
called showed discrimination in approaching us. They
(the constituents) were mostly ’orny-’anded persons.
When they visited me they would leave open my front
gate, ignore the mat, bang at the door, and say—“ Tell
Bill I want to see him.” If Bill (myself) was engaged, or
out, they would mutter, perhaps curse, and walk off,
leaving the gate open, and go and talk about me being
no good, and “ was never there when he was wanted.” A
call at McThousand’s place was a different matter. They
didn’t ask for “ Sam,” though Samuel McThousand was
his full name. They closed the gate, wiped their feet
carefully on the great man’s palatial mat, and when told
he was too busy, said respectfully that they would call
again. Then they left, closing the gate again, and went
and spoke with extra disrespect of “ Bill ” (which was
me).
“F.C.” on a new kind of girl :
Was riding on a Melb. tram-dummy, t’other day, when
the usual smart young Know-all, from Kyabram or
thereabouts, stepped off suddenly, as he hau watched
others do, and promptly struck the blocks with his ear,
thereby acquiring a gravel-rash that he’ll take back to
Rodney with him. He was assisted to the footpath while
the conductor—having, for his bosses’ protection, to get
names of witnesses and make a report—assured him
strenuously he was the worst sort of an idiot and prayed
him to get back to the hush quicker than immediately.
Half-a-mile further on a young woman sitting near me
rose quickly while the tram was negotiating a curve at a
12-mile gait, grasped the upright and swung herself out.
The gripman shouted and jammed his brakes hard on, I
made a grab at her skirts, but missed, and the girl-
stepped easily off and walked away, smiling demurely.
It took ten minutes’ pushing to get the car off the curve,
while an inspector fumed and swore. This official told
me there were, as far as he knew, but two gins in Mel-
bourne who had acquired the knack of gettingoff adummy
at full speed—and he hoped there would never be more.
“ The old hands know them, but the new men foresee a
catastrophe, and either cause one by grabbing the girl
when she’s half off or else upset everything as this man
did. Girls who jump off going trams are a pesky
nuisance.” I advised him to go after the damsel and tell
her so, but he said he thought I could find him something
better to do. So we went and found it.

COMMENTS
Tenor Lloyd, who sings agreeably without being
in the least marvellous, tells a Telegraph (Sydney) inter-
viewer that usually he smokes a couple of cigars and two
or three pipes daily, and never found tobacco hurt his
voice. R. S. Smythe will go to his grave (next century,
we hope) explaining how it is the other narcotic that tar-
nishes silvery tenors.
Said Prof. Harper, principal of St. Andrew’s
(Presbyterian) College, Sydney, recently “An in-
errant Bible is regarded as non-existent, for it is admitted
by everyone that the science of the Scriptures in no way
stands above the crude science of uninspired men at the
time they were written. Now we are being told that its
history is no more absolutely accurate than its science.
. . . We cannot now settle revelation by quoting texts.
. . . Thought and study and prayer will be needed, and
before the new authority comes to be established as the
old one was, almost all the text-books for Bible teaching
will have to be re-written.” (Make your son a religious
publisher!) But why rewrite? The average believer
merely wants “ authority ”; he is not concerned with
truth. Give him something to lean on—any painted lath
that his hereditary instinct of superstition can take
for iron—and a fico for your truth ! Truth is a nasty,
disturbing thing with sharp edges, and the edges are apt
to cut. “ Believers ” want nice, smooth lies and lullabies,
unctuous verbiage and the Kiss of Peace.
J. A. Andrews, dying slowly of consumption in
a Melb. hospital—with benevolent Anarchy still un-
realised, alas !—hopes to see his little verses collected
and published before the cheerful end. Melb. Tocsin has
the volume in hand—just a little remembrance, a little
token of the Attempt, a little record of a hope that life
was not ail in vain.
Some of Hector Macdonalds “poetry” is
printed, and very sensible “poetry” it is—good enough
to be read 'in general orders anywhere. The deceased
(and be careful of the spelling) warrior is said by Sydney
Truth to have published a book of verses: the quoted
sample states the whole duty of marksmanship—
A Dutchman’s baby toddles with a gun
And roams unheeded ’neath the dazzling sun,
Till instinct tells the distance to a yard,
And arm and eye in action are but one.
The rhymes are admirably solid : the idea no less. When
we take Moses striking the rock as a symbol of the Bard,
we shall certainly label the rock Scotch granite.

Professor Cattell, in The Popular Science
Monthly, tells how he found the world’s most eminent
thousand men. He took six biographical dictionaries —
two English,
two French,
one German,
and one Ameri-
c a n and
found in each
the 2000 men
who were al-
lowed the
longest arti-
cles. This gave
him some 6000 names. Then he
selected the men who appeared
in the lists of 4 at least three dic-
tionaries—some 1600 ; and from
these selected the thousand who
were allowed the gTeatest aver-
age space. Thus he obtained
not only the thousand men esteemed mcfct eminent, hut
also their order of eminence. According to his list the
ten most eminent men are Napoleon, Shakespear,
Mohammed, Voltaire, Bacon, Aristotle, Goethe, Caesar,
Luther, Plato—two Greeks, two Frenchmen, two Ger-
mans, two Englishmen, one Roman, one Arab, and no
Australian. “There is no doubt but that Napoleon is
the most eminent man who has lived.” And theannexed
diagram shows, proportionately, how the Eminent
Thousand are divided amongst the nations. The shaded
parts represent the most eminent five hundred, and the
heavily shaded parts the most eminent of all.
Peter Stiens, the Russian doctor who hopes to
make the blind see, bases his invention on the fact that
sight is essentially mental and not physical. The eyes
are only an instrument for receiving images which the
optic nerve conveys to the perception-centre in the brain.
Some of the lower animals have no eyes, but perceive
light with their whole bodies. Stiens gathers an image
on a screen, instead of on the retina, and conveys it to
the brain bv an electric current. He points out that the
process called cataphoresis, which some Sydney dentists
have adopted, is an analogous use of electricity. When
you ask for cataphoresis, the dentists fill your hollow
tooth with anaesthetics such as cocaine, connect a bat-
tery, and the electricity (theoretically, always ; practi-
cally, sometimes) carries the cocaine into the nerve. So
Stiens says he can send an image to the visual centres of
the brain ; and definite proof is wanted.
Consider those bedizened tom-fools posturing
and grimacing before Gov.-General Tennyson—tom-fool
Barton, tom-fool Reid, tom fool Dibbs and the rest :
what a spectacle for sane Democracy ! Is n’t it time sen-
sible men set their faces against mock courts and mil-
liner’s dummies, quasi-savages strutting an inch taller
with a rag of ribbon or a red feather? “Sensible men
do”——then why the mummery? Passementerie—frills
and trimmings—Victor Hugo calls it ; and “tout passe,
tout ment, tout n’est que passementerie,” comments a
French wit. They all use it—nose-ringed African in his
gala paint and plumed Australian horseman in his war-
paint. From frizzed Papuan to bewigged judge, from
catamaran to Cardinal Moran, there is one gaudy step.
Pitiful forked radishes ! burning incense to private vanity,
or bent on bamboozling the mob. Time the farce ended.
We hope the next generation will breed a race of poli-
ticians, lawyers, soldiers, clerics, content to rest their
claim to esteem on the truth of themselves, not upon the
lie of gew-gaws and trappings. Since America has
manxed the cat, surely Australia can.
The fact that three Gray Horses (!) stimulated
by the plaudits of three thousand Fair Girls (?) broke the
high-jump record of 6ft. sin. at Sydney “agricultural"
show, reminds us that W. H. Ogilvie’s verse-charm has
been admitted by excellent judges over the water. He
seems to be a favourite with Macmillan’s Maga., and his
work has appeared also in The Century , Temple Bar,
English Illustrated, Chambers’s, The Spectator, and
other journals. The Spectator of Feb. 28 has a charac-
teristic piece titled “ March Winds,” with this pretty
stanza—
But the winds of March that are yonder’,gliding ;
Ah ! these are the dearest winds that blow !
Mothers they seem to me, stooping, guiding
Little child-snoivdrops out of the snow.
The Playgoer, noticing Lond. performance of
The Light that Failed—
Torpenhow brings back Maisie. Her love for Dick
has now swamped every other consideration, and she
begs him to take her. But in a splendid scene the
blind man refuses the sacrifice, until at last she takes
down her hair, sobs that she is the little girl of old
times and cannot live without him, and the curtain
falls on Dick taking her into his arms.
“ She takes down her hair ! ” Detrahe et impera ! For
Not ten yoke of oxen
Have the power to draw us
Like a woman’s hair,
steals Longfellow from Howell. It is woman’s antepen-
ultimate take-down, and weak man usually falls before
bodice and smock. Why? Ask Beauty that draws us, or
ask Antiquity that created us : hair-roots are the pro-
foundest roots of sex. “Only a woman’s hair,” wrote
Swift on the slip of paper that contained a lock of
“ Stella’s.” “ Only a woman’s hair 1 ”
Longmans’ Notes on Books supplies some
definitions : —lmpression.—A number of copies printed at
any one time. When a book is reprinted without change
it should be called a new impression to distinguish it
from an edition, as defined below. Edition.—An im-
pression in which the matter has undergone some change,
or for which the type has been reset. Re-isscje.—A re-
publication at a different price, or in a different form, of
part of an impression which has already been placed on
the market.
Instead of buying current trash at 2s. 6d. or
3s. (id. and more, bush bookfellows are recommended to
spend their money on the sixpenny series. See what von
can get for sixpence nowadays ! Huxley’s Lectures and
Essays ; Olodd’s Pioneers of Evolution ; Laing’s Modern
Science and Modern Thought; Arnold’s Literature and
Dogma; Haeckel’s Riddle of the Universe; Spencers
Education; Grant Allen’s Evolution of the Idea if
God— all first-rate books. In fiction, several of Charles
Reade’s; several of Wilkie Collins’s; some (f
“ Ouida’s ”; most of Hawley Smart’s ; three or four cf
Meredith’s; Jacobs’s Skipper’s Wooing ;■ Bram Stoker's
vampire story, Bracula ; Le Fanu’s Uncle Silas;
Bart Kennedy’s A Sailor Tramp ; and many others.
Ten shillings or £\ spent on the books and authois
named, with a limitation of “Ouida” and Smart,
will bring the best value ever offered to book-buyers.
Quote the books and authors, and don’t permit substi-
tution. And make the bookseller get them ; don’t throw
away your money on the higher-priced books which he
presses on your attention, because he earns more profit
upon them. (Our Advertiser interjects remonstrance, so
we commend Our Advertiser.)
